## Evolutionary Biology

### 2AC/1NC -- Realism Pre-Cognitive

#### Realism is *[pre-cognitive]{.underline}* -- evolutionary theory proves realism is a factor of *[natural selection]{.underline}* 

**Thayer, PhD, 2k**

(Bradley A., MIT Press, *Bringing in Darwin: Evolutionary Theory,
Realism, and International Politics*, 130-131 // NC)

\*edited for gendered language

[Contemporary evolutionary theorists offer excellent arguments for
**explaining**]{.underline} some of the human **[behavior expected by
realism]{.underline}**. Particularly interesting are those in the
subdiscipline of evolutionary theory known as sociobiology, the study of
human behavior from the perspective of evolutionary theory.31 Thus far,
however, realists have not used evolutionary theory to place
[realism]{.underline} on a stronger foundation.32 After briefly
reviewing the evolutionary process, I discuss how it [**can explain the
origins of egoism and domination and why it is a better ultimate cause
of realist behavior** than those put forth by Niebuhr and
Morgenthau]{.underline}.33 the process of evolution[. In evolutionary
theory]{.underline}, Homo sapiens, or [the]{.underline} anatomically
modern [human, is]{.underline} an animal, and [like]{.underline} all
[animals behaves as ~~he~~ does \[they do\] as a result of]{.underline}
evolution by [natural selection]{.underline}.34 The essence [of
evolution by natural selection is that most behavioral characteristics
of a species evolve because they help the species survive and
reproduce]{.underline}.35 According to philosopher of biology Elliott
Sober, [there are three constituents of this process]{.underline}.36
[First,]{.underline} there must be [genetic variation]{.underline} in
the species. If all individuals are the same, then there is no basis for
change. Gene frequencies, however, alter regularly through genetic
drift, migration, mutation, and natural selection.37 Thus for sexually
reproducing species, only identical twins (or other monozygotic multiple
births) are truly identical; all others possess differences. [Second,
genetic variation must improve what biologists term
"fitness]{.underline}": A member of a species is at if it is better able
to survive and reproduce---hence the term "survival of the fittest."38
These individuals will be better represented in the next generation than
those less at. [Finally, there must be heritable variation in
fitness:]{.underline} The characteristic must be passed from parent to
offspring.39 [According to evolutionary theory, human behavioral
traits]{.underline} (the genetic causes of human behavior) [evolve and
genes that increase fitness spread though the population. By displaying
these traits, an individual stands a better chance of surviving long
enough to reproduce and of having her genes represented in the next
generation.]{.underline}

#### Genetic analysis and Darwinian evolution suggests that in the face of survival countries only act for self-preservation 

**Thayer, PhD, 2k**

(Bradley A., MIT Press, *Bringing in Darwin: Evolutionary Theory,
Realism, and International Politics*, 130-131 // NC)

[Evolutionary theory offers two sufficient explanations for the trait of
egoism]{.underline}. [The first]{.underline} is a classic Darwinian
argument: [In a hostile environment where resources are
scarce]{.underline} and thus survival precarious, **[organisms typically
satisfy their own physiological needs]{.underline}** for food, shelter,
and so on **[before assisting other]{.underline}**. [In]{.underline}
times of [danger or great stress, an organism]{.underline} usually
places its life---[its survival]{.underline}---**[before that of other
members]{.underline}** of its group, be it pack, herd, or tribe. For
these reasons, [egoistic behavior contributes to fitness.]{.underline}
Evolutionary theorist Richard Dawkins's [selfish gene theory provides
the second]{.underline} sufficient [explanation for egoism]{.underline}.
A conceptual shift is required here because Dawkins's level of analysis
is the gene, not the organism. As Dawkins explains, at one time there
were no organisms, just chemicals in a primordial "soup."42 [At first,
different types of molecules started forming by accident]{.underline},
including some that could reproduce [by using]{.underline} the
constituents of the soup---[carbon, nitrogen, hydrogen, and
oxygen]{.underline}. [Because these constituents were in limited supply,
molecules competed for them as they replicated]{.underline}. From this
competition, the most efficient copy makers emerged. [The process,
however, was never perfect]{.underline}. Sometimes mistakes were made
during replication, and occasionally these accidents resulted in more
efficient replication or made some other contribution to fitness[. One
such mistake might have been the formation of a thin membrane that held
the contents of the molecule together---a primitive cell.]{.underline} A
second might have involved the division of the primitive cell into ever
larger components, [organs,]{.underline} and so on to create [what
Dawkins calls "survival machines]{.underline}." He explains, "The first
survival machines probably [consisted of nothing more than a protective
coat]{.underline}. But making a living got steadily harder as new rivals
arose with better and more effective survival machines. Survival
machines got bigger and more elaborate, and the process was cumulative
and progressive."43 [From a genetic perspective, there is no
intentionality in this process]{.underline}, but it continued
nonetheless because of evolution. [Dawkins makes clear, however, that
the interests of the gene and the organism need not coincide at
different stages in an organism's life, particularly after
reproduction]{.underline}.44 In general, however, **[the selfishness of
the gene increases its fitness, and so the behavior
spreads.]{.underline}**

### 2AC/1NC -- Egotism = Evolutionary

#### Evolutionary biology explains egotism

**Johnson, PhD, and Thayer, PhD, 16**

(Dominic D. P., PhD from Geneva University in political science, and
Bradley, Department of Political Science and Geography at UT San Antonio
(2016). The evolution of offensive realism: Survival under anarchy from
the Pleistocene to the present. Politics and the Life Sciences, 35(1),
1-26.)//bc

[Evolutionary theory offers a powerful explanation for]{.underline} the
trait of [egoism]{.underline} (by which we mean the nonpejorative
definition of ''self-regarding, prompted by selfinterest'').86 [Given
competition for limited resources and threats from predators and the
environment]{.underline}, **[an individual organism is primed to seek
its own survival]{.underline}** [and]{.underline}---the Darwinian bottom
line---[reproductive success. The]{.underline} [organism has
to]{.underline} [ensure that its physiological needs]{.underline} (for
food, water, shelter, and so on) [are satisfied so that it can survive
and reproduce]{.underline}. The imperative for [survival in a hostile
environment]{.underline} also [requires that an individual organism
places its survival]{.underline}, **[especially in a time of danger or
stress]{.underline}**, [above the survival of others.]{.underline} The
[genes of egoistic individuals]{.underline} [survive and spread at the
expense of those that fail to]{.underline} effectively [put their own
interests first]{.underline}. Of course, [cooperation]{.underline} and
helping behaviors are common in nature, but such behaviors **[persist
only where they help the genes causing that behavior to
spread]{.underline}**. Genes obviously do not ''want'' or ''try'' to
spread, but the machinery of [natural selection]{.underline} **[means
that self-serving alleles will generally increase in relative
frequency]{.underline}** in the population over time, at [the expense of
alleles]{.underline} [that are neutral or self-sacrificing]{.underline}
for no return benefit. Evolutionary theorists now recognize, following
William Hamilton's concept of ''inclusive fitness,'' that egoism has
complexities. Darwin himself envisioned these nuances, even though he
did not know the biological mechanisms at work. Hamilton used genetic
models to show that, [while individual organisms are egoistic, **they
should be less so in their behavior toward genetic
relatives**]{.underline}, especially in parent-offspring and sibling
relationships.87,88 **[This decrease in egoism is because close
relatives share many of the same genes]{.underline}**---one-half for
siblings and parents, one-quarter for aunts, uncles, and grandparents,
and one-eighth for cousins. [An individual's Darwinian
fitness]{.underline} therefore [includes the success of related
others]{.underline} (hence the phrase inclusive fitness). The preeminent
evolutionary theorist J.B.S. [Haldane]{.underline} thus [quipped that he
would give his life to save two of his brothers]{.underline} (each
sharing half of his genes) [or eight of his cousins]{.underline} (each
sharing one-eighth of his genes).89,90 **[Inclusive fitness provides a
biological basis for the common intuition that individuals favor those
who are close genetic relatives]{.underline}**.91 [Evolutionary theory
accounts for egoism]{.underline} [and explains why cooperation can
extend to the family or close kin group]{.underline} **[but remains
difficult between unrelated individuals]{.underline}**. [Cooperation
among unrelated individuals]{.underline} **[is]{.underline}**
**[possible but only as the result of interactions that help genes
replicate in the long run]{.underline}**, [through mechanisms such as
reciprocal altruism]{.underline}, [indirect]{.underline} [reciprocity,
and signaling]{.underline}.92 [**Even cooperation**]{.underline} and
helping behavior, therefore, **[are strategies that increase an
individual's Darwinian fitness]{.underline}**---indeed, that is
precisely why they evolved.93 In nature, [genetic egoism is the basis of
natural selection.]{.underline}

#### Realism's thesis of egotism is supported by the evolutionary advantage of selfishness

**Thayer, PhD, 4**

(Bradley A., Department of Political Science and Geography at UT San
Antonio. Darwin and International Relations : On the Evolutionary
Origins of War and Ethnic Conflict, University Press of Kentucky, 2004.
ProQuest Ebook Central,
<https://ebookcentral-proquest-com.proxy.lib.umich.edu/lib/umichigan/detail.action?docID=1914962.)//bc>

[Evolutionary theory offers two sufficient explanations for the trait of
egoism]{.underline}. The first is a classic Darwinian argument: Darwin
argued that [an individual organism is concerned for its own survival in
an environment where resources are scarce]{.underline}. [It has to
ensure that its physiological needs-for food, shelter, and so on-are
satisfied so that it can continue to survive.]{.underline} The
**[concern for survival in a hostile environment also requires that in a
time of danger]{.underline}** or great stress **[an individual organism
usually places its life]{.underline}**, its survival, **[above that of
other members of the social group]{.underline}**, the pack, herd, or
tribe.53 For these reasons, [egoistic behavior contributes to
fitness]{.underline}. The selfish gene theory of evolutionary theorist
Richard Dawkins provides the second sufficient explanation for egoism.
As I discussed in chapter 1, Dawkins focuses his analysis on the gene,
not the organism. Beginning with chemicals in a primordial \"soup,\"
different types of molecules started forming, and in time efficient copy
makers emerged.54 They made mistakes, however, and these contributed to
fitness, such as the formation of a thin membrane that held the contents
of the molecule together to become a primitive cell. Over time, these
\"[survival machines]{.underline}\". [became more sophisticated due to
evolution]{.underline} Some cells became specialized, creating organs
and ultimately animal bodies. But again, as I stressed in the previous
chapter, there is no intentionality in this process. Genes did not want
to create or inhabit people, but the process continued nonetheless. The
fundamental point here is that \"[selfishness\" of the gene increases
its fitness, and so the behavior spreads.]{.underline} [The gene creates
an instinctual or genetic basis for egoism **because it is concerned
only with satisfying its wants,**]{.underline} principally reproduction
and food consumption. The organism evolved largely to satisfy the wants
of the gene, and in a similar manner egoism evolves through a
population. Egoism thus becomes a trait or adaptation in animals, such
as humans, that aids survival. Evolutionary theorists now recognize, as
a result of William Hamilton\'s idea of inclusive fitness, that egoism
is more complex than Darwin envisioned. Hamilton recognized that
[individuals are egoistic, but less so in their behavior toward genetic
relatives]{.underline}, in parent-offspring and sibling relationships.
This is [**because close relatives share at least fifty percent of their
genotype-one-half for siblings and parents**, one-quarter for aunts,
uncles, and grandparents, and one-eighth for cousin]{.underline}s. As
the great evolutionary theorist J.B.S. Haldane wrote in 1955, [the gene
that inclines a man to jump into a river to save a drowning
child,]{.underline} and thus to take a one-in-ten chance of dying,
[could flourish as long as the child were his offspring or
sibling]{.underline}. 55 The gene could also spread, albeit more slowly,
if the child were a first cousin, since the cousin shares an average of
oneeighth of his genes. Indeed, Haldane captured this point well when he
wrote that he would give his life to save two of his brothers (each
sharing half of his genotype) or eight of his cousins (each sharing
one-eighth of his genotype). As a result of the ideas of Darwin,
Dawkins, and Hamilton, [evolutionary theory provides an explanation for
what is commonly known,]{.underline} that **[individuals favor those who
are close genetic relatives]{.underline}**. Consequently, [complex
social behavior among unrelated individuals]{.underline} **[can be seen
as the interaction of selfish individuals]{.underline}**, and most
evolutionary theorists expect no tendency toward solidarity,
cooperation, or altruism beyond what is in the interests of the animals.
Similarly, [realists]{.underline} [and]{.underline}, as we will see
below, [rational choice theorists]{.underline} [also do not expect
individuals or states to show this type of behavior]{.underline} [beyond
their own self-interest]{.underline}. Thus, [evolutionary
theor]{.underline}y can [explain egoism **and suggests why cooperation
between unrelated individuals is very often difficult**]{.underline} and
remarkably unlike the behavior one encounters within the family.

### 2AC/1NC -- Dominance Hierarchies

#### Dominance behavior and hierarchies are evolutionarily constant across vastly different species

**Johnson, PhD, and Thayer, PhD, 16**

(Dominic D. P., PhD from Geneva University in political science, and
Bradley, Department of Political Science and Geography at UT San Antonio
(2016). The evolution of offensive realism: Survival under anarchy from
the Pleistocene to the present. Politics and the Life Sciences, 35(1),
1-26.)//bc

Evolutionary theory can also explain dominance. Like egoism, [the desire
to dominate is a trait of human nature]{.underline} (which, as with
egoism, we stress does not necessarily apply to every individual or
situation but is a statistical tendency underlying behavior). As
evolutionary economist Robert Frank has explained, [**''Evidence
suggests that we come into the world equipped with a nervous system that
worries about rank.** Something inherent in our biological
makeup]{.underline} **[motivates us to try to improve]{.underline}**,
[or at least maintain]{.underline}, **[our standing against those with
whom we compete]{.underline}** [for important positional
resources]{.underline}.''94 In the context of evolutionary theory,
[dominance usually means that particular individuals]{.underline} in a
social group [have priority of access to resources]{.underline} [in
competitive situations]{.underline}.95 A wide variety of [animals
exhibit a form of social organization called a dominance
hierarchy]{.underline}, in which members of a social group each have a
status rank descending from the alpha male down through all the other
individuals to the lowliest subordinates[. A dominance hierarchy is
created competitively]{.underline}, [often violently, **and is
maintained forcefully**]{.underline}, [but it can]{.underline} serve to
[prevent or reduce]{.underline} [conflict]{.underline} within a group
[because it establishes a pecking order that is generally
respected]{.underline}. As with all things in nature, [dominance
hierarchies vary considerably]{.underline}. Hierarchies may be weak or
strong, and ''alpha'' males may sire nearly all offspring or just more
than others. However, what is striking is the prevalence and potency of
dominance in social organization, despite variations in the specifics.
[Dominance behavior]{.underline} **[occurs in thousands of taxonomic
groups ranging from fish and reptiles to birds and
mammals.]{.underline}** Of particular note regarding the impact of
dominance on human behavior are the roles of both phylogeny (a species'
ancestral lineage) and ecology (its adaptations to local conditions).
With regard to phylogeny, [most primates and all the great
apes]{.underline} (the group to which humans belong) [have strong social
dominance hierarchies, and humans are no
exception]{.underline}---**[dominance hierarchies have been extensively
documented among humans in a wide variety of settings and
eras]{.underline}**.96,97,98 With regard to ecology, dominance
hierarchies are a common form of social organization in the kind of
ecological settings in which humans evolved (social groups with
competing interests, variation in power, and finite resources). The
strength of dominance hierarchies in humans is debated and varies
empirically, but such hierarchies are always evident in some form or
other. [Even where dominance hierarchies are actively
suppressed]{.underline}, such as in more egalitarian small-scale
societies, **[suppression itself is evidence of the competition for
status that simmers beneath the surface]{.underline}**.99 In historical
and contemporary societies, **[competition for power is all too
obvious]{.underline}**, and [the quest for status, dominance, and
leadership is ancient and ever present]{.underline}.100,101,102,103
Males of most mammal species are particularly competitive with each
other over females. Indeed, the competition for mates is subject to a
special type of evolutionary selection process---sexual selection, as
opposed to standard natural selection. Reproductive access to females
tends to be highly skewed, with a few males responsible for a large
proportion of progeny. This is because a single male can reproduce with
multiple females, whereas females can usually only reproduce and rear
the offspring of one male at a time, with a long delay before becoming
reproductively available again. In some species, reproductive access is
settled by coercion, in which the strongest male defeats rivals to
dominate a harem. In other species, males cannot coerce females, but the
females are choosy about with whom they mate, leading to selection
pressures for males to demonstrate or signal their quality as attractive
partners. In either case, it is females rather than males that are the
limiting factor in sexual competition, making male competition for
available females intense. Sexual selection has led to costly biological
adaptations, such as fighting, the growth of heavy ''weapons'' (e.g.,
antlers), risky courtship displays, or adornments that signal genetic
quality (e.g., gigantic tails). These types of adaptations not only
consume precious time and energy but can also decrease survival in
other, nonreproductive domains of life (for example, the plumage of male
peacocks limits their ability to fly). Thus, the power of sexual
selection can lead to the evolution of traits that actually damage
survival in order to achieve superiority over other males.104,105
Reproduction trumps survival in evolution. [Sexual selection is
typically responsible for the hierarchical nature of group-living animal
species]{.underline}, including humans, [as males fight for rank and the
reproductive benefits in brings]{.underline}. As we would expect, [this
leads to sex differences in the desire for status.]{.underline} Both
laboratory experiments and real-world observations have identified
empirical differences between men and women in a range of social
behaviors, not least that men tend to have relative-gains, or
''zero-sum'' motivations (wanting to get ahead at the expense of
others), whereas women tend to favor payoff-maximization, or
''variable-sum'' motivations (content to do well even if others also do
well in the process).106,107,108,109 It is well established that
[dominance and statusseeking behaviors]{.underline} [in humans are based
on many of the same biochemical and neurological processes as in other
mammals]{.underline}, **[such as the secretion and uptake of
testosterone and serotonin]{.underline}**, [which modify
status-challenging behavior]{.underline}.110,111 However rational we may
like to think we are, [our judgments and decision-making are
nevertheless influenced by cognitive mechanisms and
biochemicals]{.underline} circulating in our bodies and brains that
relate to dominance behavior.112,113,114,115 Dominance hierarchies need
not only benefit those at the top. An [organized social structure can
help promote the harvesting of resources]{.underline}, [coordinate group
activity, and reduce within-group conflict]{.underline}. Although alphas
in the hierarchy tend to have the highest reproductive success, other
[males may benefit from group membership by gaining protection from
other groups]{.underline}, or by biding their time for a chance to
challenge the alpha male when they become strong enough or old enough.
**[Ethological studies have shown that hierarchical dominance systems
within a primate group's social network can reduce overt
aggression]{.underline}**, [although aggression increases again when the
alpha male is challenged]{.underline}.116,117,118 To summarize, a
species that lives communally could have two broad forms of social
organization. [The group can accept organization with some
centralization of power]{.underline} ([dominance
hierarchies]{.underline}), [or it can engage in perpetual conflict
(''scramble competition''), which incurs costs in terms of time, energy,
and injuries, as well as depriving the group of many benefits of a
communal existence,]{.underline} such as more efficient resource
harvesting.119 Among social mammals, and primates in particular,
dominance hierarchies have emerged as the primary form of social
organization. Thus far, we have emphasized [a state of ''anarchy'' in
evolutionary history]{.underline}, **[in which there was no overarching
power to provide protection from predators]{.underline}**, rivals, or
other threats. **[Egoism and dominance arose as strategies that provided
solutions to achieving survival and reproduction in this
environment]{.underline}**. However, [dominance hierarchies were in some
sense a mechanism by which this anarchy could be
suppressed]{.underline}---at least within the group---to the benefit of
all group members since they share at least some common interests (such
as avoiding conflict). [This collective benefit points to the special
and much more significant role of anarchy at a higher
level]{.underline}---**[anarchy between groups]{.underline}**. As we
show in the next section, competition between groups is especially
significant for human evolution, and for international politics,
precisely because it is at the intergroup level where anarchy reigns
supreme and is much harder to suppress.

#### States' tendencies towards aggression and selfishness are derived from the evolutionary need to maintain dominance hierarchies

**Thayer, PhD, 4**

(Bradley A., Department of Political Science and Geography at UT San
Antonio. Darwin and International Relations : On the Evolutionary
Origins of War and Ethnic Conflict, University Press of Kentucky, 2004.
ProQuest Ebook Central,
<https://ebookcentral-proquest-com.proxy.lib.umich.edu/lib/umichigan/detail.action?docID=1914962.)//bc>

**[Evolutionary theory can also explain domination]{.underline}**. Like
egoism, [the desire to dominate is a trait]{.underline}. In the context
of evolutionary theory, **[domination usually means that particular
individuals in social groups have regular priority of access to
resources]{.underline}** in competitive situations. For most social
mammals, [a form of social organization called a dominance
hierarchy]{.underline} [operates most of the time.]{.underline} The
creation of the [dominance hierarchy may be violent and is almost always
competitive]{.underline}. A single leader, almost always male (the alpha
male), leads the group. The ubiquity of this social ordering strongly
suggests that such a [pattern of organization]{.underline} [contributes
to fitness.]{.underline} Ethologists categorize two principal types of
behavior among social mammals in a dominance hierarchy: dominant and
submissive.56 [Dominant mammals have enhanced access to mates, food, and
territory]{.underline}, **[increasing their chances of reproductive
success]{.underline}**.57 **[Acquiring dominant status usually requires
aggression]{.underline}**.58 Dominance, however, is an unstable
condition; to maintain it, [dominant individuals must be willing to
defend their privileged access]{.underline} to available resources as
long as they are able. Evolutionary anthropologist Richard Wrangham and
ethologist Dale Peterson explain why an individual animal is motivated
to vie for dominant status: \"The motivation of a male chimpanzee who
challenges another\'s rank is not that he foresees more matings or
better food or a longer life.\"59 Rather, \"those rewards explain why
\... selection has favored the desire for power, but the immediate
reason he vies for status \... is simply to dominate his peers.\"60
[Dominant animals often assume behavior reflecting their
status]{.underline}. For example, [dominant wolves and rhesus monkeys
hold their tails higher than other members of their group in an effort
to communicate dominance.]{.underline} A dominant animal that engages in
such displays is better off if it can gain priority of access to
resources without having to fight for it continuously.61 [Such signaling
behavior is found among submissive social mammals as well. They often
try to be as inconspicuous as possible]{.underline} and recognize what
is permitted and forbidden given their place in the hierarchy. **[This
behavior shows that the subordinate accepts its place in the dominance
hierarchy]{.underline}** [and]{.underline}-at least temporarily-[will
make no effort to challenge the dominant animal.]{.underline}
Ethologists argue that [dominance hierarchies evolve because they help
defend against predators, promote the harvesting of resources, and
reduce intragroup conflict]{.underline}.62 A species that lives
communally has two choices. It can either accept organization with some
centralization of power, or engage in perpetual conflict over scarce
resources, which may result in serious injury and deprive the group of
the benefits of a communal existence, such as more efficient resource
harvesting.63 Ethological studies have confirmed that a hierarchical
dominance system within a primate band minimizes overt aggression and
that group aggression often increases when the alpha male is
challenged.64 For primates and especially humans, the dominance
hierarchy may have produced a fortuitous result: great intelligence.65
As cognitive psychologist Denise Dellarosa Cummins argues, it has had a
profound effect on human evolution: \"[The fundamental components of our
reasoning architectur]{.underline}e [evolved in response to pressures to
reason about dominance hierarchies]{.underline}, the social organization
that characterizes most social mammals.\"66 Her study and others have
found that dominance hierarchies have contributed to the evolution of
the mind, which in turn has contributed to fitness. According to
Cummins, [submissive individuals can detect, exploit, and circumvent the
constraints of domination. If an animal can take what it wants by
force**, it is sure to dominate the available
resources**]{.underline}-[unless its subordinates are smart enough to
outwit]{.underline} [it]{.underline}. To survive, a subordinate must use
other strategies: deception, guile, appeasement, bartering, alliance
formation, or friendship. Thus intelligence is particularly important to
the survival of subordinates. \"[The evolution of mind
emerges]{.underline},\" Cummins writes, \"**[as a strategic arms race in
which the weaponry is ever-increasing mental capacity to represent and
manipulate internal representations of the minds of
others.]{.underline}**\"67 From their studies of chimpanzee societies,
ethologists have learned that the struggle for survival is best
characterized as a struggle between those who are dominant and those
seeking to outwit them, i.e., between recognizing an opponent\'s
intentions and hiding one\'s own. The following example illustrates how
a subordinate chimpanzee, Belle, who knows the location of hidden food,
attempts to deceive Rock, who is dominant: Belle accordingly stopped
uncovering the food if Rock was close. She sat on it until Rock left.
Rock, however, soon learned this, and when she sat in one place for more
than a few seconds, he came over, shoved her aside, searched her sitting
place, and got the food. Belle next stopped going all the way \[to the
food\]. Rock, however, countered by steadily expanding the area of his
search through the grass near where Belle had sat. Eventually, Belle sat
farther and farther away, waiting until Rock looked in the opposite
direction before she moved toward the food at all, and Rock in turn
seemed to look away until Belle started to move somewhere. On some
occasions Rock started to wander off, only to wheel around suddenly
precisely as Belle was about to uncover some food \.... On a few trials,
she actually started off a trail by leading the group in the opposite
direction from the food, and then, while Rock was engaged in his search,
she doubled back rapidly and got some food. 68 [Despite the \"arms
race\" described by Cummins to outwit dominance, the subordinate members
of the group continue to participate]{.underline} in the dominance
hierarchy **[because doing so increases the chances of
survival]{.underline}**. As ethologist David Barash explains**[, if
subordinates \"are more fit by accepting]{.underline}**\"
[subordinate]{.underline} \"[ranking than by refusing to
participate]{.underline}, **[then some form of social dominance
hierarchy will result]{.underline}**.\"69 Humans and other primates
evolved a mental architecture to address the difficulties they
encountered in dominance hierarchies. These problems, which \"directly
impact survival rates and reproductive success,\" include two crucial
needs: \"the necessity to recognize and respond appropriately to
permissions, obligations, and prohibition,\" and the necessity \"to
circumvent the constraints of hierarchy by dint of guile, particularly
through successfully forecasting others\' behavior. \"70 Because [human
mental architecture was created through evolution]{.underline}, **[it
remains part of human behavior today]{.underline}**, as cognitive
psychology studies show. 71

### 2AC/1NC -- Offensive Realism

#### Evolutionary theory explains offensive realism -- egotism, domination, and ethnocentrism are rooted in evolutionary needs

**Thayer, PhD, 4**

(Bradley A., Department of Political Science and Geography at UT San
Antonio. Darwin and International Relations : On the Evolutionary
Origins of War and Ethnic Conflict, University Press of Kentucky, 2004.
ProQuest Ebook Central,
<https://ebookcentral-proquest-com.proxy.lib.umich.edu/lib/umichigan/detail.action?docID=1914962.)//bc>

**[Evolutionary theory allows realists to advance offensive realist
arguments without seeking an ultimate cause in]{.underline}** either the
**[anarchic international state system or in theological or metaphysical
ideas]{.underline}**. [Realism based on evolutionary theory reaches the
same]{.underline} [conclusions]{.underline}, [but the]{.underline}
ultimate [causal mechanism is different]{.underline}: human evolution in
the anarchic and perilous conditions of the late-Pliocene, Pleistocene,
and most of the Holocene epochs. Specially, **[evolutionary theory
explains why humans are egoistic]{.underline}**, **[strive to dominate
others, and make ingroup/out-group distinctions]{.underline}**.
**[These]{.underline}** adaptations in turn **[serve as a foundation for
offensive realism]{.underline}**. The central issue here is what causes
states to behave as offensive realists predict. Mearsheimer advances a
powerful argument that anarchy is the fundamental cause of such
behavior. The fact that there is no world government compels the leaders
of states to take steps to ensure their security, such as striving to
have a powerful military, aggressing when forced to do so, and forging
and maintaining alliances. This is what neorealists call a self-help
system: leaders of states are forced to take these steps because nothing
else can guarantee their security in the anarchic world of international
relations. I argue that [evolutionary theor]{.underline}y also
[offers]{.underline} [a fundamental cause for offensive realist
behavior]{.underline}. Evolutionary theory explains why individuals are
motivated to act as offensive realism expects, whether an individual is
a captain of industry or a conquistador. My argument is that **[anarchy
is even more important than most scholars ofinternational relations
recognize]{.underline}**. The [human environment of evolutionary
adaptation was anarchic]{.underline}; **[our ancestors lived in a state
of nature in which resources were poor]{.underline}** a**[nd dangers
from other humans and the environment were great]{.underline}**-so great
that it is truly remarkable that a mammal standing three feet
high-without claws or strong teeth, not particularly strong or
swift-survived and evolved to become what we consider human. [**Humans**
**endured because natural selection gave them the right behaviors to
last in those conditions**]{.underline}. [This]{.underline} environment
[produced the behaviors examined here:]{.underline} [egoism, domination,
and the in-group/out-group distinction]{.underline}. These specific
[traits are sufficient to explain why leaders will behave,]{.underline}
in the proper circumstances, [as offensive realists expect them to
behave]{.underline}. That is, [even if they must hurt other humans or

their power]{.underline}**, defined as either [control over
others]{.underline} (for example, through wealth or leadership) [or
control over ecological circumstances]{.underline} (such as meeting

food-and why some, particularly males, will seek to dominate others by
maintaining a privileged position in a dominance hierarchy. Clearly, as
the leaders of states are human, they too will be influenced by

discussed these two elements of my argument: that evolutionary theory
allows realist scholars to explain why state decision makers are

circumstances permit.s1 These adaptations were critically important in
the course of human evolution and remain a significant ultimate cause of
human behavior. Recalling that [biology is good
probability]{.underline}, [not destiny]{.underline}, [we should expect
that leaders of states and major decision makers will possess these
traits and are not likely to suppress them]{.underline} the way a

suppress them, they are likely doing so only for tactical reasons or as
required by specific circumstances. In fact, [a state\'s
elites]{.underline}-the captains of industry and media, and military and
political leaders-**[are more likely than average to show these traits
in abundance]{.underline}** [since most leaders rise to the top of their
respective hierarchies through a very competitive process]{.underline}.
**[This is almost always the case for political leaders]{.underline}**.
[The rise to the top is the result of an often arduous,]{.underline}
[and]{.underline} perhaps [physically dangerous, proces]{.underline}s[;
those who triumph,]{.underline} whether Mao or Clinton, **[are almost
certain to be egoistic individuals]{.underline}** [who are used to
dominating the individuals]{.underline} or institutions [around them.
Even those who inherit the throne have discovered]{.underline} time and
again **[that keeping it involves almost endless struggle and
