### 2AC/1NC -- Heuristics Inevitable

#### Heuristics cannot be changed -- evolution and natural selection lock-in responses 

**Petersen, 15**, \[Michael Bang Petersen holds a Phd in Poltical
Science, and is a professor of Politcal Psychology and Behavior\], (Feb
2015, \"\",Political Psychology, Vol. 36, No. S1, Supplement: Advances
in Political Psychology, accessed 7-19-2019), //Snitty

This is the fundamental principle. [Humans evolved as political animals
and, in order to be successful as a species, our minds are required to
come naturally equipped with a number of political
heuristics]{.underline}. **[Political heuristics cannot be presumed to
be \"learned\" in the sense of logically deduced or acquired through
explicit instruction]{.underline}**. [To anyone interested in
understanding political heuristics, this should be a welcome
message.]{.underline} When in Popper\'s (1959) \"context of
discovery\" - the context of hypothesis formulation - [we are no longer
confined to our own fallible intuitions when considering the structure
of political heuristics. Instead, we can utilize one of the best
validated and most powerful scientific theories of all times to
understand the heuristics used by modern citizens: the theory of
evolution by natural selection.]{.underline} In processes of **[natural
selection]{.underline}**, origin - and, in particular, the functional
requirements related to this origin - **[determines
structure]{.underline}**. Hence, by (1) utilizing anthropological and
archaeological knowledge to specify the kinds of political challenges
our ancestors have faced, (2) considering the nested representational
and motivational problems that would have needed to be solved in order
to meet these challenges, and (3) considering the evolutionarily
recurrent cues available for these systems to process to reach these
solutions, we can build testable hypotheses on the structure of
political heuristics. Principle 2. [The Function of Many Political
Heuristics Is to Solve Evolutionarily Recurrent Adaptive
Problems]{.underline} In the traditional perspective, the function of
heuristics is to help citizens formulate value- consistent opinions with
minimal cognitive effort. [Evolved heuristics, in contrast, have
numerous, highly different functions; although the overarching goal of
all of them was to help our ancestors survive and reproduce under
evolutionarily recurrent conditions]{.underline}. **[Each heuristic
-]{.underline}** [and its underlying representational and motivational
system]{.underline} - **[is designed by natural selection to solve a
particular problem]{.underline}**. [In this regard, consistency between
values and attitudes or between attitudes expressed at certain points in
time has most likely not been the most important problems facing our
ancestors]{.underline}. [Whenever modern political issues or events
exhibit the cues that would have disclosed a problem under ancestral
circumstances]{.underline}, [we ought to **expect the relevant systems
to come online**]{.underline}. [Our minds are built to infer indirectly
whether an adaptive problem is present from the cues available, and our
mind will presume that the adaptive problem is indeed present if the
appropriate set of cues is present]{.underline}. Cues surrounding social
welfare debates, such as needy individuals requesting help, activate
representational and motivational systems designed for small-scale
social exchange (Petersen, 2012a). [Similarly, cues surrounding criminal
justice debates, such as cost-infliction and malicious intents, activate
representational and motivational systems designed for small-scale
counterexploitation]{.underline} (Petersen, Sell, Cosmides, & Tooby,
2012).

### 2AC/1NC -- Aggression

#### Aggression is [biological]{.underline} --- psychological adaptations determine reactions to conflict

**Klasios**, **19** -- Researcher for York University (John,
\"Aggression among men: An integrated evolutionary explanation\",
Science Direct, July-August 2019, Vol. 47, p. 29-45))SK

\*\*we do not endorse gendered language

A number of [biosocial
[criminologists](https://www-sciencedirect-com.proxy.lib.umich.edu/topics/medicine-and-dentistry/criminologist)]{.underline}
have attempted to [illuminate aggression]{.underline} (as well as crime
and
[delinquency](https://www-sciencedirect-com.proxy.lib.umich.edu/topics/social-sciences/delinquency)
more generally) [in terms of its
[heritability](https://www-sciencedirect-com.proxy.lib.umich.edu/topics/medicine-and-dentistry/heritability)]{.underline}.
Here, my aim will be twofold: First, to bridge behavior genetics with
the [explanatory
model](https://www-sciencedirect-com.proxy.lib.umich.edu/topics/psychology/explanatory-model)
developed in this paper, and then to briefly discuss the evolutionary
processes that can act to generate heritable variation in traits
connected to male aggression. [Heritable variation in violent crime has
been established by behavior geneticists]{.underline} (e.g., [Barnes et
al.,
2014](https://www-sciencedirect-com.proxy.lib.umich.edu/science/article/pii/S1359178918301666#bb0050);
[DiLalla & Gheyara,
2011](https://www-sciencedirect-com.proxy.lib.umich.edu/science/article/pii/S1359178918301666#bb0205);
[Ferguson,
2010](https://www-sciencedirect-com.proxy.lib.umich.edu/science/article/pii/S1359178918301666#bb0250);
[Rhee & Waldman,
2002](https://www-sciencedirect-com.proxy.lib.umich.edu/science/article/pii/S1359178918301666#bb0570)).[7](https://www-sciencedirect-com.proxy.lib.umich.edu/science/article/pii/S1359178918301666#fn0035)
On the one hand, these studies show us how much of the variance in a
given population, at a given time, can be explained by heritable
factors. Yet on the other hand, heritability studies are largely silent
on the mechanistic and developmental details that subserve the
heritability of traits. In order to see how these two sides of the coin
are interrelated, we can start by considering the concept of reactive
heritability, which sketches how heritable traits express or magnify
themselves developmentally (e.g., [Tooby & Cosmides,
1990](https://www-sciencedirect-com.proxy.lib.umich.edu/science/article/pii/S1359178918301666#bb0680);
[von Rueden, Lukaszewski, & Gurven,
2015](https://www-sciencedirect-com.proxy.lib.umich.edu/science/article/pii/S1359178918301666#bb0585)).
[To illustrate how]{.underline} such [processes]{.underline} can [work,
we can consider an example germane to **aggression**, whereby an
individual inherits above **average physical strength**.]{.underline}
From the figurative point-of-view of the underlying psychological
adaptations that govern aggression (broadly speaking), and all else
equal, it is unknown what the level of physical strength is in the
organism that the adaptations are developing and operating in. However,
it is possible that [natural-selection can design]{.underline} such
[adaptations so that they can assess what the agent\'s physical strength
level is]{.underline}---perhaps, for instance, [by attending to feedback
regarding how often, and to what degree, the agent is either successful
or unsuccessful in physical [conflicts of
interest](https://www-sciencedirect-com.proxy.lib.umich.edu/topics/social-sciences/conflict-of-interest)]{.underline}
with other agents. Given these assessments, [the underlying
psychological adaptation can then compute an overall index of the
agent\'s physical strength, and then utilize that index when judging if
and when to instrumentally mete out aggression]{.underline}. Whether
such an adaptation has actually evolved, and, if so, what its design
features are, are of course empirical questions. However,
[**evolutionary psychologists** have]{.underline} indeed [found
experimental evidence that suggests that a psychological adaptation has
evolved in men that can assess one\'s physical strength, and then use
that assessment as an "internal regulatory variable" which
governs]{.underline}, for instance, [one\'s **proneness to use anger**
as a bargaining tactic during conflicts]{.underline} of interest with
other agents ([Sell, Cosmides, et al.,
2009](https://www-sciencedirect-com.proxy.lib.umich.edu/science/article/pii/S1359178918301666#bb0620);
[Sell, Tooby, & Cosmides,
2009](https://www-sciencedirect-com.proxy.lib.umich.edu/science/article/pii/S1359178918301666#bb0625)).

### AT: Cooperation Checks Aggression

#### Group selection/cooperation doesn't check aggression -- evolutionary forces of individual competition are more powerful

**Johnson, PhD, and Thayer, PhD, 16**

(Dominic D. P., PhD from Geneva University in political science, and
Bradley, Department of Political Science and Geography at UT San Antonio
(2016). The evolution of offensive realism: Survival under anarchy from
the Pleistocene to the present. Politics and the Life Sciences, 35(1),
1-26.)//bc

If intergroup conflict is so important, might evolution favor the most
cooperative groups? [Evolution is sometimes argued to operate on groups
rather than individuals]{.underline} (''group selection''). This idea is
important because[, if individuals are working for the good of the
group]{.underline} rather than only for themselves[, then groups
composed of more-cooperative individuals may do better than
less-cooperative groups]{.underline}, meaning that genuinely altruistic
traits (sacrificing one's own interests for the good of others) can
spread in the population.184,185 However, **[there are several reasons
why this possibility does not affect our argument.]{.underline}** First,
**[group selection is a controversial hypothesis]{.underline}**, [which
has been rejected by many prominent evolutionary
biologists]{.underline}.186 While [selection]{.underline} at the level
of groups is possible in principle, it [requires special conditions to
overcome]{.underline} **[what are generally agreed to be the much more
powerful forces of competition and selection acting on
individuals]{.underline}**, and [these forces are always in play whether
groups are in competition with each other or not.]{.underline}
Therefore, even the strongest advocates of group selection, such as
David Sloan Wilson, argue that, in any given case, [it remains an
empirical question as to whether or not the selection pressures acting
at the level of individuals are outweighed by selection
pressures]{.underline} potentially [acting at the level of the
group]{.underline} (so called multi-level selection). While biological
group selection in humans is possible in theory, [there have not been
any published empirical examples]{.underline}. Second, [even if group
selection does occur**, it can only increase altruism within
groups.**]{.underline} Between groups, [group selection would do the
opposite]{.underline}, [maintaining or even exacerbating
conflict.]{.underline}187 Because [the premise is that selection
operates at the level of groups]{.underline}, a[ltruistic traits can
only spread if altruism helps spread the genes responsible for
it]{.underline} at the expense of other genes, and [that must occur via
intergroup competition or conflict]{.underline}. Therefore, [to advocate
group selection]{.underline} over individual selection **[does nothing
to reduce predictions regarding human conflict or
aggression]{.underline}**. If anything, **[group selection would tend to
increase violence]{.underline}**, [since between-group
competition]{.underline} (conflict among strangers) [can be more brutal
than within-group competition]{.underline} (conflict among kin and
fellow group members). In Matt Ridley's words, **[to prefer group
selection over individual selection ''is to prefer genocide over
murder]{.underline}**.''188 Group selection can promote cooperation and
altruism, but only within the group. The dark side of group selection is
the unfettered conflict it presupposes and predicts between groups in
competition with each other. Third, it is important to remember that the
empirical observation of altruism in nature does not imply or demand
group selection. [Given group selection's theoretical
constraints]{.underline}, **[it should be a last-resort
explanation]{.underline}** (subject to empirical testing), not a first
point of call. As we have explained, there are several mechanisms by
which altruistic or helping behavior can (and have) evolved because of
the benefits of helping others that accrue to oneself ---not least,
altruistic behavior among kin, reciprocity, and reputation formation. In
short, [you do not need group selection to explain
altruism]{.underline}. In the right contexts, helping others can help
oneself. The [strategic allocation of resources to others often advances
one's own]{.underline} [Darwinian fitness]{.underline}. **[We do not
need to resort to group selection unless altruism cannot be explained by
more conventional mechanisms]{.underline}** based on individual
selection. George C. Williams famously made this point in response to so
called nave group selectionists of the time, and his insight has
continued to be reiterated to biology students ever since.189 While
biological group selection among humans is unlikely, the selection of
cultural traits among groups is possible. The constraints on biological
group selection, such as [significant differences in a given
trait]{.underline} between groups and low migration, [are relaxed in the
case of cultural traits,]{.underline} [since groups actively promote
cultural distinctions]{.underline} and have many mechanisms to prevent
flows between them.190 Therefore, [it is]{.underline} not just likely
but [quite apparent that]{.underline} many [cultural traits]{.underline}
have [evolved out of group-level competition]{.underline}---sometimes
referred to as memes, as opposed to genes. (Examples include the spread
of Christianity or Islam at the expense of traditional religions over
the last 2,000 years.) However, once again, the potential for cultural
group selection does not change or challenge our argument. To the extent
that cultural group selection extends back into our evolutionary past,
cultural traits have not been consistently or powerfully contrary to the
evolved traits of egoism, dominance, and ingroup/outgroup bias. Indeed,
cultural selection has often reinforced, not reduced, these very
behaviors over human history. Moreover, and lastly, cultural differences
have themselves represented an additional cause and consequence of
conflict. In addition to fighting over resources, we can now fight over
ideology as well.

#### Intergroup competition and conflict is evolutionarily inevitable -- cooperation only happens when it's beneficial to the individual 

**Johnson, PhD, and Thayer, PhD, 16**

(Dominic D. P., PhD from Geneva University in political science, and
Bradley, Department of Political Science and Geography at UT San Antonio
(2016). The evolution of offensive realism: Survival under anarchy from
the Pleistocene to the present. Politics and the Life Sciences, 35(1),
1-26.)//bc

Humans evolved as a distinct lineage principally in the Pleistocene era
(from 2 million to 10,000 years ago), and our analysis therefore
requires a discussion of the small-scale hunter-gatherer groups that
formed the social and ecological context for that period of human
evolution. Of the many features of hunter-gatherer society and
organization, we focus on intergroup relations, since these are most
relevant to the behaviors associated with international relations.
[While relations within groups might be characterized by coordination
and cooperation]{.underline} (although internal conflict was important
too), **[relations between groups were characterized by competition and
conflict]{.underline}** (although external cooperation and trade was
also possible). A key debate in evolutionary anthropology has revolved
around the origins and extent of intergroup conflict among
huntergatherers, and the emerging consensus is that such conflict is
(and has long been) significant and widespread, and that it serves
adaptive functions.59 Let us first consider these functional advantages.
[Why would hunter-gatherer groups fight at all]{.underline}? What is the
logic for risking life and limb in engaging in violent aggression
against other groups? Conflict may seem costly to all parties involved,
winners and losers alike, but [what matters for natural selection is
whether fighting]{.underline}, [despite its costs, can bring net
benefits to Darwinian fitness]{.underline}. Evolutionary theory would
expect that **[intergroup conflict contributes to fitness in certain
circumstances]{.underline}** [if successful defense and offense against
outgroups yield resources and reduce competition in an environment
defined by finite resources]{.underline}.60,61 A resource is any
material substance that has the potential to increase the individual's
ability to survive or reproduce, such as food, shelter, territory,
coalition allies, and members of the opposite sex.62 What an
evolutionary perspective allows us to understand is that [the origins of
warfare and the functions of warfare are interconnected.]{.underline}
**[The origins of warfare are rooted in the imperative to gain and
defend resources necessary for survival]{.underline}** **[and
reproduction in dangerous and competitive conditions]{.underline}**.
[Competition for resources results in situations where consumption
by]{.underline} [one individual or group diminishes the amount available
for others]{.underline}, or where one individual or group controls the
distribution of resources and thus can deny them to others.63,64 In the
Pleistocene era, [any group facing a shortage of resources]{.underline}
(or a need for more, as the group expands) [could]{.underline} have
adopted one or a combination of three basic strategies. First, the group
could [eliminate or reduce consumption to make the resource last. This
strategy was clearly not an option for critical resources]{.underline},
such as food and water. Second, [the group might seek an alternative for
the resource]{.underline}, [perhaps through technological innovation or
by substitution]{.underline}. In the Pleistocene era, this strategy
could have been an option with some resources but not others. Third,
**[the group could acquire more of the resource from outside of their
territory]{.underline}** **[through migration to uninhabited areas,
trade, theft, or warfare]{.underline}**.65,77,67,68,69,70 Although
**[warfare]{.underline}** is certainly costly to any member of a group
who is killed or wounded, as well as in terms of the resources and time
expended, it **[can become the sole]{.underline}** (or least bad)
**[choice for a group if migration is risky]{.underline}** due to
factors such as inhospitable or unproductive terrain or hostile
neighboring groups, and where trade is difficult or impossible. For
example[, a group seeking a fixed]{.underline} [source of water may be
unable to trade for it if the group lacks adequate resources to offer in
exchange]{.underline}. Warfare might then be necessary for offensive
purposes, to plunder resources from others. [Aggression may be a risky
strategy]{.underline}, **[but it is a more attractive option than
starvation or other lethal dangers]{.underline}**. [Any]{.underline}
given [individual's Darwinian fitness will be increased if they can
successfully seize the resources of others]{.underline} at sufficiently
low cost.71 Of course, warfare also may be waged for defensive reasons,
such as to defend critical resources from the advances of others.72 E.O.
Wilson captures the evolutionary logic succinctly, saying that
[humans]{.underline} would [fight wars]{.underline} ''**[when they and
their closest relatives stand to gain long-term reproductive
success,'']{.underline}** and he continues, ''[despite appearances to
the contrary, warfare may be just one example of the rule that cultural
practices are generally adaptive in a Darwinian sense]{.underline}.''73
An evolutionary approach allows the expectation that contemporary humans
possess specific behavioral traits that contributed to fitness in the
past, including the willingness to fight to retain or gain the resources
necessary so that the individual, the family, and the extended family
group would continue to survive and reproduce.74 Unsurprisingly, direct
evidence of human behavior from the Pleistocene era is rare, but in
addition to archeological finds, we have evidence from recent and
contemporary indigenous societies that offer a model for the behavior of
our distant ancestors, who lived under similar social and ecological
conditions. Napoleon Chagnon's work among the Yanomamo of the Amazon
revealed that [indigenous groups had a constant need to find new
territory as they expanded and split]{.underline}, and [they experienced
a constant fear of other groups]{.underline} [because violent conflict
was a recurring strategy used to stake out a livelihoo]{.underline}d.75
The Yanomamo are just one example of a pattern that extends to a wide
range of indigenous societies around the world.76,77 Across such
societies, around 15 percent of male deaths occurred in warfare, which
compares to a figure of around 1 percent for the United States and
Europe in the so-called bloody 20th century (and in many of those
small-scale indigenous societies, the rate of male deaths from warfare
is much higher than the average figure of 15 percent).78,79 It has been
argued that such high levels of conflict among indigenous societies
might have been caused by pressure from more developed societies
encroaching on their territories and way of life from the outside.
However, a study by Wrangham and Glowacki, which explicitly looked at
warfare among [hunter-gatherers who were surrounded by other
hunter-gatherers]{.underline}, found that [warfare was just as common in
this more ''natural'' setting.]{.underline}80 Evidence from across the
cumulative research of archeologists and anthropologists indicates that
[**violence is a widespread feature of small-scale foraging societies**
and follows a pattern that is consistent as far back as we can see in
the ethnographic and archeological record]{.underline}.81 Wrangham's and
Glowacki's work has also established empirical support for the
evolutionary logic in the patterns of intergroup conflict. Hunter
gatherers have recurrent tendencies, including hostility toward members
of different societies, and for killing to be carried out in relative
safety---that is, only when there is a strong asymmetry in power between
subgroups, such as in a raid or ambush (the ''imbalance of power''
hypothesis). By contrast, as ''rational actor'' theorists would expect,
hunter[-gatherers are averse to the risk of fighting symmetric
battles]{.underline} [with roughly equivalent numbers on each
side.]{.underline}82 Importantly, [sustained instances of imbalances
of]{.underline} [power over evolutionary history]{.underline} **[would
have led to the selection of contingent aggression]{.underline}**. In
other words, [since imbalances of power]{.underline} [offer systematic
opportunities]{.underline} for low-cost aggression over time, [we should
expect human groups to]{.underline} have **[developed a disposition to
act aggressively against others when the opportunity
arises,]{.underline}** because opportunistic aggression is a strategy
that pays off on the average. Indeed, Wrangham and Glowacki find
evidence that after ''**[warriors killed members of a neighboring
society,]{.underline}** [the]{.underline} [killer's group benefited as a
whole via territorial expansion''83]{.underline}--- precisely as has
been shown for intergroup killings by chimpanzees. Chagnon, Wrangham and
Glowacki and others have also shown that [individuals]{.underline}, as
well as the group, [may gain significant reputational and reproductive
advantages of participation in warfare]{.underline}. Although warfare is
a high-stakes collective action problem, **[warriors are willing to
participate because over evolutionary time the dividends have tended to
outweigh the costs]{.underline}**.84,85

## Status Competition

### 2AC/1NC -- Insecurity

#### Status competition arises from insecurity over international credibility and humiliation -- causes military aggression and imperialism

**Barnhart, PhD, 16**

(Joslyn (2016), BA Reed College MA Claremont Mckenna PHD University of
California LA, Status Competition and Territorial Aggression: Evidence
from the Scramble for Africa, Security Studies, 25:3, 385-419, DOI:
10.1080/09636412.2016.1195620) //bc

This theory of status assertion overlaps to some degree with other
status based theories of behavior. [Social Identity Theory]{.underline}
(SIT) [posits that groups want to maintain positive, distinctive
identities]{.underline} [and that an unfavorable comparison with a
reference group engenders a desire to enhance one's
position]{.underline}.35 While [states]{.underline} may choose to seek
status through less competitive strategies such as imitation and social
creativity, they [are more likely to seek status through a strategy of
social competition]{.underline} [when the borders between groups appears
to be "impermeable."]{.underline}36 [Disrespect can be understood
as]{.underline} a signal of impermeability---as [a sign that
higher-status]{.underline} [states are unwilling to accept new
members]{.underline} into their ranks. [Impermeability]{.underline},
however, [suggests a sustained state]{.underline} [of restricted
access]{.underline} over time, [not solely in the form of
disrespect]{.underline}. I argue instead that a single instance of
disrespect may be sufficient to lead states to engage in competitive
assertions of status. Instances of humiliation also increase the
likelihood of competitive strategies, though they are not signals of
boundary impermeability. I also argue that [status-challenged states
with sufficient capabilities are likely to first pursue competitive
strategies over imitation or social creativity]{.underline} for a number
of reasons. First, as suggested above, because [humiliation and
disrespect]{.underline} engender anger, they **[serve as a trigger for
aggressive action]{.underline}**.37 Second, because [competitive
strategies]{.underline} [rely upon established bases of
status]{.underline}, [they are less risky than a strategy of
creativity]{.underline} with which a state may or may not succeed in
establishing new dimensions of status. Finally, [competitive strategies
also enable status-threatened states to send a more targeted signal to
states that have disrespected it than do imitation or
creativity]{.underline}. [This signal]{.underline} often [involves
vigorously]{.underline} [exercising the right or pursuing the exact
interest that the disrespected state was originally denied]{.underline}.
[Wohlforth]{.underline} presents an alternative model, [arguing that a
state will compete for status when it matches higher-status
state]{.underline}s **[in some but not all of the key material
dimensions of status]{.underline}**.38 He argues that [periods of power
transition]{.underline} **[create the conditions for status
ambiguity]{.underline}** in which [states have an incentive to strive
for]{.underline} [preeminence]{.underline}. I argue, however, that while
instances of humiliation and disrespect may be more likely to occur
during shifts in relative capabilities, [states also experience
humiliation and disrespect when the distribution of capabilities is
relatively stable and unambiguous]{.underline}.39 Because of the
difficulty of objectively measuring how one's characteristics rank
relative to others, [states obtain estimates of their
status]{.underline} [through the amount of consideration they receive
from others]{.underline}. [Instances of disrespect allow for
such]{.underline} [estimates and]{.underline} therefore [serve as
triggers for status seeking]{.underline}, whether the underlying
distribution of forces is shifting or not. Finally, realist analyses of
[status competition]{.underline} (for example, Robert Gilpin's War and
Change in World Politics) **[view military aggression as arising from
inconsistency between a state's degree of influence and its military
power as states seek to demonstrate relative military
capability]{.underline}**.40 I argue, in contrast, [that status is
partly]{.underline} but not wholly [based on relative capabilities and
that direct military]{.underline} [conflict is one among many
status-seeking strategies]{.underline}. States need not engage in direct
conflict with rivals or in costly displays of capability or resolve in
order to demonstrate their ranking relative to others. Numerous less
risky and less costly status-seeking strategies, such as the extension
of imperial control or the adoption of policies opposed to dominant
states, are available to humiliated and disrespected states that allow
them to signal the status they expect to hold in the international
system.

#### States engage in status competition -- five reasons.

**Volgy\* and Gordell\*\*, 6/3/2019** (Thomas J. Volgy\*, Professor of
Political Science at the University of Arizona and the Executive
Director of the International Studies Association; and Kelly Marie
Gordell\*\*, International Relations PhD candidate at the School of
Government and Public Policy at the University of Arizona, "Rising
powers, status competition, and global governance: a closer look at
three contested concepts for analyzing status dynamics in international
politics", Contemporary Politics, ISSN: 1356-9775 (Print) 1469-3631, pp.
9-13, 6/3)micahw

When do states engage in status competition? The renewed emphasis within
the international relations literature on status, status attri- bution,
and status competition is a welcome trend. It is clear that [states care
about their status]{.underline} and [foreign policy makers perceive that
high status attribution is salient for them]{.underline}, for both
domestic political and foreign policy reasons. However, we raisefive
warn- ings about how scholars address status seeking and status
competition. **[First]{.underline}**, we suggest [that it is dangerous
to conflate competition and conflict between states as driven
exclusively or primarily by status considerations]{.underline}. [Policy
makers'desires to maintain or enhance their status may be a'fundamental
state motivation']{.underline}(Duque, 2018, p. 1) [but status concerns
likely constitute a variable condition rather than a constant state of
affairs for most states]{.underline}. If so, then we need to specify
conditions under which **[policy makers are motivated to engage in
international politics as a function of their need for greater status,
or from a fear of being unable to maintain the status they
have]{.underline}**. 8 It is a dis- torting academic exercise to assume
that almost all foreign policy activity is motivated by status
considerations, any more than solely by security concerns or domestic
political advantages. [Sometimes the desire to acquire a naval carrier
is based on perceived security needs rather than the need to acquire
a'status toy.']{.underline} **[Second]{.underline}**, we do not believe
that [the pursuit of greater status]{.underline}, [or the need to
maintain extant status]{.underline}, [should automatically equate to
status competition and conflict between states]{.underline}. In fact,
the conditions and constraints involved for states potentially engaging
in status-seeking policies are complex (as suggested in Figure 9), and
do not automatically lead to extensive competition or conflict. As we
had noted earlier, status-seeking strategies may depend in substantial
part on where states are positioned (e.g. Roren & Beaumont, 2018), and
the extent of their dissatisfaction with their positioning. [States on a
strong upward status trajectory]{.underline} (e.g. China)
[are]{.underline} likely to behave differently, and [likely to take
fewer risks than states in danger of losing their status]{.underline},
as their status is declining in the group hierarchy, or if they may be
in danger altogether of falling out of the status club (Russia).
Additionally, [it is unlikely that different types of status-seeking
strategies produce similar levels of inter-state competition or
conflict]{.underline}. Larson's work (Larson,2017; Larson &
Shevchenko,2003, 2010) applying SIT theory to status seeking suggests
that there are three broad, non-mutually exclusive strategies available
to states seeking higher status or for trying to keep the status they
have. These include social mobility (emulating the values of
higher-status states), social competition (striving to equal or surpass
the domi- nant group), and social creativity (revaluing a negative
characteristic as positive, or, iden- tifying a new dimension on which
the state may demonstrate superiority). While all three strategies can
generate substantial conflict between states, social mobility and social
crea- tivity generate fewer conflicts than social competition, and may
generate substantial cooperation (Duque,2018, p. 18). Which of these
strategies will be pursued by states seeking additional status, and the
conditions under which status seeking generates sub- stantial
inter-state conflict and competition requires substantial additional
theoretical and empirical work. **[Third]{.underline}**, [too much of
the extant scholarship ignores the premise that the global contexts in
which states operate are also likely to impact on the status seeking
options]{.underline}. For example, Wohlforth (2009) argues that [the
more stratified the distribution of capabilities in international
politics, the less the ambiguity about positioning, and the ability of
states to do something about their positioning]{.underline}. [The more
unambiguous and highly stra- tified the international system, the less
likely that states will engage in status competition]{.underline} as
especially membership in the most prestigious of status clubs is
exceedingly difficult to attain for states not already part of the
grouping. Wohlforth's warning is important: it suggests that [the
structure of the international system can significantly constrain
additional status seeking by states, even when policy makers prefer to
change their status positioning in international politics]{.underline}.
9 We have yet to assess systematically how these considerations impact
on the extent to which foreign policy makers will prioritize their
status concerns, and engage in further status seeking.
**[Fourth]{.underline}**, [there is substantial, unresolved controversy
in the status literature regarding the relevant reference point for
status seekers. Status is inherently both a relational and a per-
ceptual concept]{.underline}. [When policy makers assess their own
status, whether in terms of group membership or a hierarchical ordering,
what is the relevant reference group?]{.underline} The [literature has
offered]{.underline} at least [three competing answers]{.underline} to
this question. [One is that status is assessed in terms of 'aspiration':
unless one has already achieved positioning at the apex of a hier-
archy, states always seek entrance to status groups with higher
standing]{.underline}, and in terms of hierarchical ordering, are
typically seeking a higher position than what they perceive to have been
attributed to them. 10 A [second approach suggests that, at least for
major and regional powers, it is the global attribution of status by the
global community of states that matters]{.underline} (Bezerra, Cramer,
Hauser, Miller, & Volgy,2015; Miller et al.,2015). [A third perspective
suggests that it is pri- marily the 'relevant reference group' that
matters to policy makers, and there are two can- didates]{.underline}:
[status attribution in the hierarchy]{.underline} by other club members
(e.g. other members of the major power club), or, attribution by those
other states that [are deemed most relevant to the state in
question]{.underline} (Renshon,2017). Which states are relevant
reference points? For most states [status attribution may be
regional]{.underline}: their relative status compared to those states
with which they most often interact, and typically those would be other
states in the region. 11 [For rising powers]{.underline}, the [relevant
states]{.underline} [could]{.underline} [be]{.underline} both the [club
members]{.underline} where they are seeking entrance, [and]{.underline},
depending on whether or not such status seeking constitutes a zero-sum
game, [other rising powers seeking membership in the same
club]{.underline}. The appropriate reference group matters to policy
makers, and it should matter as well for our central question of the
extent to which status seeking by'rising'powers equals status
competition amongst them. Clearly, [such status seeking does not have to
result is status competition]{.underline}. Both [India and Brazil could
join the major power club without directly competing against each other
for additional status]{.underline}, unless the status marker is one of
gaining a permanent seat in the UN Security Council. China and Russia
can both increase their ranking in the major power hierarchy and need
not define such outcomes as constituting a zero-sum process. Of course,
[the extent to which such status seeking leads to substantial
competition and conflict also depends on the type of status seeking
strategy the two states would adopt]{.underline}. **[Status competition
and conflict is much more likely for states that seek to become regional
powers in the same region]{.underline}**. Two or more states seeking to
be recognized as the dominant power in a geopolitical space makes for
both competition and conflict; [Turkish and Iranian regional status
aspirations]{.underline} in the Middle East [are unlikely to be played
out in any manner short of a zero-sum context]{.underline}.
**[Fifth]{.underline}**, we caution that [status seeking strategies will
differ depending on the status tra- jectories and capability
trajectories of status aspiring states]{.underline}. [For instance, the
Russian Fed- eration]{.underline}---a state that lost its status in the
superpower club and for a while had difficulty maintaining its
membership in the major power club during the 1990s---has engaged in a
series of status seeking strategies (Larson & Shevchenko,2003; 2010;
Lewis,2018) that are dramatically different from that of China, a rising
power that entered the major power club after the Cold War's end and
continues developing rapidly its economic and military capabilities. It
is understandable that as the Soviet Union disintegrated, the resulting
Russian Fed- eration lost its predecessor's status as a superpower. What
is more interesting is that it [somehow maintained its status as a major
power, especially during the 1990s when its economy was in shambles, its
military beaten internally by a weak Chechen rebellion, and its
political system in disarray]{.underline}. 12 Such a halo effect for a
declining power is likely to cause substantial worry for its policy
makers as its status increasingly depends on its his- torical
positioning (and the path dependency associated with status attribution,
a point also underscored by Duque (2018)) while its capabilities to
sustain global activity and influence are in decline. Again, a
[comparison between the Russian Federation and China]{.underline} (which
emerged into the major power club around 1990 and with capabilities in
an upward trajectory) **[is a useful illustration regarding divergent
paths and difficulties associated with declining versus upward
trajectories in capabilities, status, and status
strategies]{.underline}**. The differences in relative capability
trajectories for these two states are obvious, as indicated inFigures
10and 11.In 1989, the Chinese economy was a bit smaller than that of the
Soviet Union; today it is roughly more thanfive times larger than that
of the Russian Federation (Figure 10). At the end of the Cold War
Chinese military spending was roughly ten percent of the Soviet Union;
by 2016, China's military spending was more than three times that of the
Russian Federation (Figure 11). On relative capabilities alone, and as
well on the trajectory of their capabilities, these two states have
dramatically different capacities to influence the course of
international affairs. To the extent that such capability trajectories
constrain status seeking strategies, these two states may not be
comparable; to the extent that the growth of capabilities may impact on
satisfaction with the status quo, they also signal substantial
differences in risk-taking for maintaining or increasing their status in
international affairs. The ebb andflow of recent current events
underscore the substantial differences between Russian risk-taking
versus Chinese caution. The capture of Crimea and the con- tinued
underwriting of tensions in eastern Ukraine, the strong military and
political invol- vement with the Assad regime in Syria, and the
aggressive interference in the American elections of 2016 are but a few
signs of such Russian risk-taking. Russian commitment to modernizing its
extensive nuclear forces even during times of economic difficulties, is
another. Meanwhile, [China has exhibited relatively risk-averse policies
outside of its immediate backyard]{.underline} (in the East and South
China seas), [ranging from a modest response to the U.S. bombing of its
Serbian embassy]{.underline} 13 in 1999 [through its quiet diplomacy and
negotiations with the U.S. in response to President Trump's blustering
denunciations and tariffthreats over China's trade
policies]{.underline}. **[It is difficult to decipher whether or not
this comparison is an anomaly or one that is typical of the differences
between declining versus rising powers, without a systematic and
comparative analysis that moves beyond these two cases]{.underline}**.
Nor can we tell without a more systematic and comparative analysis
across a broader variety of cases whether or not these differences in
strategies (high risk-taking and aggressive for the Russian Federation
and low-risk, conservative on the part of China) is a function of
differences in trajectories or their relative dissatisfaction with the
global status quo. Yet these two cases are strongly suggestive of
dramatically different status-seeking strategies by states with
different capa- bility trajectories and different issues related to
their relative status in the major power club's hierarchy. In this
sense, their approaches to status seeking may not be comparable. They
are also suggestive of another issue: the reference point for status
aspiration may be historical rather than contemporary, and not
necessarily one of competition and conflict between'rising'powers. [The
Russian status reference appears to be more in the context of its status
as attributed by Western states and powers]{.underline} (e.g.
Neumann,2016), [and not in comparison to China's rising
status]{.underline}. The Chinese reference may be in the context of its
historical focus on global South leadership, and thus may be at least as
con- cerned with its status among the entire community of states as much
as with those that are members of the major power club. In fact, there
is no reason to assume that status seeking among rising powers must be a
zero-sum game of competition and conflict. The creation of institutions
such as the BRICS and the G-20 illustrate that substantial cooperation
between rising powers can also achieve higher status. **[Whether rising
powers actually engage in status competition with each other may very
well depend on the nature of their trajectories, their membership in
major or regional power status clubs, and the extent of their
dissatisfaction with the pro- gress they are making in either seeking
club membership or their placement within the hierarchy once membership
is granted]{.underline}**. 14 [We need to assess systematically the
range of factors, both environmental and endogenous to rising powers
that would trigger or mini- mize status competition between
them]{.underline}; such work is not presently available in our knowl-
edge base regarding how'rising'powers seek additional status in world
politics. [The focus of this special issue is to add to that knowledge
base.]{.underline}

### 2AC/1NC -- Perception

#### Regime leaders pursue status through *[perceptive]{.underline}* measures not through *[material]{.underline}* advantages 

**Onea, PhD, 14**

(Dr. Tudor A. Onea is affiliated to the Department of International
Relations , Bilkent University. Dr. Tudor A. Onea is currently providing
services as Assistant Professor. Dr. Tudor A. Onea has authored and

Affairs, Political Science, International Relations and International
Security., *Between dominance and decline: status anxiety and great
power rivalry*, 131-132, // NC)

This link between individual and group status does not hold solely for
small groups but is also relevant for large groups. In modern settings,
individuals have a constant high stake in how their nation performs by
comparison to other nations, because the higher the nation's standing,
the stronger their pride as members of the nation and of its political
manifestation, the state. To quote Greenfeld: 'nationality elevated
every member of the community which it made sovereign. It guaranteed
status. National identity is, fundamentally, a matter of dignity. It
gives people reasons to be proud.'30 That is to say that
[decision-makers have to care about their state's standing for two
mutually reinforcing reasons. First, decision-makers identify with their
state because they are not only members of the nation-state themselves,
but also its chief representatives on the world stage]{.underline}.31
[Second, they would be compelled to cater to]{.underline} the sentiments
of their [domestic publics]{.underline} or/and of powerful domestic
groups [such as the military that regard status as a matter of national
prid]{.underline}e[. To this extent, maintaining]{.underline} their own
personal [status]{.underline} in their respective domestic societies [is
tied to how high or low is the status of the state
internationally]{.underline}. Moreover, even if leaders bow to domestic
pressure for their own interest, the demand for status conscientious
policies derives from a high psychological investment of citizens in the
international standing of their state.32 [Status]{.underline} may also
be significant because of two additional instrumental reasons: it [adds
to the coercive capacity of the state, and implicitly, to its security;
and it increases the state's soft power, and therefore bolsters its
legitimacy,]{.underline} which, in turn reduces its costs of exercising
leadership. However, these forms of instrumentality are more complex
than usually assumed. The straightforward connection between a state's
policy and its perception by other states has been contested [-- states
do not acquire a reputation for strength by firmness, or for weakness by
giving in to their opponents, **and consequently reputation is not
proven as producing material advantages**.]{.underline}33 Thus, [the
repeated concern of decision-makers for status in the absence of
verifiable material payoffs is easier to understand if status is
conceptualised as sought foremost for the **psychological satisfaction
it provides**]{.underline}, rather than for concrete benefits alone. It
is even conceivable that decision-makers who claim to act so as to
manipulate the opponent's perceptions of their strength are motivated by
deeper concerns for competitiveness in terms of status and for the
related need to maintain domestic morale.34 **[Furthermore, status may
add to the soft power of a state, but the pursuit of status may also
undermine soft power]{.underline}**.35 For instance, it could be argued
that the US intervened against Iraq in 2003 as a demonstration of
strength in the aftermath of 9/11. The military success of the Iraq war
enhanced (before the insurrection) the prestige of the US and
contributed to its status. But at the same time, it affected negatively
the soft power of the US in global polls.36 Hence, while this article
does not rule out that status may constitute a useful instrument, it
regards status primarily as an end in itself

#### ***[Status Anxiety]{.underline}* theory suggests larger differences in *[capabilities]{.underline}* and *[reputation]{.underline}* lead to more stability** 

**Onea, PhD, 14**

(Dr. Tudor A. Onea is affiliated to the Department of International
Relations , Bilkent University. Dr. Tudor A. Onea is currently providing
services as Assistant Professor. Dr. Tudor A. Onea has authored and
co-authored multiple peer-reviewed scientific papers and presented works
at many national and International conferences. Dr. Tudor A. Onea
contributions have acclaimed recognition from honourable subject experts
around the world. Dr. Tudor A. Onea is actively associated with
different societies and academies. Dr. Tudor A. Onea academic career is
decorated with several reputed awards and funding. Dr. Tudor A. Onea
research interests include International Relations, International
Affairs, Political Science, International Relations and International
Security., *Between dominance and decline: status anxiety and great
power rivalry*, 138-139, // NC)

[A final proviso must be made]{.underline} concerning statements by
French and British decision-makers [that reflect concern for the
upsetting of the balance of power by the rising state]{.underline}, and
as such may expose a long-term security risk, but may [also]{.underline}
convey concern [for the loss of dominant status]{.underline}. Both
interpretations may not be mutually exclusive, since decision-makers
'may try to balance power while at the same time struggling to alter
their status in the global hierarchy'.68 However, even as the two
interpretations remain difficult to tell apart, [the status anxiety
model may be to some extent more warranted]{.underline}. [The balance of
power model assumes that the most dangerous distribution of power is an
asymmetrical one]{.underline}, [in which a state possesses significantly
more capabilities]{.underline} (and presumably status) [relative its
competitors]{.underline}. Therefore, as long as the distribution of
power between states is even, no security threat is present. [But for
hegemonic theories]{.underline}, [such as status anxiety]{.underline},
the opposite is the case: **[the steeper the disparity of capabilities
and prestige, the more stable will the international hierarchy
be]{.underline}**. [Hence, the flatter the hierarchy becomes due to the
emerging symmetry in status between the declining dominant state and its
main contender]{.underline}, the higher the danger of rivalry.69 The
point is that, in both cases examined, the dominant state and the
next-in-line power were almost level in terms of status and
capabilities, which, if balance of power logic applied, should have
resulted in mutual forbearance. To this extent, the French and British
invocations of the balance of power may represent efforts to legitimise
the dominant power's resistance to the rising power.

#### Status seeking is intrinsic to state identity -- international power is rooted in social perception

**Barnhart, PhD, 16**

(Joslyn (2016), BA Reed College MA Claremont Mckenna PHD University of
California LA, Status Competition and Territorial Aggression: Evidence
from the Scramble for Africa, Security Studies, 25:3, 385-419, DOI:
10.1080/09636412.2016.1195620) //bc

[Humans are hardwired to care about their status]{.underline} as
individuals and [about the status of groups with which they
identify]{.underline}.7 Numerous scholars have demonstrated the
significant degree to which status concerns drive international
behavior.8 [International status is based on the collective beliefs of
others about a state's rank on admirable characteristics]{.underline}
[such as military capability, wealth, demographic position, or
diplomatic influence]{.underline}.9 Because objective rankings of status
dimensions are often impossible, **[a state's rank is rooted in social
perception]{.underline}**.10 [International status seeking is therefore
the process of attempting to shape other states']{.underline}
[perceptions]{.underline} [about one's rightful position]{.underline}
[in the international hierarchy]{.underline}.11 Status seeking may be an
innate human trait, but actors do not seek status at all times. [States
are particularly inclined to engage in status competition]{.underline}
[**when their status has been called into question** by an instance of
disrespect]{.underline} [or by a humiliating international
event]{.underline}. To show "respect" is to behave in a manner
consistent with another state's status; to show disrespect is to deny
another state the degree of esteem or consideration it feels entitled
to, thereby implicitly refusing to recognize the state's desired
status.12 [Disrespect]{.underline} therefore [depends upon a state's
expectation of how much consideration and deference it believes others
should show it,]{.underline} **[an expectation that is derived from the
state's identity]{.underline}**.13 Disrespect may assume an evaluative
form in which others clearly acknowledge but disregard a state's
interests or a nonevaluative form in which others ignore the state's
interests, rights, and concerns altogether.[14 An act of disrespect may
occur]{.underline} [within the public sphere for all to see or may occur
privately between two states. A state is humiliated when it believes
that its position has been lowered]{.underline} in the eyes of others
**[and that this lowered estimation will result in a future decline in
respect and deference]{.underline}**.15 [Humiliation]{.underline} may be
an intended act.16It [may]{.underline} also, however, [be
self-inflicted]{.underline} [when a state fails to live up to the
expectations of the international community. Defeat in conflict to a
"weaker" state,]{.underline} for instance, [or the involuntary loss of
territory]{.underline} to a lower-status state [will likely cause the
international community to question the state's position]{.underline}
within the international status hierarchy. Humiliating events occur on
the world stage and thereby threaten to generate common knowledge within
the international community of the state's decline.17 [Humiliated or
disrespected state]{.underline}s that have the capabilities to do so
**[will likely engage in competitive acts with the intention of
signaling the status]{.underline}** **[they expect to hold in the
international system]{.underline}**.18 Such [competitive acts may occur
within a bilateral environment as a disrespected state attempts to
signal to a particular rival]{.underline} [that it expects to be treated
with a greater degree of deference]{.underline}. [They may also occur
within]{.underline} more [multilateral environments as states
that]{.underline} have been humiliated or disrespected on the world
stage [attempt to shape the perceptions of the international
community]{.underline} of their right to heightened status.19
Competitive [status-seeking acts]{.underline} [may include efforts to
gain membership into elite clubs]{.underline} [through the acquisition
of symbols of great power]{.underline} or regional status.20 [They may
also include direct military confrontation]{.underline} or competition
over spheres of influence, the size of weapons arsenals, or the extent
of territorial holdings. Finally[, states may attempt to
demonstrate]{.underline} their [existing infl]{.underline}uence [over
international outcomes]{.underline} [in an effort to minimize the
appearance of influence]{.underline} by the superior state.21
Status-challenged states are likely to adopt competitive status-seeking
strategies aimed at asserting their "rightful position" in the
international system for two primary reasons.22 First, both [humiliation
and disrespect]{.underline} **[engender strong emotional
responses]{.underline}** such as anger [that increase the likelihood
that a state will behave in an aggressive manner]{.underline} toward
others.23 Such emotional reactions to humiliation will lead states to
first and foremost desire revenge upon the state that directly or
indirectly threatened their status.24 Second, [leaders of
disrespected]{.underline} and humiliated [states will desire to protect
the state's self-image]{.underline} [for fear that if they do not, **the
state will face demotion within the international status
hierarchy**]{.underline}.25 This demotion has both instrumental
consequences, in that [a decline in status is a decline in influence,
but also emotional consequences as well in that states,]{.underline}
like individuals, [seek to have a voice and to be respected by those in
their community]{.underline}. Such [states]{.underline} therefore [seek
clear and effective ways to establish common knowledge]{.underline}
within the international community of their state's distinctive
capabilities, their intention for the state to be perceived as high
status, and their expectation that others within the international
community will treat it accordingly.26

#### status competition isn't only on material power but also credibility and societal recognition

**Dal, Ph.D., 6/13/2019** (Emel Parlar, Associate Professor at Marmara
University's Department of International Relations, "Status competition
and rising powers in global governance: an introduction", Contemporary
Politics, ISSN: 1356-9775 (Print) 1469-3631, pp. 3-5, 6/13)micahw

Status and status-seeking behaviour in IR For a growing number of
scholars coming from different research traditions, **[status matters in
international politics]{.underline}** (Dafoe, Renshon, & Huth, 2014;
Paul, Larson, & Wohlforth, 2014b). [The increasing number of
studies]{.underline} [that dealt with status by using different theories
and methodologies is a clear result of the attributed importance to this
research theme in IR]{.underline}. Within the literature, [status
is]{.underline}, however, [overwhelmingly ascribed to the material
sources]{.underline} such as military and economic capabilities such as
industrial and technological wealth and power, military spending and
military might (Larson & Shevchenko, 2003; Larson & Shevchenko, 2010;
Paul, Larson, & Wohlforth, 2014; Wohlforth, 2009) [as well as material
related actions]{.underline} such as military assertiveness, state of
diplomatic relations, membership in major international organizations,
numbers of head-of-state visits or hosting the Olympics (Larson &
Shevchenko, 2003; Larson & Shevchenko, 2010; Pu, 2017), the possession
of superior technological capabilities (Larson and Shevchenko 2003,
2010) or nuclear weapons (O'Neill, 2001). Aside these material
attributions, in a very restricted number of studies status has also
been attached to fundamental values, political system or ideology,
culture or civilization or moral superiority (Carvalho & Neumann, 2014;
Luard, 1976; Neumann, 2014). [Despite the fact that a large body of
literature focuses on largely material foundation of
status]{.underline}, [it has been widely accepted that material strength
alone cannot bring higher status to states.]{.underline} [Status can
only be possible through achieving a certain level of international
legitimacy]{.underline} (Stephen, 2014). In fact, status is a social
phenomenon and is generally defined as 'a recognized position (a
hierarchy) in a social hierarchy, implying relations of dominance and
deference'. From that vantage point, in the existing literature [there
is an intrinsic relationship between status and the concepts like
identity, prestige, esteem and honour]{.underline} (Larson & Shevchenko,
2014). The literature also emphasizes that [social status refers to a
position in a social system with its distinctive array of designated
rights and obligations]{.underline}. [As such, status as a social
relation actually depends on recognition and acceptance]{.underline}.
This brings us to the question of status inconsistency. Status
inconsistency in international relations suggests that differences on
several status dimensions have certain consequences that can be observed
in states' foreign policy choices. A state may have high material
capabilities in terms of economic and military assets. However, in some
cases, it may not translate this achieved status into an ascribed status
due to the lack of status recognition. [A country with relatively little
material capacity can acquire higher ascribed status mainly due to the
positional closeness to the major powers]{.underline}. This type of
circumstances is referred as [status inconsistency]{.underline} [in
international relations]{.underline} which [may]{.underline} also [lead
to deviant behaviours, even conflict and violence]{.underline}. Volgy,
Corbetta, Grant, and Baird (2011) suggested that [status inconsistency
can be observed in both underachieving or overachieving
terms]{.underline}. Overachievers are the ones who have gained
over-recognition yet lacking critical material means associated with
that status while underachievers are not granted recognition even though
they have material capacity and will for that status (Volgy & Mayhall,
1995). Another point in the existing literature on status focuses on the
way states compare their status in a given society. In this sense,
[status communities are important theoretical innovation to understand
the way countries make status comparison]{.underline}. [It is often
acknowledged that status is relative and status inconsistency generally
occurs within specific referent status communities of peer
competitors]{.underline}. A significant portion of this literature draws
heavily from social comparison theory (SCT) which observes that
individuals judge their worth, and accordingly derive self-esteem,
through comparisons with others (Paul, Larson, & Wohlforth, 2014a, p.
17). According to this transposed argument, **[states become status
seekers because]{.underline}**, like individuals and groups, **[they
have an innate desire for favourable social status comparisons relative
to their peers]{.underline}** (Wohlforth, 2009, p. 35). Where social
comparisons help actors ask 'How do I rank relative to my peers?'
temporal comparisons help actors evaluate how they have improved or
declined over time (Clunan, 2009, p. 12). Who is rising, at which degree
and for which status? In the last decade, there has been tremendous
changes in rising powers' global governance policies as a result of huge
power transitions from the Global North to the Global South and the rise
of salience of informal groupings or status clubs in the international
system. This shift has made rising powers both more cooperative and
competitive in many issue areas of global governance from global
financial governance to the funding of the UN and its special agencies,
from development cooperation to climate change. [The uncertainties
around which power is rising]{.underline}, which is not also [adds
additional confusion to the assessment of these states' status seeking
policies]{.underline}. In their article collected under this cluster on
status competition and rising powers, Thomas J. Volgy and Kelly Marie
Gordell touch upon the uncertainties around the way the so-called rising
powers are rising, have risen or declining and try to assess the linkage
between the typology of rising power and their differing status-seeking
strategies emanating from the variations observed in their rise.
[Despite extensive scholarly attention to the concepts of rising power
and status/status-seeking]{.underline}, [contestation regarding the
classification of state powers]{.underline} (which are rising, which are
declining, and what status clubs are considered to be the targets of
rising powers), the conditions under which status concerns play a role
for the rising powers, and the consequences that can exist when it comes
to global and regional forms of governance [continues to
remain]{.underline}. As suggested by Volgy and Gordell, lack of
consensus regarding even categorization of 'rising' versus 'risen', or,
declining powers for instance becomes problematic in attempts to better
understand how status is regarded by states, and the potential for it to
influence state actions, and the variation in risks that states are
willing to take when they are rising, have risen, or are in decline.
Indeed, countries thought of as 'rising powers' ultimately vary since
the term rising can be understood in a variety of ways. For instance,
Volgy and Gordell argue that states such as Brazil, China, India,
Russia, and South Africa stand apart from the other states like Turkey.
This is in terms of opportunity, willingness, and overall competence to
operate as major or regional powers for example, among the broader
community of states. These states and their actions and interactions
have consequences for the extent of global and regional governance
within the international political system. As underlined by Volgy and
Gordell [there exists a linkage between the level of rise of the
so-called rising powers and their status competition
policies]{.underline}. For the authors, [the majority of states within
this special issue have effectively risen, with some potentially still
rising]{.underline} (from one grouping to another), while two countries,
Iran and Turkey, are considered at best rising, but with uncertain
trajectories. [Whether the rising powers may or may not create influence
in the international system determines the extent to which these states
need to engage in status competition]{.underline}. In short, [not all
rising powers can upgrade their status by the adoption of status
competition strategies since their 'rise' and their rising actorness
depend on the positive impact they may create vis--vis the major powers
and other actors of the current international system]{.underline}. Some
status-seeking strategies are much more appropriate for a group of
rising powers, while others are not.

[The more status competition will extend to additional

rivalry.**]{.underline}

### 2AC/1NC -- Power = Zero Sum

#### **Positions of power are zero-sum -- challenges to those positions is the fundamental cause of rivalry**

**Onea, PhD, 14**

(Dr. Tudor A. Onea is affiliated to the Department of International
Relations , Bilkent University. Dr. Tudor A. Onea is currently providing
services as Assistant Professor. Dr. Tudor A. Onea has authored and

at many national and International conferences. Dr. Tudor A. Onea
contributions have acclaimed recognition from honourable subject experts

decorated with several reputed awards and funding. Dr. Tudor A. Onea
research interests include International Relations, International
Affairs, Political Science, International Relations and International
Security., *Between dominance and decline: status anxiety and great
power rivalry*, 152-153, // NC)

[Rivalries between great powers over dominance have captured scholarly
attention since the days of Thucydides. However, the bulk of studies
have concentrated on shifts in capabilities, while **neglecting the
motives that produce such positional rivalries.** The status
anxiety]{.underline} hypothesis is an effort to **[address this
omission]{.underline}**, [by tracing their occurrence and
continuation]{.underline}, sometimes for decades, to the clashing status
requirements of the dominant power and those of the next-in-line state.
In a nutshell, [status anxiety argues that the refusal of the dominant
power to allow the succession of the challenger will be a **fundamental
cause of rivalry,**]{.underline} [worsening as the rising power
threatens to overtake the current leader in additional
dimensions]{.underline}. The purpose of this article was to formulate
this hypothesis as well as subject it to preliminary testing. **[The
findings suggest that status anxiety represented a significant
influence]{.underline}**, though not necessarily excluding additional
balance of power considerations, [in the foreign policy decision-making
of declining dominant powers]{.underline}: France in the mid-eighteenth
century and Britain at the turn of the twentieth century. [Status
anxiety thus helps account for the hostility France manifested towards
Britain]{.underline}, [and Britain towards German demands for superior
status]{.underline}, a reaction which is more problematic to account for
by theories stressing solely physical security and material gains. A
further contribution of this endeavour is that it suggests the existence
of a dominant power club, distinct from the club of major or great
powers, and, as such, following a different set of rules.138 [The
existing status literature has concentrated exclusively on]{.underline}
the latter club, [arguing]{.underline} persuasively that [there is no
impediment]{.underline} for granting either admission to new members or
opportunities [for further advancement to current ones]{.underline}.139
[Hence, status competition in the great power club is seen as nonzero
sum. But the dominant power club has a membership of one]{.underline},
which makes it unfeasible for the dominant power to satisfy the demands
of dominant power aspirants without voluntarily surrendering its
supremacy. Accordingly, [status competition over the **dominant position
is more likely to be zero-sum** and lead to rivalry.]{.underline} The
findings of this article are at this point only plausible, yet they
highlight the need for further research covering the entire universe of
dominant powers in order to determine both if intense status anxiety
always prompts conflict and if reduced status anxiety or its absence
lead to stability. Consequently, additional studies of the role of
status for dominant powers foreign policy should be conducted, extending
beyond the current en vogue concentration on the foreign policy of
rising powers alone[. Indeed, dominant powers' status anxiety may be
increasingly policy-relevant, if unipolarity were to erode due to a
steady shrinking of distance between the US and China]{.underline}.

#### Status seeking among rising powers is not Zero-sum only if the nature of their trajectories is similar 

**Volgy and Gordell, PhD, 19**

Thomas J., Kelly M., Thomas John Volgy is a Professor of Political

*Rising powers, status competition, and global governance: a closer look

In fact, **[there is no reason to assume that status seeking among
rising powers must be a zero-sum game of competition and
conflict]{.underline}**. [The creation of institutions such as the BRICS
and the G-20 illustrate that substantial cooperation between rising
powers can also achieve higher status]{.underline}. [Whether rising
powers actually engage in status competition]{.underline} with each

membership in major or regional power status clubs,]{.underline}** and
the extent of their dissatisfaction with the progress they are making in
either seeking club membership or their placement within the hierarchy

that may actually stimulate status competition between rising powers.
For example, enlarging the permanent membership in the UN Security
Council may pit one rising power against another; enlargement may also

members.]{.underline} View all notes We need to assess systematically

this special issue is to add to that knowledge base.

### 2AC/1NC -- Competition Inevitable

#### *[Social comparison theory]{.underline}* proves absent objective measures of success competition for favorable social status is inevitable 

**Freedman, PhD,** 8-26-20**15**

(Joshua Freedman is a Visiting Assistant Professor of Politics at
Oberlin College (starting fall 2019), where he teaches courses on global
politics, international law, international security, and the
Israeli-Palestinian Conflict. Freedman earned a Ph.D. (June 2019) from
the Department of Political Science at Northwestern University, Status
