[https://journals.sagepub.com/doi/pdf/10.1177/1354066115603781
//](https://journals.sagepub.com/doi/pdf/10.1177/1354066115603781%20//)
NC)

A significant portion of the [IR literature draws heavily from
behavioral economics and social psychology]{.underline}, and their
respective observations that individuals judge their worth, and
accordingly derive self-esteem, through comparisons with others (Larson
et al., 2014: 17). [According to]{.underline} Leon Festinger's (1954:
118) [social comparison theory logic, in the absence of "objective
evidence," individuals will make self-evaluative judgments about their
opinions and abilities by comparing themselves]{.underline} [with the
opinions and abilities of their peers.]{.underline} Experiments in the
SCT tradition have subsequently shown that individuals are highly
sensitive to their relative position. When individuals engage in
downward comparisons --- those made with people worse off --- they
appear to reduce distress and enhance self-esteem, while upward
comparisons --- those made with people better off --- can be
"demoralizing, because one is forced to face one's own inferiority"
(Wood, 1989: 239). In economics, the logic of SCT has informed debate on
the individual utility of absolute versus relative consumption.
[Experiments operating in this tradition have shown that, contrary to
traditional economic models premised on the notion that an individual's
sense of well-being derives from their absolute standard of living, it
is actually an individual's relative living standard]{.underline} that
proves to be a far more important economic determinant (Frank, 1999:
109). As Frank (2005: 137--140) showed across a number of experiments,
individuals increasingly favor scenarios that give them larger homes and
higher salaries relative to their peers than those that give them a
greater absolute value in home size and salary but a lower position in
these assets relative to their peers. Frank (2004: 140) concluded, in
effect, that "positional concerns loom large in human motivation" to
such an extent that individuals appear willing to sacrifice absolute
consumption for relative gains. 802 European Journal of International
Relations 22(4) Likewise, where individuals derive parts of their
identity from membership in various social groups (Tajfel, 1978), they
will also derive significant self-esteem from comparing the achievements
and qualities of their in-group to those of a reference group (Larson
and Shevchenko, 2010: 68). [According to this transposed argument,
states become status seekers because,]{.underline} as with individuals
and groups, **[they have an innate desire for favorable social status
comparisons]{.underline}** relative to their peers (Wohlforth, 2009:
35). Larson and Shevchenko (2010: 68, 70, 86) argue, for example, that
China primarily compares its achievements to Japan, the US, and Russia,
and that unfavorable comparisons with these actors are a main source of
Beijing's status insecurity. Thus, the literature on status in world
politics is often framed in the language of accommodation, and
adjustment (Larson et al., 2014: 4), [which presupposes that **status
insecurities develop from lateral social comparisons and can be resolved
through lateral improvements**.]{.underline}

#### The drive for [status]{.underline} is inevitable but checked by [stable leadership]{.underline} --- threats to power encourages [status-seeking]{.underline} and [imperialism]{.underline}

**Barnhart, 16** - Assistant Professor of Government (Joslyn, \"Status
Competition and Territorial Aggression: Evidence from the Scramble for
Africa\", Security Studies, 7-8-2016, Vol 25, Issue 3, p. 417-419))SK

This article has demonstrated that [humiliated]{.underline} and
disrespected [states are likely to engage in **competitive
status-seeking behavior**]{.underline} and that they are often willing
to do so [in spite of]{.underline} their material and [strategic
interests]{.underline}. The cases above demonstrate that responses to
humiliation and disrespect need not involve demonstrations of one's
military effectiveness relative to that of potential rivals. France, in
a move that undoubtedly changed few minds about its military capability
relative to that of potential European rivals, handily acquired control
of the bankrupt Tunisian government within less than two weeks.
Similarly, [Germany's initial act of imperialism involved little more
than planting a flag at a remote]{.underline}, barren port. Rather,
[both states sought to signal their expectation of holding higher status
by exercising the prerogatives associated with their desired
status]{.underline}. Because it often threatens the status, security,
and interests of other states, [status seeking engendered by humiliation
and disrespect often has a significant impact on international
behavior]{.underline}. [Germany's decision to become an empire, for
instance, generated status concerns for Italy, leading it to embark upon
a colonial policy of its own]{.underline}.146 While in 1882 Minister of
Foreign Affairs Pasquale Mancini privately decried the system of
territorial colonies as "sterile and harmful \... a source of weakness
rather than of strength," in May 1884, he ordered the occupation of
Massawa in current-day Eritrea in January of 1885.147 The timing of this
shift in attitude can be pinpointed almost exactly to Bismarck's
imperial debut. [The Italian statesman came to see German actions as
setting a new precedent that threatened the relegation of Italy to a
lower status]{.underline}.148 [A]{.underline}s one [diplomat
stated]{.underline} at the time, "[Germany had acted; Italy must
act]{.underline}."149 If Italy were to ever acquire its "place in the
sun," it would have to "abandon prudence" and engage in costly African
expansion like the other European powers.150 The [French and German
adoption of forward policies in Africa also presented direct threats to
Britain's unchallenged status as the preeminent seafaring
power]{.underline}. With the French ratification of the Congo treaty and
the possibility of French ownership of much of the west coast of Africa,
the British began to detect a consistent French policy of anti-British
antagonism.151 [British leaders]{.underline} also [increasingly
confronted the antagonistic intentions]{.underline} of the "young and
ambitious" Germany, which was continuing its assault on Britain's "Munro
\[sic\] doctrine" in the west. Over time, [British leaders came to
realize that if Britain were to remain a preeminent power, **they could
not allow themselves to be "cheeked by Bismarck** or anyone
else]{.underline}."152 [Without a "positive and vigorous effort" to
assert British rights as the preeminent power, Britain would be left
behind, suffering the inevitable **decline of an empire**.]{.underline}
As The Times put it in 1891, "[With the colonies massed around us we can
hold our]{.underline} own in the ranks of the world [Power]{.underline}s
\... [without them we must sink to the position of a merely European
kingdom]{.underline} a position [which]{.underline} for England [entails
slow but sure decay]{.underline}."153 British leaders agreed, with
Neville Chamberlain stating that a failure to expand further would
"strike at the root cause of our great position."154 [By the turn of the
century, Britain---the once reluctant imperialist---had acquired
possession of over roughly one-quarter of the entire African
continent]{.underline}.155 The European powers collectively had assumed
control of 95 percent of the entire African continent. While concerns
about status have not generated similar competition for territory over
the last century, [recent territorial claims by **Russia and China**
suggest that the **potential for status-driven conquest
remains**]{.underline}.156 The likelihood that states in the
contemporary system will seek status through territorial expansion
increases in cases in which states' spheres of interest are disrespected
by prominent powers within the international community. [Territorial
expansion]{.underline}, however, [is only one of many ways that states
in the contemporary international system may assert their status

may choose to lead multilateral interventions, to conduct weapons tests,

the exact competitive status-seeking strategy contemporary states engage
in, we should expect the general conditions outlined in this article to
apply. States that have been humiliated or disrespected will be likely
to engage in competitive behaviors in an effort to secure their status.
[As with French and German conquests in Africa in the 1880s, the actions
such states take in the contemporary world are likely to have
far-reaching consequences.]{.underline}

### 2AC/1NC -- Multipolarity Bad

#### Psychologically, Multipolar or Bipolar positions of power incentivize *[more competition]{.underline}* because of similarities in status 

**Garcia et al. PhD, 13**

(Stephen M., Avishalom Tor, Tryrone M. Schif, Notre Dame Law School,
Stephen M. Garcia got his Ph.D. at Princeton University, Avishalom Tor

Similarity. As the perceived similarity of target to actor increases, so
do comparison concerns and thus competitiveness. [Similarity refers both
to similarity in terms of ability or performance on the comparison
dimension]{.underline} (Festinger, 1954, p. 120). For instance, two
golfers are similar to the extent that their performance is similar. Yet
similarity can also refer to similarity of personal characteristics or
attributes more generally, beyond the specific comparison dimension.
Experiments in this vein (Goethals & Darley, 1977) showed [that people
tend to compare themselves with others who are most similar to
them]{.underline} along a number of different attributes that are not
necessarily relevant to the specific comparison context (e.g., "dot
estimation ability" that was determined by false feedback; D. T. Miller,
Turnbull, & McFarland, 1988). Thus, two Psychology of Competition 5
academics who are both persons of color, from the same university, from
a similar PhD vintage, and working in the same field are highly similar
in terms of personal characteristics and are more likely prone toward
mutual social comparison. **[The inclination to compare with similar
others--- whether in terms of performance or
characteristics---]{.underline}** **[begets competitive behavior toward
them]{.underline}**. To illustrate, [one experiment found that
competitive performance]{.underline}--- measured by reaction time to a
geometric-shape recognition task---[increased with the degree of
performance similarity with a rival in competitive
situations]{.underline} (Dakin & Arrowood, 1981). To the extent that
hostility is related to competitiveness, "horizontal hostility" research
(White & Langer, 1999; White et al., 2006) has showed that comparison
concerns increase among similar minority groups, who strive to see their
own group as superior to another, similar group. Among similar minority
groups, the one that is further removed from the mainstream harbors
greater hostility to its less extreme minority counterparts. Examples
include vegans' greater hostility attitudes toward vegetarians (White et
al., 2006) or punks' more hostile attitudes toward gothics (White et
al., 2006). [However, comparison concerns brought about by similarity
among groups also generate competitive rivalry]{.underline}. For
example, [an analysis of a data set on N]{.underline}ational
[C]{.underline}ollegiate [A]{.underline}thletic
[A]{.underline}ssociation [basketball teams]{.underline} [found that a

proximity, performance histories, and academic quality---**[led to more
intense experiences of rivalry and competitiveness]{.underline}** for
their players (Kilduff et al., 2010).

### 2AC/1NC -- Logic of Emulation

#### Status competition drives states to the logic of emulation -- attempting to acquire status symbols and credibility through military or economic buildup 

**Ward, PhD, 19**

(Steven, PhD from Georgetown University's Department of Government in
2012, MA in Security Studies from Georgetown's Edmund A. Walsh School of
Foreign Service. "Logics of Stratified Identity Management in World
Politics." International Theory, vol. 11, no. 2, 2019, pp. 211--238.,
doi:10.1017/S175297191800026X.)//bc

The other category of [responses to national status
dissatisfaction]{.underline} [involves]{.underline} [promoting or
working collectively toward some sort of change in the state's
position]{.underline} in or orientation toward the international social
hierarchy. These logics are collective: in SIT's terms, [they aim at
achieving some sort of social change for the entire
in-group]{.underline}. [Responses]{.underline} [in this category are
still usefully understood as phenomena that vary at the individual
level.]{.underline} Different individuals, situated differently within
the national community, with different psychological profiles and
different interests, may prefer and promote different approaches to
redressing a national status deficit. Responses remain collective so
long as they are premised on the maintenance of a strong affiliation
with the state as an identity category, and so long as they are meant to
work by changing the state's position in or orientation toward the
international status hierarchy. I draw on existing distinctions familiar
from social psychological work on intergroup status dynamics to identify
three collective logics of stratified identity management: emulation of
in-groups with higher standing; transformation of some element of the
intergroup comparative situation; and rejection of status quo norms,
rules, institutions, and political arrangements. Emulation Emulation
refers to any effort to improve the group's status by acquiring
symbolically significant resources that are consensually valued and
thought necessary for the achievement of higher status. In short, the
[logic of emulation]{.underline} **[involves an effort undertaken by or
on behalf of an in-group to climb an existing status
'ladder]{.underline}**' -- [to acquire admirable characteristics or
competently perform consensually valued practices]{.underline} in order
to convince relevant others to recognize that the group belongs to a
higher status position than it currently occupies (or to defend an
existing -- but insecure -- position). Notably, this conceptualization
says nothing about the substance of moves informed by the logic of
emulation. That is driven by the nature of what Nexon and Neumann
(following Bourdieu) call capital in the particular social field within
which advanced position is sought. 39 The kinds of characteristics and
performances that are consensually valued -- that are thought to be
status enhancing -- vary across different status communities, different
hierarchies, and over time. In some contexts, for example, [symbols of
military and economic power are understood as the most relevant forms of
status-producing capital]{.underline}. Nexon and Neumann suggest that
[this is typically true of status communities that occupy the top of the
international hierarchy]{.underline} -- in other words**[, to establish
a claim to 'great power' status, or 'world power' status, or to compete
for the position of hegemon,]{.underline}** **[a state will need to
acquire symbolically significant resources]{.underline}** [and
competently perform symbolically significant practices]{.underline}
**[that indicate a certain level of advancement in economic and military
fields]{.underline}**. 40 Still, [the specific form that symbolically
significant practices]{.underline} and resources [take]{.underline} is
constructed and [varies over time]{.underline}. In the late 19th and
early 20th centuries, salient symbols of 'world power' status were
battleships and colonies. Thus, elites in Wilhelmine Germany and
Imperial Japan who promoted efforts to gain entry into the 'world power'
club did so by advocating the construction of large navies and the
acquisition of empires. 41 Over the next several decades, the symbolic
significance of empire was reversed, and [battleships were replaced by
aircraft carriers, space programs, and nuclear weapons as geopolitically
significant status symbols]{.underline}. 42 [Other]{.underline} -- less
violent and conflict prone -- [resources]{.underline} and performances
[may also be understood as capital and sought for reasons related to the
competition for status]{.underline}. For example, Rhamey and Early argue
that Olympic medals increase status, which implies that investing in
athletic prowess is a means of competing for position in global or
regional hierarchies. 43 Towns argues that [practices and institutions
related to gender have also been]{.underline} -- in various ways --
[symbolically significant markers of status.]{.underline} [During the
late 19th and early 20th centuries, for instance, political institutions
that excluded women from power signaled civilization and
advancement.]{.underline} More recently, [political institutions that
aim at inclusion have become symbolically valuable]{.underline}, and
Towns suggests that [the incentive to compete for status is a critical
part of the explanation for the spread of these institutions around the
world]{.underline}. 44 Neumann also argues that even the status
community at the top of the global hierarchy -- the 'great powers' --
has had not just a material and geopolitical basis but also an
institutional and cultural component. [Competition for
status]{.underline} within this community [requires not just acquiring
symbolically significant military or economic resources]{.underline}
(like battleships) or competently performing symbolically significant
military or economic practices (like empire building) **[but also
adopting consensually valued political and cultural institutions and
practices (like democracy or free markets).]{.underline}** 45 [The logic
of emulation subsumes a number of important arguments]{.underline}
[about how states seek]{.underline} (or defend) their
[status]{.underline}. For instance, Renshon argues that [status
dissatisfaction leads states to start wars or militarized disputes as a
way of publicly demonstrating military capacity]{.underline}. This works
because [military capacity is a 'valuable attribute or possession' that
drives status attribution]{.underline} in international relations. 46
Thus, to the extent that [improving and demonstrating military
capacity]{.underline} is a strategy for seeking status, it [functions
via the logic of emulation]{.underline}: by **[advancing the
status-seeker's position along a consensually valued dimension of
comparison in the hope of thereby securing recognition from relevant
others.]{.underline}** Similarly, Barnhart argues that [states whose
status is under threat respond by acquiring symbolically significant
resources to strengthen the foundation of their position]{.underline}.
During the late 19th and early 20th centuries, within the 'great power'
status community, this involved competitions for colonial territory,
since empire was, at the time, a symbol of great power status. 47 The
logic of emulation subsumes two of Larson and Shevchenko's
status-seeking strategies. [Social competition]{.underline}, according
to Larson and Shevchenko[, refers to an attempt by a lower status state
to catch or pass a higher status state along the dimensions of
comparison that constitute the latter's advantage.]{.underline} Since,
according to Larson and Shevchenko, [international status hierarchies
are typically founded on military and economic power,]{.underline}
social competition entails the acquisition of symbolically significant
military and economic resources. On the other hand, social mobility
refers -- in Larson and Shevchenko's translation -- to status
advancement via the adoption of the practices, norms, and values of
higher status states. 48 Though these strategies involve substantively
different sets of behaviors in Larson and Shevchenko's framework --
[arms racing]{.underline}, for instance, on the one hand;
[democratization]{.underline}, for example, on the other -- the [logic
by which each is supposed to result in higher status is the
same]{.underline}. **[Both work by advancing the state's position along
consensually valued dimensions of comparison]{.underline}** in [order to
convince relevant others to recognize the former's status
claims.]{.underline}

### 2AC/1NC -- ! -- Distancing

#### Weak national status causes individuals to distance themselves from the state by identifying with larger ethnic or smaller subnational groups

**Ward, PhD, 19**

(Steven, PhD from Georgetown University's Department of Government in
2012, MA in Security Studies from Georgetown's Edmund A. Walsh School of
Foreign Service. "Logics of Stratified Identity Management in World
Politics." International Theory, vol. 11, no. 2, 2019, pp. 211--238.,
doi:10.1017/S175297191800026X.)//bc

[Changes in identification can insulate an individual from the negative
social psychological consequences of low, inadequate, or threatened
national status]{.underline} in two broad ways: first, [by weakening the
individual's attachment to the state]{.underline}, [thereby reducing the
degree to which the individual's self-esteem depends on the state's
status]{.underline} [and]{.underline}, second, [by]{.underline} -- to
some extent -- [replacing the national identity category within the
individual's social identity with some other significant in-group that
contributes more positively to the individual's
self-conception.]{.underline} Identification dynamics can function at
different levels of aggregation, with different consequences for
politics and foreign policy. The most obvious way in which
[dissatisfaction with national status may prompt a change in
identification is by weakening ties to the state relative to subnational
identity categori]{.underline}es. [This is the inverse of]{.underline}
the [nation-building dynam]{.underline}ic that Sambanis, Skaperdas, and
Wohlforth link to state status gains: they argue that [victory in war
increases national status]{.underline}, [which incentivizes individuals
to identify more strongly with the state than with subnational identity
categories]{.underline}. Reversing this logic implies that [reductions
in the state's status produce incentives for **individuals to identify
more weakly with the state and more strongly with subnational
groups.**]{.underline} Examples of this dynamic are easy to identify.
[The loss of Spain's last American possessions after the 'Disaster' of
1898]{.underline} [was felt as a devastating reduction in Spanish status
in some quarters]{.underline}. It constituted for many 'a crisis of both
identity and legitimacy: identity because their own self-esteem was
bound up with that of the nation; legitimacy because those to whom they
had, willingly or unwillingly, entrusted the interests of the nation had
signally failed to defend those interests'. 23 One consequence was that
[some subnational identity groups were strengthene]{.underline}d. For
instance, [the Disaster 'reinforced the feeling among many Catalans that
they had a separate national identity]{.underline} [and a different
historical destiny and that these were incompatible with those of
Castile']{.underline}. 24 The loss of status that accompanied [the
erosion of the British Empire after World War]{.underline}
[II]{.underline} [had a similar effect on the strength and coherence of
the 'British' national identity]{.underline}. David McCrone notes that
'the loss of empire eroded \[the British\] identity at home and abroad',
with a variety of consequences. 25 For one thing, this likely
contributed to the increased salience of sub-British and especially
racial identity categories: Enoch Powell's 'hostility to immigration
from the Black Commonwealth', for instance, flowed from an '"ethnic"
definition of nationality', and 'rested on his recognition that England
lay at the core, and that in many respects "Britain" had come to an
end'. 26 It is also likely that [the loss of the British Empire
contributed in some manner to the increasing salience of Scottish
identity]{.underline} in the decades after the end of World War II. 27
[Concerns about the implications of lost status on the strength of
national identity]{.underline} and the prospects for national
disintegration [are also central to understanding Charles de Gaulle's
desire]{.underline} [to return France to the ranks of the 'world powers'
during the 1960s.]{.underline} 28 There is even evidence that [recurrent
anxiety about relative national decline]{.underline} has [contributed to
partisan animosity in the United States over the past three
decades]{.underline}. 29 Thus, one novel insight that emerges from this
framework is that anxiety about or dissatisfaction with national status
may trigger or exacerbate processes that reduce the strength of national
identity categories and hence unravel or disintegrate political
coherence inside the state. [Identification dynamics can also operate at
trans- or supra-national levels]{.underline}, in which case they may
have very different consequences. It is possible, for instance, that an
[individual could compensate for inadequate or unsatisfying national
status]{.underline} **[by identifying more strongly with an identity
category constituted at a higher level of aggregation than the
state]{.underline}**. Social psychologists refer to this sort of process
as '[recategorization']{.underline}. A classic example comes from
post--Cold War Germany. [East Germans dealt with negative social
comparisons vis--vis higher status West Germans]{.underline} [by
tending to identify more strongly simply as 'Germans'.]{.underline} 30
The [Anglo-American power transition]{.underline} during the late 19th
and early 20th centuries [provides another example of
recategorization]{.underline} at work. [Although the rise of the United
States as a continental hegemon presented a serious threat to
British]{.underline} [status]{.underline}, it [did not generate intense
Anglo-American rivalry.]{.underline} 31 Part of the reason was that some
[British elites dealt]{.underline} with this development by
**[identifying more broadly with the Anglo-Saxon ethno-linguistic
family]{.underline}** and celebrating its apparently glorious future. 32
[Cecil Spring-Rice]{.underline}, for instance, [was a long-time British
diplomat and became ambassador to the United States during World War
I]{.underline}. [He was resigned to the inevitability of British decline
**but took comfort in the fact that the United States was 'the real
fortress of our race'**]{.underline}. 33 According to Anderson, 'the
contemplation of America's growing strength gave \[Spring-Rice\] much
satisfaction'. 34 Spring-Rice was not alone: other British leaders made
similar references to 'race patriotism', as did some American leaders.
35 This likely had real consequences for Anglo-American relations:
Anderson suggests that 'the most powerful incentive among the British
populace toward support of American imperialism was the assumption of
Anglo-American racial affinity'. 36 The upshot was that the expansion of
American power and influence -- in important ways a source of
consternation for British leaders and elites concerned about their place
in the world -- became a source of pride. 37 Both versions of this logic
involve [shifts in individual identification as responses to national
status dissatisfaction]{.underline}. Both have firm foundations in
insights from the same social psychological frameworks that have
informed existing analyses of status in IR. They also have potentially
significant implications for both domestic politics and foreign policy.
Yet i[dentification dynamics linked to changes in or anxiety about
national status remain largely ignored]{.underline} or at least
unstudied by analysts interested in understanding the role of status in
international politics. 38

### 2AC/1NC -- ! -- Rivalries

#### yes impact to status competition -- opportunity, willingness, and competence is important.

**Volgy\* and Gordell\*\*, 6/3/2019** (Thomas J. Volgy\*, Professor of
Political Science at the University of Arizona and the Executive
Director of the International Studies Association; and Kelly Marie
Gordell\*\*, International Relations PhD candidate at the School of
Government and Public Policy at the University of Arizona, "Rising
powers, status competition, and global governance: a closer look at
three contested concepts for analyzing status dynamics in international
politics", Contemporary Politics, ISSN: 1356-9775 (Print) 1469-3631, pp.
15-16, 6/3)micahw

[We do not believe that our concerns about rising, status maintaining,
and status declin- ing powers is a trivial one]{.underline}. In fact,
[the strategies needed, and the risks that states are willing to take
for status purposes very much revolve around the'status of their
status']{.underline}. The risk- taking actions of the Russian Federation
are dramatically different from that of China. These differences are
not, we claim, simply historical or cultural in nature (e.g. Freedman,
2016; Kotkin,2016) but have to do as much with their respective current
trajectories and the consequent risks their policy-makers are willing to
take under these different circumstances. We offer less certainty
regarding Iranian and Turkish aspirations and the likelihood of their
successes. [The extent to which we can distinguish particular countries
as powers from the broader community of states largely depends
on]{.underline} three components: **[opportunity, willingness, and
competence]{.underline}**. As rising powers, these two [states have the
potential to gain membership to a high status-generating
club]{.underline}. [To do so, however, would require,]{.underline} even
for regional power status, [not only a vast commitment to increasing
their economic and mili- tary clout, but as well a substantial
commitment to their region of interest]{.underline} (the Middle East,
presumably). Even then, regional power status would only be conferred if
one of the two would substantially exceed the other both in capabilities
and activism in the region. That is not likely in the near future: the
costs, both domestic and international, would be too high for such a
status race. What would such a status race mean for governance,
especially regional governance in the Middle East? The role of rising
powers, as they navigate between the givens of their regions and the
pressures of global governance, is uncertain (e.g. Destradi, Nolte, &
Prys-Hansen,2018). In the case of Iran and Turkey, their limitations are
not the only issue. These two states (along with several other Middle
Eastern countries) are also sub- stantially constrained in a region by
several other regional power pretenders that also lack the capabilities
to dominate the region, but are caught in a dense set of rivalries that
make it exceedingly difficult for them not to resist the rise of a
regional power (Volgy, Gordell, & Bezerra,2019). One major lesson of the
[post-Cold War]{.underline} era, with respect to global governance, has
been that [it is far easier to create new regional institutions than to
create global ones]{.underline} (Volgy et al.,2009). No wonder then,
that much of the new formal architectural construction since 1990 has
been regional in scope, in Latin America (Brazil), Southern Africa
(South Africa) post-Soviet space (Russia), and Asia (China). Turkey and
Iran face different chal- lenges in their region: [there is no other
region as conflict-ridden andfilled with as many strategic rivalries as
the Middle East]{.underline} (Volgy et al.,2019), [making regional
governance exceedingly difficult]{.underline}, even if there was a
dominant power. 16 Ultimately, [the inability of rising powers to
effectively establish new architecture within this environment suggests
the need to keep this track record in mind when evaluating their
status-seeking strategies]{.underline}.

### 2AC/1NC -- ! -- War

#### Status competition causes war --- only [unipolarity]{.underline} can dampen conflict 

**Wohlforth, '9** - Professor of Government in the Dartmouth College
Department of Government (William C., \"Unipolarity, Status Competition,
and Great Power War\", Cambridge Core, January 2009,
https://www.cambridge.org/core/journals/world-politics/article/unipolarity-status-competition-and-great-power-war/3BCD0B8AB97EB360AD84C32658AF6906))SK

The [evidence suggests that narrow]{.underline} and asymmetrical
capabilities [gaps foster **status competition**]{.underline} [even
among states relatively confident of their basic territorial
security]{.underline} for the reasons identified in social identity
theory and theories of status competition. [Broad patterns of evidence
are consistent with this]{.underline} expectation, suggesting that
[unipolarity shapes strategies of identity]{.underline} maintenance [in
ways that **dampen status conflict**.]{.underline} The implication is
that [unipolarity helps explain low levels of military competition and
conflict]{.underline} among major powers [after 1991]{.underline} and
that [a return to **bipolarity or multipolarity** would increase the
likelihood of such **conflict**]{.underline}. This has been a
preliminary exercise. The evidence for the hypotheses explored here is
hardly conclusive, but it is sufficiently suggestive to warrant further
refinement and testing, all the more so given the importance of the
question at stake. If status matters in the way the theory discussed
here suggests, then the widespread view that the rise of a peer
competitor and the shift back to a bipolar or multipolar structure
present readily surmountable policy challenges is suspect. Most scholars
agree with Jacek Kugler and Douglas Lemke's argument: "\[S\]hould a
satisfied state undergo a power transition and catch up with dominant
power, there is little or no expectation of war." 81 [Given that today's
rising powers have every material reason to like the status quo, many
observers are optimistic that the rise of peer competitors can be
readily managed by fashioning an order that accommodates their material
interests.]{.underline} Yet it is far harder to manage competition for
status than for most material things. [While diplomatic efforts to
manage status competition seem easy]{.underline} under unipolarity,
theory and [evidence]{.underline} [suggest that it could present much
greater challenges as the system moves back to bipolarity or
multipolarity.]{.underline} When status is seen as a positional good,
efforts to craft negotiated bargains about status contests face long
odds. And this positionality problem is particularly acute concerning
the very issue unipolarity solves: primacy. [The route back to
bipolarity or multipolarity is thus **fraught with
danger.**]{.underline} [With two or more plausible claimants to primacy,
positional competition and the potential for **major power war** could
once again form the backdrop of world politics]{.underline}.

## Conflict Deaths/Pinker

### 2AC/1NC -- Battle Deaths Decreasing

#### Battle deaths are maintaining a downward trend -- improvements in medicine and NGOs

**Fazal, PhD, 14**

(Tanisha, Ph.D.: Political Science, Stanford University, Stanford, CA,
2001. "Dead Wrong?: Battle Deaths, Military Medicine, and Exaggerated
Reports of War\'s Demise." 2014.
http://muse.jhu.edu/article/553214.)//bc

As a first cut in bringing a broader set of casualty data into the
discussion of the changing incidence of war, I provide wounded-to-killed
ratios for four of the most frequent belligerents from 1775 to 2008 (see
figure 3). Where possible, data on civil wars in which these countries
were involved are included as well. All four graphs in this figure
demonstrate a significant upward trajectory in wounded-to-killed ratios.
France sees a dramatic rise after World War II, while Israel and Russia
demonstrate steadier inclines; the U.S. ratio jumps during Operations
Enduring Freedom and Iraqi Freedom, in particular. In figure 4, I
assemble these and other data to graph wounded-to-killed ratios over the
past two centuries using a smoothed spline function to plot a curve
through the data.84 [The figure demonstrates an upward trend in
wounded-to-killed ratios, **especially beginning in the latter half of
the twentieth century.**]{.underline} Note that these data include
interstate wars (such as the world wars), extrasystemic wars (such as
the American War of Independence), and civil wars (such as Russia's wars
with Chechnya in the 1990s and Indonesia's civil wars with leftists).
Although an ideal next step would be to regress some function of time as
well as the wounded-to-killed ratio on battle deaths, missing data limit
my confidence in taking this path. [The PRIO battle death data aggregate
all battle deaths for all conflicts in a given year; the
wounded-to-killed dataset contains a much sparser grouping of
data]{.underline}. Running regressions on battle deaths would therefore
require imputing values for a significant amount of missing data. In the
online appendix, I take this approach and find that, [although the
year]{.underline} \[End Page 115\] [in which a conflict occurs tends to
be a significant predictor]{.underline} (and depressor) [of battle
deaths in bivariate regressions,]{.underline} **[the coefficient on
"year" tends to be both positive and statistically
insignificant]{.underline}** w[hen interpolated wounded-to-killed ratios
are included]{.underline} in the analysis. By contrast, [the coefficient
on the wounded-to-killed ratio is almost always negative]{.underline},
although significant in only some models.85 As wounded-to-killed ratios
improve, battle deaths decline. **[The data suggest that both battle
deaths and battle casualties are on the decline]{.underline}**.
Producing a revised version of figure 1's count of battle deaths based
on available data would be problematic, however, for two reasons. First,
doing so would require using predicted values of wounded-to-killed
ratios from figure 4 and then plugging these already imputed values into
the wounded-to-killed ratio formula (W/K = R, where W = wounded, K =
killed, and R = ratio) to generate an estimated number of wounded that
could then be added to the known number of killed. Not only would such a
line be empirically questionable, but it would look almost exactly like
figure 1, because it would be a function of those data. Second, it would
also look as though the number of battle deaths had not declined at all
while casualties were on the decline, because it would dramatically
increase the scale of the graph to account for the much greater number
of casualties than fatalities. In the absence of more comprehensive data
on the wounded, I take a simpler approach. [Rather than trying to impute
values for all of the years under consideration]{.underline}, **[I look
only at the endpoints in figure 1 to compare the percentage change in
battle deaths with (imputed) battle casualties]{.underline}**. As figure
5 shows, [from 1946 to 2008,]{.underline} **[the number of battle deaths
declined by 50 percent]{.underline}**. By contrast, [estimated battle
casualties during the same period declined by less than 20
percent]{.underline}. Thus, the data suggest that **[battle deaths
declined more than twice as quickly than battle casualties during this
time frame]{.underline}**. If this is true, then it may be premature to
declare victory on war. The findings represented in figure 5 and the
preceding analysis raise two additional points. First, more data on the
battle wounded are needed before making a true comparison between the
decline in battle deaths and the incidence of battle casualties. Second,
the available data suggest that, [even accounting for the battle wounded
and notwithstanding improvements in medical care in
conflict]{.underline} [zones]{.underline}, [the number of casualties of
war still fell]{.underline}. **[This finding tempers]{.underline}**, but
does not negate, **[the empirical claim made by Goldstein and Pinker
about the decline of war more generally]{.underline}**. Indeed, in many
ways it is consistent with their arguments. [Improvements in medical
care in conflict zones are partially]{.underline} \[End Page 116\] a
[result of the humanitarian revolution celebrated]{.underline} [by the
declinist theory of war.]{.underline} Nongovernmental organizations
([NGOs]{.underline}) such as the ICRC and Mdicins sans Frontires (MSF)
[are now able to access conflict zones]{.underline}, and **[state
militaries make tremendous investments in the well-being of their
troops, both on and off the battlefield.]{.underline}**

#### Battle deaths are on a downward trend -- fewer interstate and civil wars post-Korean War

**Lacina, PhD, et al. 6**

(Bethany, PhD, Stanford, 2011, et al. "The Declining Risk of Death in

To improve on the fatalities data for the COW conflicts we use estimates

World Wars I and II]{.underline}. The trend in battle deaths worldwide
is also lumpy, revealing the influence of a few major conflicts within

#### Conflict deaths have been steadily decreasing since WWII

**Lacina, PhD, and Gleditsch, PhD, 5**

(Bethany and Nils Petter. "Monitoring Trends in Global Combat: A New
Dataset of Battle Deaths." European Journal of Population / Revue
Europenne De Dmographie, vol. 21, no. 2/3, 2005, pp. 145--166. JSTOR,
[www.jstor.org/stable/20164300](http://www.jstor.org/stable/20164300).
\*Charts have been omitted.) //bc

There has been a great deal of alarmist writing about the bloody
post-Cold War era, often painted in the media as a world of
unprecedented internecine conflict. Snow (1996, pp. 1-2, 105-113) holds,
for instance, that armed conflicts of the 1990s, which he calls
\"uncivil wars,\" were less principled, less focused on political goals,
and therefore bloodier than many in the past. Sarkees et al. (2003, p.
65) strike a similar note of pessimism, arguing that the risk of
suffering death in battle has trended neither up nor down since the
Napoleonic wars. Such remarks are surprising given that [organisations
that monitor the global incidence of armed conflict have found that the
late 1990s and first years of the twenty-first century
enjoyed]{.underline} **[a downward trend in warfare]{.underline}**
[between and within states]{.underline} (Gleditsch et al., 2002; State
Failure Task Force, 2003). What do global trends in battle deaths tell
us about the amount of armed combat taking place in the world today?
Figure 2 provides our estimate of the global trend in battle deaths from
1946 to 2002 in state-based armed conflicts, while Table 1 notes the
five conflicts that inflicted the largest numbers of battle deaths
during that era. Figure 2 reveals that [the long-term trend in battle
deaths has been sharply downward. The late 1940s and early
1950s]{.underline} [were very grim]{.underline}; [the combined impact of
the Chinese Civil War and the Korean War were augmented by the French
Indochina War]{.underline} (estimated to have killed 365,000 in battle)
[and the Greek Civil War]{.underline} (154,000 battle deaths[). The
Vietnam War forms the next hump in the dataset,]{.underline} [and the
combined impacts of the Iran-Iraq]{.underline} [war]{.underline}
[and]{.underline} the [Soviet intervention in Afghanistan constitute the
third peak]{.underline}. In the mid-1990s, the world seemed to enter
another trough in the number of battle deaths, until the war in the
Democratic Republic of Congo (DRC), with an estimated 145,000 battle
deaths in 1998-2001, and the interstate war between Ethiopia and Eritrea
that killed 50,000 from 1998 to 2000. **[Each peak is significantly
lower than the previous one]{.underline}** [and the trough in 2002 is
the lowest for the entire period]{.underline}.7 Thus, the good news
about battle deaths since World War II is that [although there have been
multiple major international security crises **their military scale has
progressively diminished**]{.underline}. [This may reflect]{.underline}
**[increasingly pacific behavior among the great powers]{.underline}**,
[who possess the resources]{.underline} and the military technology
(such as aerial power and heavy artillery) [to inflict large numbers of
battle deaths]{.underline} in the wars they start, join, or provide with
support. Each of the five largest conflicts identified was a war of this
type. [The Korean War and the Vietnam]{.underline} [War]{.underline}
[were]{.underline} massive [Cold War confrontations, and the Chinese

military assistance.]{.underline} [The wars between Iran and Iraq and
the Soviet invasion of Afghanistan were]{.underline} also
[driven]{.underline} in part [by the logic of Cold War]{.underline}
politics and the parties were armed by the US and USSR. By contrast,
[declining tension between]{.underline} the [superpowers]{.underline}
**[hastened the de-escalation of the Soviet war in

the late 1980s]{.underline}** (Sliwinski, 1989, pp. 40-41). And while
[the]{.underline} recent [war]{.underline} [in the DRC involved regional

1 constitute more than half of the estimated toll of global battle
deaths in the period 1946-2002]{.underline}**, accounting for about 5.76
million battle deaths out of a total of about 10 million[. The five wars

the rest of the story of global warfare]{.underline}. Figure 3 shows the

excluded. What seemed like a down ward trend in battle violence vanishes
and the world seems to fluctuate between high and low war intensity
years (although the final year is the lowest for the entire period).
Thus, the bad news about global battle deaths is that, although the
now-terminated Cold War played a devastating role in driving major
battle death events, there is a persistence of smaller scale, more
diverse conflicts, the trend in which is less obvious. Even so, [the
data by no means support the sombre picture]{.underline} painted by
Sarkees et al. (2003) [of a world of]{.underline} basically [constant
total rates of combat.]{.underline} Table 2 shows that the mean and
median numbers of battle deaths in a year of conflict after the Cold War
era were significantly lower than during the Cold War. Nor do we find
support for Snow\'s hypothesis of increasingly bloody civil conflicts,
at least when measured according to the number of battle deaths; [the
mean and median number]{.underline} [of combat fatalities in a
year]{.underline} of internal or internationalised internal conflict
**[have fallen dramatically since the end of the Cold
War.]{.underline}**

#### OMG wrong -- PRIO data is misrepresented -- war deaths have been consistently decreasing

\*OMG = Ziad Obermeyer, Christopher J. L. Murray, and Emmanuela Gakidou

**Spagat, PhD, et al. 9**

(Michael, et al. Professor of Economics at Royal Holloway College,

[OMG]{.underline} join Sarkees, Wayman, and Singer (2003) in
