work at the genetic level to spread the genes that made individuals more
cooperative within successful groups. It also requires]{.underline}, as
Haidt acknowledges, [a genetic basis for such feelings of sanctity and
loyalty]{.underline} -- the values that are supposed to enhance group
effectiveness. Moreover, [individuals who lack these qualities within
successful groups must be less likely to survive and reproduce than
those who possess them]{.underline}. **[These are very dubious
biological and social assumptions]{.underline}**. **[There is no easy
way to test for the selection of complex skills]{.underline}**, as we
cannot control for the effects of socialization. [Ashkenazi
Jews]{.underline}, for example, [are]{.underline} greatly
[overrepresented among the world's most successful and prominent
violinists,]{.underline} cellists and pianists. **[Does this reflect a
genetic advantage]{.underline}**, or have Jewish families and culture
encouraged an interest in music and nurtured whatever talent appeared?
**[There is never a one-to-one relationship between a gene and a
behavior]{.underline}**. Most [traits are not the result of single
genes]{.underline}, **[but combinations of them and complex patterns of
their activation]{.underline}**. [This is even more likely to be true of
any social orientations that are found to be hereditary.]{.underline} If
so, beneficial social traits may require environmental triggers, or may
appear, at least statistically, as part of a suite of traits, only some
of which may be beneficial. [The evolutionary picture far more complex
and less linear than Haidt acknowledges]{.underline}. It [may be
--]{.underline} and my reading of history and sociology suggests it is
the case -- [that groups and societies benefit from a diverse set of
skills and orientations]{.underline}, not the narrow palette that Haidt,
or anyone else, emphasizes. Haidt insists that whatever traits make a
group more efficient at procuring food make it more fit and likely to
survive. As noted, he attributes this advantage to a single social
variable: group cohesion. It is conventional wisdom that [sedentary
agriculturalists triumphed over nomads because they had access to more
calories.]{.underline} I am at a loss to attribute this development to
group cohesion, which was presumably an equally valuable asset in both
kinds of societies. History is more complex than Haidt acknowledges.
**[Haidt's invocation of natural selection within societies is as
questionable as his application of it across them]{.underline}**. He
maintains that in the ancient world the brave and loyal were more likely
to make it home from war than loners, cowards and those who avoided
military service. He further insists that women were more likely to
choose the former for mates. [No evidence is offered for these
assertions, and it is easy to construct counter narratives.]{.underline}
**[Haidt's approach to social selection is embarrassingly simplistic in
conception]{.underline}**, at odds with current understandings of
genetics and historically and sociologically uninformed. Most
importantly, **[it rests on a set of arbitrary, self-serving assumptions
that he uses to pass off snake oil as science.]{.underline}**

### Evolution =/= IR

#### *[Unique networks]{.underline}* mean natural selection theories can't be applied to international relations 

**Lebow, PhD, 13**

(Richard Ned Lebow is a fellow of the British Academy is an American
political scientist best known for his work in international relations,
political psychology, classics and philosophy of science. He is
Professor of International Political Theory at the Department of War
Studies, King\'s College London, Bye-Fellow of Pembroke College,
University of Cambridge, and James O. Freedman Presidential Professor
Emeritus at Dartmouth College*, you can't keep a bad idea down:
Evolutionary biology and international relations*, 8-14, Palgrave
Macmillan UK // NC)

Is natural selection an appropriate mechanism in politics? Kenneth Waltz
thinks so and makes it central to his theory of international politics.
He is clear that the international system selects for certain kinds of
behavior: that which copes most effectively with anarchy and the threats
to survival associated with it. Over time, units become more alike, and
this allows him to ignore state-level variation and construct a theory
entirely at the system level. Waltz does not draw directly on evolution,
but on microeconomics, where the theory of the firm is explicitly
Darwinian in conception. [**Natural selection does not work in
international relations**.]{.underline} If [a giraffe with a long neck
can reach more leaves]{.underline}, it is more likely to survive and
reproduce. Over multiple generations, the statistical average of giraffe
neck lengths will increase -- but not beyond the point where it is no
longer functional. [Unlike necks, **political skill is not
inherited.**]{.underline} [Skillful leaders do not produce more
offspring than their unsuccessful counterparts]{.underline}, nor are
they followed in office by equally qualified successors. Frederick the
Great transformed Prussia from a backwater march into a great power. His
descendants recklessly challenged Napoleonic France and in 1806 lost
most of the territory he had gained. Hardenberg, Gneisenau and
Scharnhorst reorganized the Prussian state and army, regaining their
country's status as a great power. Bismarck built on this foundation,
unified Germany and made it the dominant power in Europe. Wilhelm II and
his advisors gambled recklessly and needlessly on victory in a European
war, which led to Germany's defeat, partial dismemberment, costly
reparations and restrictions on rearmament, and the size of its armed
forces. Gustav Stresemann made gradual but notable progress in
reintegrating Germany into the European political community. Hitler led
Germany into an even more destructive war that left the country smaller
in size and divided into two ideologically opposed, quasi-independent,
rump states. Over the course of the next half-century, the leaders of
the German Federal Republic pursued cautious policies that gained
independence, respect and trust for their country, and ultimately
reunification. Germany's seesawing is more typical of international
relations than the kind of development associated with evolution.
[Natural selection is inappropriate to the study of international
relations for a second important reason]{.underline}: [it **does not
cull the herd of actors who are weak, old or otherwise unable to
compete**]{.underline}. The total number of states in the world shrank
in the nineteenth century because of Napoleon's consolidation of the
Holy Roman Empire, German and Italian unification, and colonial
expansion. In the twentieth century, it has increased from 57 in 1900 to
193, not counting Taiwan.3 Even in Europe, it has grown from 32 in 1945
to 43 today. [As some astute observers of the contemporary scene have
noted, the **international system is ordered in such a way to keep
failed states alive**, **with consequences not always beneficial for
their inhabitants**]{.underline} (Rotberg, 2004).

## Status Competition

### Status Competition Wrong

#### 'Status competition' is flawed --- their authors are [totalizing]{.underline} 

**Volgy and Gordell, 19** (Thomas J. Volgy: Professor of Political
Science. Kelly Marie Gordell: doctorate candidate at the school of
Government and Public Policy at the University of Arizona. \"Rising
powers, status competition, and global governance: a closer look at
three contested concepts for analyzing status dynamics in international
politics\", Contemporary Politics, June 3, 2019, pg. 9))SK

However, [we raise five warnings about how scholars address status
seeking and status competition]{.underline}. First, we suggest that [it
is dangerous to conflate competition and conflict between states as
driven **exclusively**]{.underline} or primarily **[by status
considerations]{.underline}**. Policy makers' [desires to
maintain]{.underline} or enhance their [status may be]{.underline} a
'[fundamental]{.underline} state motivation' (Duque, 2018, p. 1[) but
status concerns]{.underline} likely [constitute a **variable condition**
rather than a constant state of affairs]{.underline} for most states. If
so, then [we need to specify conditions under which policy makers are
motivated to engage]{.underline} in international politics [as a
function of their need for]{.underline} greater [status]{.underline},
[or from a fear of being unable to maintain]{.underline} the
[status]{.underline} they have.8 [It is a distorting academic exercise
to assume]{.underline} that almost [all]{.underline} foreign policy
[activity is motivated by status considerations, any more than solely by
security concerns or domestic political advantages]{.underline}.
[Sometimes the desire to acquire a naval carrier is based on **perceived
security needs rather than**]{.underline} the need [**to acquire a
'status toy**.']{.underline} Second, [we do not believe that the pursuit
of greater status]{.underline}, or the need to maintain extant status,
[should automatically equate to status competition and conflict between
states]{.underline}. In fact, the [conditions and
constraints]{.underline} involved for states potentially engaging in
status-seeking policies [are]{.underline} [complex]{.underline} (as
suggested in Figure 9), [and do not automatically lead to extensive
competition]{.underline} or conflict. As we had noted earlier,
status-seeking strategies may depend in substantial part on where states
are positioned (e.g. Roren & Beaumont, 2018), and the extent of their
dissatisfaction with their positioning. [States on a]{.underline} strong
[upward]{.underline} status [trajectory (e.g. **China**) are likely
to]{.underline} behave differently, and likely [to take **fewer
risks**]{.underline} than states in danger of losing their status, as
their status is declining in the group hierarchy, or if they may be in
danger altogether of falling out of the status club (Russia).

### AT: Status Symbols

#### IR status competition *[presupposes]{.underline}* hegemony is contingent on status markers, rather *[temporal comparisons]{.underline}* are necessary to understand the actions of great powers 

**Freedman, PhD,** 8-26-20**15**

(Joshua Freedman is a Visiting Assistant Professor of Politics at
Oberlin College (starting fall 2019), where he teaches courses on global
politics, international law, international security, and the
Israeli-Palestinian Conflict. Freedman earned a Ph.D. (June 2019) from
the Department of Political Science at Northwestern University, Status
insecurity and temporality in world politics, 798,
[https://journals.sagepub.com/doi/pdf/10.1177/1354066115603781
//](https://journals.sagepub.com/doi/pdf/10.1177/1354066115603781%20//)
NC)

[The act of recognition in IR is too often defined as a transaction
between established and aspiring actors, which presupposes that actors
always know what status recognition entails but simply disagree on
whether the state in question deserves recognition]{.underline}.
[Successful transactions, however, **depend on both the will and the
capacity of negotiating actors**]{.underline}. [It is not sufficient for
the US to merely]{.underline} be willing [to recognize China as a great
power]{.underline} if both states hold mutually inconsistent views on
the signals that are believed to constitute this act[. Understanding how
these inconsistencies arise, and how they can be mitigated]{.underline},
[should be of interest to IR]{.underline} scholars studying status, and
[yet these issues are rarely explored precisely because the desire for
status recognition is always assumed to originate within a social
comparison logic]{.underline}. [China's status concerns]{.underline}, it
is argued, [challenge this logic by elevating Taiwan as a critical
marker of the country's great power status]{.underline}. **[We cannot
understand this move without first understanding the impact of negative
temporal comparisons on status concerns]{.underline}**. Moreover, just
as temporal comparisons are a common form of self-evaluation for
individuals, a temporal theory of status in world politics is not unique
to China. Rather, the universe of cases that benefit from this approach
encompass any state or political community that has suffered a major
instance of status loss in its history. [Much can still be learned by
applying this temporal approach to these cases. Indeed, part of the task
going forward should be to **ferret out other country
cases**]{.underline}, whether they be contemporary or historical, where
temporal comparisons have had, or are having, significant implications
for status recognition. In addition to these positive cases, the
possibility of negative cases30 presents its own interesting challenges.
These would be cases where unfavorable temporal comparisons exist in the
context of status loss but are not frequently invoked and produce no
observable consequences. Just as we should be interested in why states
may privilege social over temporal comparisons (or vice versa) when
evaluating status, we should be equally interested in how states can
seemingly overcome and ignore unfavorable comparisons in one realm for
favorable comparisons in the other. The temporal theory of status in
world politics outlined in this article complicates the policy
recommendations put forward by the status incentives literature
precisely because the past, as a status metric, is elusive. If Beijing's
concept of status recognition is temporally contingent, the bar can
always move higher. After Taiwan, Outer Mongolia?31 The risk, then, for
the status incentives literature is that established great powers may
recognize rising powers, using the signals and practices that they see
fit, and find that recipient actors still feel unrecognized and so
behave as actors struggling for recognition.

### AT: Western Studies

#### Western studies are limited to *[western culture]{.underline}* specifically -- it can't accurately explain eastern competitiveness 

**Garcia et al. PhD, 13**

(Stephen M., Avishalom Tor, Tryrone M. Schif, Notre Dame Law School,
Stephen M. Garcia got his Ph.D. at Princeton University, Avishalom Tor
is a Professor of Law and Director of the Research Program on Law and
Market Behavior, Global Professor of Law, University of Haifa Faculty of
Law, The Psychology of Competition: A Social Comparison Perspective, 4,
11-2013
https://scholarship.law.nd.edu/cgi/viewcontent.cgi?referer=https://www.google.com/&httpsredir=1&article=1943&context=law_faculty_scholarship//
NC)

[The research we draw on to build **the present framework principally
used participants from Western**,]{.underline} more individualistic
**[cultures, raising the possibility that this framework is culture
specific and may not apply to Eastern cultures]{.underline}** that
construe the self as part of a larger network (Markus & Kitayama, 1991).
We think it plausible, however, that [some dynamics underlying
competitiveness may be common across cultures]{.underline} (Tang, 1999;
Toda, Shinotsuka, McClintock, & Stech, 1978), though both the base rates
of social comparison and competitiveness and the impact of certain
variables we identify may be culture dependent (A. P. Buunk, Carmona,
Peir, Dijkstra, & Dijkstra, 2011). [For example, Chinese precollege
students use competitive strategies, whereas American precollege
students use cooperative strategies in the realm of test
taking]{.underline} (Tang, 1999). Yet this difference is not cultural
per se, rather resulting from different institutional structures of
testing: college entrance exams are a onetime event in 10 Garcia et al.
China but repeatable in the United States. Accordingly, a study
comparing the strategies used by students in the two cultures found that
the psychological predictors of "competitive types" and "cooperative
types" did not vary by culture or origin (Tang, 1999). [The same may
hold for the impact of individual and situational factors, which can
remain influential despite significant differences in their concrete
cross-cultural manifestations]{.underline}. To illustrate, a common
Japanese maxim states that "the nail that sticks out gets hammered,"
suggesting it may be more important for Japanese relative to American
populations to avoid falling behind the competition than to break ahead
of the competition. Indeed, findings indicate that the Japanese may be
slightly more motivated than Americans by being ranked near the bottom,
whereas Americans may be slightly more motivated near the top rank
(Heine et al., 2001). In this case, therefore, the situational factor of
proximity to a standard matters but manifests differently across
cultures.

### AT: Competition Inevitable

#### **Competitiveness increases when the number of competitors decreases** 

**Garcia et al. PhD, 13**

(Stephen M., Avishalom Tor, Tryrone M. Schif, Notre Dame Law School,
Stephen M. Garcia got his Ph.D. at Princeton University, Avishalom Tor
is a Professor of Law and Director of the Research Program on Law and
Market Behavior, Global Professor of Law, University of Haifa Faculty of
LawThe Psychology of Competition: A Social Comparison Perspective, 4,
11-2013
https://scholarship.law.nd.edu/cgi/viewcontent.cgi?referer=https://www.google.com/&httpsredir=1&article=1943&context=law_faculty_scholarship//
NC)

One ubiquitous feature of competitions is the number of competitors. Yet
how exactly does our competitiveness change as the number of competitors
changes? Are we more motivated to compete on exams, in a sales office,
or on the treadmill at the gym as the number of competitors decreases?
[Research has showed the number of competitors (N) to be an important
situational factor of social comparison. Previously]{.underline}, a
number of [**studies found that the intensity of competitive behavior
increases as N decreases,** but these studies generally confounded
expected payoff with the number of competitors]{.underline}: as N
decreased, expected value increased. For example, auction bidders have a
greater tendency to exceed their bidding limits when vying against a few
versus many bidders for one object (Ku et al., 2005). [A study of the
game show Weakest Link]{.underline} (Pillutla & Ronson, 2005) [found
that contestants behaved more competitively toward other contestants as
the number of players decreased in subsequent rounds]{.underline}. And
[research on tournaments]{.underline} (Ehrenberg & Bognanno, 1990) has
[found the same pattern]{.underline} (see also Boudreau, Lacetera, &
Lakhani, 2011; Casas-Arce & Martnez-Jerez, 2009). **[However, in all
these settings, the diminished N changes the payoff structure of the
competition]{.underline}** (Vandegrift & Holaday, 2012) and leaves open
the possibility that the increased competitiveness observed is caused by
the higher expected payoffs with decreased Ns---that is, by the
incentive structure of the competition. Research on the N effect (Garcia
& Tor, 2009; Tor & Garcia, 2010), [however, found that comparison
concerns intensify, and **competitiveness increases as the number of
competitors decreases,**]{.underline} **[even when controlling for
overall expected payoffs]{.underline}**. For instance, in an analysis of
SAT data at the state level for all 50 U.S. states, researchers tested
the prediction that the lower the average number of test takers per
venue in a state, the higher that state's average SAT score. By dividing
the total number of test takers per state by the total number of
test-taking opportunities in that state, a "test-taking density"
variable was created. As predicted, when controlling for various
demographic factors, a significant inverse relationship between
test-taking density and state-level average SAT scores emerged (Garcia &
Tor, 2009). [To examine the N effect in a controlled setting, one study
recruited undergraduates to complete a short, easy quiz in an aggregated
pool of 10 or 100 competitors]{.underline}, with a monetary incentive
for speed without compromising accuracy (Garcia & Tor, 2009). [As
predicted, participants finished]{.underline} the quiz significantly
[faster in the 10-competitors than in the 100-competitors
condition]{.underline}, without a significant difference in accuracy.
One reason for the effect of N on the competitiveness is that comparison
concerns Psychology of Competition 7 decrease with N. Indeed[, people
harbor fewer comparison concerns and lose interest in comparison
information as N increases]{.underline} (Garcia & Tor, 2009; Tor &
Garcia, 2010). [Therefore,]{.underline} notwithstanding the potential
contribution of additional mechanisms (see Mukherjee & Hogarth, 2010;
Tor & Garcia, 2010), [comparison concerns significantly drive the N
effect.]{.underline} Other research findings also suggest that
comparison concerns decrease with N. For example, personalized
comparisons to a specific individual lead to greater increases in the
motivation to compete than do comparisons to people more generally
(Buckingham & Alicke, 2002; W. M. P. Klein, 2003; Locke, 2007). W. M. P.
Klein (2003) found that people behaved more competitively and were less
willing to provide helpful hints on a feedback task when their
performance was compared with a "single other" versus the "average
other." Dubbed the local dominance effect (Zell & Alicke, 2010), this
work likewise describes how local social comparison information is
likely to carry more weight than global comparison information and may
contribute to the stronger social comparison concerns one finds in
smaller-N settings.

### 2NC/2AC -- Taiwan R/C

#### Their interpretation of hegemony relies on the idea that China wants *[status markers.]{.underline}* however, *[Temporal comparison theory]{.underline}* suggests Taiwan is the root cause of China's role as a *[status seeker]{.underline}* 

**Freedman, PhD,** 8-26-20**15**

(Joshua Freedman is a Visiting Assistant Professor of Politics at
Oberlin College (starting fall 2019), where he teaches courses on global
politics, international law, international security, and the
Israeli-Palestinian Conflict. Freedman earned a Ph.D. (June 2019) from
the Department of Political Science at Northwestern University, Status
insecurity and temporality in world politics, 798,
[https://journals.sagepub.com/doi/pdf/10.1177/1354066115603781
//](https://journals.sagepub.com/doi/pdf/10.1177/1354066115603781%20//)
NC)

[China spends more in absolute dollars on its military than any other
country in the world after the US]{.underline}. Its prolific military
spending, which increased by 170%1 between 2004 and [has been sustained
by an incredible period of economic growth that saw China surpass Japan
as the world's second-largest economy in 2010]{.underline}. While still
far behind the US, China's US\$8.2 trillion gross domestic product
(GDP)2 is well ahead of its other United Nations Security Council (UNSC)
peers: Britain, France, and Russia. In the relative and material terms
of economic strength and military spending,3 [China is the second most
powerful state in the world,4 and yet it has been]{.underline} described
by Larson and Shevchenko (2010) **[as a "status seeker]{.underline}**,"
by Suzuki (2008: 51) as **[a "frustrated great power,"]{.underline}** by
Scobell (2012: 719) as a society **[in dire need of "international
respect]{.underline}**," and by Deng (2008: 8) as "the most
status-conscious country in the world." What accounts for this
disconnect, and what does it mean for thinking about states and
international relations that China has all of this material power and
yet appears to be engaged in a profound struggle for status and great
power recognition? [This article argues that China's status insecurity
results primarily from transaction and signaling problems endemic to the
practice of status recognition in great power politics. As the
sociology, economics, and political science literatures make clear, an
actor's status depends on two conditions: that they can properly signal
their desire for]{.underline} (or right to) [status; and that the rest
of society can properly signal their recognition]{.underline} of this
claim.5 [Signaling problems]{.underline} may arise in both of these
conditions and [are exacerbated]{.underline}, I argue, [by functional
and ontological differences in the way]{.underline} in which
individuals, groups, and, by transposition[, states engage in
self-evaluation]{.underline}. If [for recognition]{.underline} between
two willing parties [to succeed, both actors need to agree on the
signals]{.underline} [that constitute recognition,]{.underline} then it
is imperative that what the aspiring actor holds to be necessary for it
to "arrive" as a great power in the international system is shared by
those granting recognition. Much of the status literature in
International Relations ([IR]{.underline}) [has overlooked this dilemma
by assuming that all states pursue status for similar
reasons]{.underline} by virtue of engaging in similar methods of
self-evaluation. A significant portion of [this literature draws heavily
from]{.underline} social comparison theory [(SCT),]{.underline} and its
observations that individuals judge their worth, and accordingly derive
self-esteem, through comparisons with others (Larson et al., 2014: 17).
[According to this]{.underline} transposed argument[, states become
status seekers because,]{.underline} like individuals and groups,
**[they have an innate desire for favorable social status comparisons
relative to their peers]{.underline}** (Wohlforth, 2009: 35). Thus, the
literature on status in world politics is often framed in the language
of accommodation, and adjustment (Larson et al., 2014: 4[), **which
presupposes that status insecurities develop from lateral social
comparisons and can be resolved through lateral
improvements.**]{.underline} [Yet,]{.underline} while
[individuals]{.underline} engage in self-evaluation and derive
self-esteem through social comparisons with their peers, they also
engage in selfevaluation and derive self-esteem [through temporal
comparisons with their past]{.underline}. Psychology has addressed this
distinction for some time, noting that individuals repeatedly rely on
both social and temporal sources of information when engaging in
selfevaluation. [Applied to IR,]{.underline} temporal comparison theory
([TCT) suggests that status insecurities in the international system may
not be the exclusive province of unfavorable social comparisons with
peers]{.underline}, [but,]{.underline} rather, [may actually be the
outcome of actors succumbing to the stresses and failures of their own
history]{.underline}. The implications of applying this temporal
approach, I argue, are twofold. First, I suggest [that if actors develop
status insecurities because]{.underline} of unfavorable
[temporal]{.underline} rather than social [comparisons]{.underline},
then [they]{.underline} Freedman 799 [will]{.underline} be more likely
to [associate status recognition with the **correction of**]{.underline}
what they perceive to be **[a historic wrong]{.underline}** than they
are to associate it with the club goods and symbols commonly linked with
great power status. **[In this sense, China's perception of its own
status recognition should hinge]{.underline}** as much
**[on]{.underline}** the rights and obligations afforded to it by the
other great powers in the international system as it should on its
ability to match an idealized image of itself from its past. Here**[,
the unresolved]{.underline}** and unfavorable **[status of
Taiwan]{.underline}** becomes as important a marker of China's perpetual
lack of status as unfavorable voting rights in the world's major
institutions. As such, [the second implication of]{.underline} [applying
a temporal approach]{.underline} [to the study of status in IR is the
problem that it poses for the "status incentives]{.underline}"
literature, w[hich advances a logic]{.underline} of status recognition
[centered around international institutions and diplomatic protocol. If,
however, a country's status insecurities result from unfavorable
temporal comparisons]{.underline}, then, as the first implication
suggests, **[the ideas that they hold as being necessary for their own
arrival as a great power may not hinge on such commonly held status
markers.]{.underline}** In raising this critique, this article takes as
its starting point Pouliot's (2014: 194) contention that "practical
notions of status evolve over time and space" precisely because "\[i\]n
the absence of a symbolic hegemon ... various understandings of status
compete against one another, and markers often remain ambiguous and
disputed." Accordingly, in asking why China appears fixated on the issue
of great power status and recognition despite its objective economic and
military power, and despite the observations of many that it has already
been recognized as a great power,6 this article seeks to understand the
source of China's perception that it lacks status recognition, and
accordingly the source of its status insecurity. In making the claim
that China's status insecurity can be better understood by incorporating
TCT into the existing literature, the goal of this article is to
convince social comparison theorists in IR of the incompleteness of
their argument by presenting TCT's usefulness and applicability. The
goal of this article is not --- borrowing language from Price and
Tannenwald (1996: 152) --- "to defeat \[social comparison theorists\] in
some epic Lakatosian battle as if only one can exist and the other must
perish." Stuart Albert (1977: 489), who inaugurated the temporal
comparison research program in psychology, admitted as much when he
remarked that: \[j\]ust as it is not always true that individuals are
obsessed with self-evaluation and select social comparison processes as
the means for doing so, so it is equally wrong to believe that an
individual is completely engaged in explicit temporal comparison
processes to maintain an enduring self-identity over time.

### 2NC/2AC -- Econ R/C

#### Economic inequality is the root cause of status competition 

**Haslam, 17** - Professor of Psychology, University of Melbourne (Nick,
\"Distress, status wars and immoral behaviour: the psychological impacts
of inequality\", Conversation, 3-26-2017,
<http://theconversation.com/distress-status-wars-and-immoral-behaviour-the-psychological-impacts-of-inequality-75183))SK>

\*\*We do not endorse ableist language

It is well known that [economic inequality is rising]{.underline}. In
most industrialised nations the [distribution of wealth and income is
becoming increasingly concentrated]{.underline}. In the United States,
the top 10% of earners make more than [nine times as
much](http://inequality.org/income-inequality/) on average as the
remainder, and in Australia the ratio approaches five. It is also well
known that [rising economic inequality is associated with a range of
**economic and social ills**]{.underline}. More [unequal
societies]{.underline} tend to [have worse
[health](https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/25577953), more
[obesity](https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/16020644), more [violent
crime](http://journals.sagepub.com/doi/abs/10.1177/073401689301800203),
more [political
instability](http://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/0014292195000305),
and more [institutional
corruption](http://journals.sagepub.com/doi/abs/10.1177/000312240507000107)]{.underline}.
What is less well known is that [economic inequality also has
psychological effects]{.underline}. For example, there is evidence that
[people]{.underline} in more unequal societies tend to h[ave lower
levels of [life
satisfaction](http://www.gini-research.org/system/uploads/374/original/DP_38_-_Ferrer-i-Carbonell_Ramos.pdf)
and higher rates of
[depression](https://jhu.pure.elsevier.com/en/publications/income-inequality-and-depression-prevalence-across-the-united-sta-3).]{.underline}
[These]{.underline} and other psychological [effects]{.underline} on
individuals [may help to explain the larger scale social
effects]{.underline} of inequality. An important new [review
article](http://onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/10.1111/spc3.12304/abstract)
makes a strong case for the explanatory role of two phenomena in
particular. [Nicholas
[Buttrick]{.underline}](http://psychology.as.virginia.edu/people/profile/nrb8pv)
[and]{.underline} [Shigehiro
[Oishi]{.underline}](http://psychology.as.virginia.edu/people/profile/so5x)
[argue]{.underline} economic [inequality breeds mistrust and **status
competition**]{.underline}. These have downstream effects on health and
well-being in more unequal societies. Mistrust Buttrick and Oishi
demonstrate that [inequality is associated with a generalised **distrust
of other people**]{.underline}. People who see large economic
disparities tend to believe the economic system is unfair and others are
advancing themselves by questionable means. This lack of trust is
amplified by high levels of class-based segregation in unequal
societies, which reduces opportunities for people to interact outside
their socioeconomic bubbles. The [combination of distrust and
unfamiliarity contributes to a lack of social cohesion and a sense that
socioeconomic divides are **deep and inevitable**]{.underline}. Mistrust
undermines social connection and civility. [Studies]{.underline}
comparing US states [find that]{.underline} residents of [more unequal
states are less likely to participate in]{.underline} [social
[groups]{.underline}](https://dash.harvard.edu/bitstream/handle/1/4551796/alesina_participation.pdf?sequence=2),
a form of disengagement associated with poorer health outcomes according
to [Australian
research](http://blogs.lse.ac.uk/politicsandpolicy/the-social-cure-why-do-groups-make-us-healthier-and-how-can-we-capitalise-on-these-curing-properties/).
Wealthy residents of these states are also less likely to [contribute to
charity](http://www.pnas.org/content/112/52/15838.full). [People in more
unequal]{.underline} American [states even show differences in their
personality and willingness to engage in **immoral
behaviour**]{.underline}. [One
study](https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/21612851)found they score
relatively low on agreeableness -- a trait that includes tendencies to
be friendly and cooperative.
[Another](http://journals.sagepub.com/doi/abs/10.1177/0956797611435980)
revealed that people from these states were more likely to conduct
Google searches for academic cheating aids. [Diminished
trust]{.underline} in economically unequal societies [may have large
social effects.]{.underline} Low generalised trust partially accounts
for the relationship between [inequality and
mortality](https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC2951926/)
observed in a sample of 33 countries, which endures even when public
health expenditures are statistically controlled. It also partially
explains the link between [inequality and homicide
rates](https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/20525751) in those countries.
In short, a society where people do not trust one another is at risk of
ill health and violent crime. Such societies tend to have relatively
large economic disparities. According to Buttrick and Oishi, mistrust is
only one of the psychological processes that account for inequality's
adverse social effects. The other is status competition. [People in
highly unequal societies are more concerned with where they stand on the
**status hierarchy**]{.underline}. [Status anxiety may appear as fears
of losing one's economic standing or concerns about how others perceive
it]{.underline}.

### AT: ! -- Status Competition

#### there is no impact to status competition -- multiple checks prove

**Dal, Ph.D., 6/13/2019** (Emel Parlar, Associate Professor at Marmara
University's Department of International Relations, "Status competition
and rising powers in global governance: an introduction", Contemporary
Politics, ISSN: 1356-9775 (Print) 1469-3631, pp. 10-12, 6/13)micahw

Since [status is very closely linked to the identity management of
states and can be ascribed as a result of the overlapping of
self-perception and recognition]{.underline}, [states always remain in
need of achieving positive identity, role and status relative to their
peers or higher-status states in the international
hierarchy]{.underline}. Additionally, the current [power
shifts]{.underline} accelerated in the international system since the
mid-2000s, the emerging crisis in multilateralism as well as the rise of
informal institutions in global governance seem to have [made states
turn to status politics more ambitiously than they have done in the
past]{.underline}. Another characteristic of status-seeking policies of
rising powers is that they are mostly leader-driven. [Internal dynamics
of rising powers and their leaders' foreign policy choices also shape
the way and degree to which they engage in status competition in status
clubs]{.underline}. **[Here the question that needs to be asked is
whether status competition among rising powers leads to an increase in
their status recognition in the eyes of their peers and higher-status
states]{.underline}**. In status clubs like G20 for instance, status
competition among rising powers is generally less obvious mainly due to
the structure of the G20 which is composed of both rising, middle and
major powers. However, this does not point to a lack of competition
among rising powers. In contrast, [among the rising powers of G20
competition and cooperation coexist]{.underline} and **[in some cases
status competition may generate further cooperation as indicated by
Sino-Russian relations]{.underline}**. **[The competition--cooperation
coexistence also justifies the argument that status and its end result
-- status recognition -- are all social processes and
relational]{.underline}**. Given this, [it will not be false to argue
that status competition among rising powers may also contribute to the
reinforcing of their status recognition]{.underline}. Here, it must also
be remembered that status recognition is high among states that have
common values, shared interests and resources. This argument also brings
us to the following conclusion: **[if correctly used by states, status
competition among the states in the same peer group may serve as an
important tool to reinforce their diplomatic network]{.underline}**.
This means that status competition emanating from geopolitical rivalry
among peers may end up with cooperation. This also suggests that [status
competition among rising powers]{.underline} does not always lead to
aggressive behaviour. In contrast, it [may pave the way for the
reinforcement of cooperation among the lower-status states in the same
peer grouping]{.underline}. On the other hand, [rising
powers]{.underline} are certainly not mirror images of one another. They
have different motivations and capacity of influence in global
governance in general and in status clubs in particular. Similarly, they
pose different challenges for major powers. This also i[mpacts the way
major powers recognize their status internationally and positively
perceive their status-seeking strategies as a way to upgrade their
status vis--vis]{.underline} both [the major powers]{.underline} and
their peers. A close assessment of status competition among rising
powers shows that [this competition is peaceful rather than being
conflictual or aggressive]{.underline}. [For instance, rising powers aim
to upgrade their status in status clubs through economic, geopolitical,
ideational and normative means]{.underline}. This is not to say that
military power does not play any role in the current status rivalry
among rising peers. Of course, it can be used as a tool of coercion
backing their other status-seeking strategies. In short, **[status
competition among rising powers is not in the form of the classic
political-military competition]{.underline}**. It also does not look
like the great power competition which is much more complex today due to
the inexistence of roughly equivalent great powers as seen in the
rivalry of U.S and China and of U.S and Russia. Last but not least,
**[since status is relational, status competition goes beyond the
material attributes of states]{.underline}**. Ideational patterns are
equally important in order to assess rising powers' status-seeking
behaviours in different status clubs. [The current literature on status
politics does not focus too much on the ideational factors behind the
quest of rising powers for attaining higher status in formal and
informal international organizations]{.underline}. The existing
literature on status competition also neglects the role of ideas in
states' pursuit of 'rivalry' strategies against each other. Further
studies on status/status politics may well go beyond the use of
'material attributes' in the assessment of status-seeking strategies of
lower status states. [The measuring of states' status in the
international hierarchy and their sub-hierarchies also appears as a new
field of research on status/status politics]{.underline}.

## Conflict Deaths

### AT: Pinker

#### Pinker and Goldstein's data doesn't take into account failed homicides or unsuccessful attempts at territorial conquest

**Fazal, PhD, 14**

(Tanisha, Ph.D.: Political Science, Stanford University, Stanford, CA,
2001. "Dead Wrong?: Battle Deaths, Military Medicine, and Exaggerated
Reports of War\'s Demise." 2014.
http://muse.jhu.edu/article/553214.)//bc

Several sources of data appear to support the claim that war is on the
decline. Pinker draws on Lewis Richardson's argument that armed
conflicts tend to follow power law distributions in their incidence and
magnitude. This finding \[End Page 97\] suggests that large, cataclysmic
wars will be relatively rare.7 Related, Pinker refers frequently to John
Mueller's finding that great powers no longer fight each other.8 In
addition, using data from The Great Big Book of Horrible Things9---an
encyclopedia of atrocities---Pinker finds that the percentage of
territorial wars from 1651 to 2000 that resulted in the redistribution
of territory dropped precipitously in the early to mid-twentieth
century.10 In reaching their conclusions, both Goldstein and Pinker rely
heavily on the Peace Research Institute Oslo (PRIO) battle deaths
data.11 Pinker, for example, graphs the rate of battle deaths in
state-based conflicts, the number of state-based conflicts, and the
deadliness of interstate and civil wars. These graphs principally cover
the second half of the twentieth century and beyond.12 Figure 1
replicates a version of Pinker's figure 6-4, which illustrates a
downward trend in war fatalities. As Pinker notes, this trend is
particularly stark with respect to interstate wars.13 In making their
claims, [Goldstein]{.underline} [and]{.underline} especially
[Pinker]{.underline} [rely heavily on the number of battle fatalities as
a metric of war]{.underline}.14 Battle deaths are used widely by
international relations scholars because death is unambiguous. Yet,
**[what if the probability of dying from wounds sustained in war has
changed over time]{.underline}**?15 \[End Page 98\] To [make a claim
about the decline]{.underline} (or rise) [of X]{.underline} (insert your
most or least-favorite phenomenon here), [one must consider the
possibility of selection effects]{.underline}. For example, [if one were
to use the number of first-class letters delivered by national postal
services as a measure of social cohesion within a country]{.underline},
[it would at first seem sensible to conclude that the world's richest
states saw significant drops]{.underline} in social cohesion beginning
in the late 1990s. The [precipitous decline in postal delivery does
not,]{.underline} however, indicat[e a decline in the desire to
communicate as much as a shift to modern forms of communication such as
email and Facebook]{.underline}. I illustrate this point further using
two other examples from Pinker's book. First, [prior to his discussion
about the decline of war,]{.underline} [Pinker argues that interpersonal
violence has also declined]{.underline}. He presents some of his
strongest evidence on this point in his figure 3-4, which shows a
dramatic decline in homicide rates in Western Europe and nonstate
societies from 1300 to 2000.16 [Missing from]{.underline} this and
[Pinker's subsequent graphs]{.underline}, however, **[is a count of
attempted]{.underline}** \[End Page 99\] **[homicides]{.underline}**.
[The reason to include attempted homicides is that the likelihood of
surviving a knife]{.underline} [wound]{.underline} in London [was
dramatically lower in 1400 than the likelihood of surviving the same
wound today.]{.underline} Significant advances in medical care make such
care both more available and more effective today than in the past.
Here, [Pinker's decision not to define "violence"]{.underline} **[is
particularly problematic]{.underline}** in assessing his claim about its
putative decline.17 [If by "violence" Pinker means
fatalities]{.underline}, then [he is correct that violence is on the
decline]{.underline}. [A standard dictionary definition of
violence]{.underline}, [however]{.underline}, **[would include the
attempt or threat to inflict bodily harm]{.underline}**.18 Second,
[Pinker makes a similar misstep in his brief analysis of territorial
conquest]{.underline}. Drawing on data from Mark Zacher[, he illustrates
a dramatic drop in the percentage of territorial wars]{.underline}
resulting in the redistribution of territory beginning in the early to
mid-twentieth century.19 But again, [missing from Pinker's
analysis]{.underline} is **[whether attempts at territorial
redistribution also declined]{.underline}**. [Unsuccessful attempts to
redistribute territor]{.underline}y **[could be more violent than
successful ones;]{.underline}** [scholars therefore need to examine both
failed and successful efforts at territorial redistribution]{.underline}
to determine whether such efforts are becoming less frequent. I follow
Pinker and use Zacher's data to show that [even though the number of
territorial redistributions has declined]{.underline}, [the number of
territorial wars]{.underline}---that is, wars where at least one
belligerent aims to redistribute territory---**[has actually increased
over the course of the twentieth century]{.underline}** (see figure
2).20 Ignoring failed attempts at homicide, territorial redistribution,
or killing in war might possibly be unproblematic for, or even
supportive of, the thesis that war is on the decline. Data on attempted
homicides, for example, might reveal that kind-hearted passersby are
increasingly likely to intervene to save the life of a potential murder
victim. Similarly, some scholars have argued that a norm against
forcible territorial conquest accounts at least in part for the observed
decline of such conquest.21 But [in the realm of battle
deaths,]{.underline} [this argument]{.underline} \[End Page 100[\] is
more difficult to support]{.underline}. **[One would have to
convincingly show that today's soldiers are more likely to try to save a
severely wounded comrade]{.underline}** or enemy than **[they were in
the past]{.underline}** [because they have been influenced by modern
humanitarian norms]{.underline}. The conventional wisdom suggests,
however, **[that soldiers fight to protect one another,22 and there is
no evidence to show that this motive has changed.]{.underline}**

[Counting war fatalities]{.underline} as a means to measure the
frequency of war [is a]{.underline}n apparently sensible but,
ultimately, [flawed strategy given recent developments in technology and
warfare]{.underline}. Death is final and corpses are easier to count
than the wounded. **[That fewer people are dying in war, however, does
not mean that war is at an end.]{.underline}** Indeed, [it does not even
necessarily mean that war has become more humane.]{.underline} \[End
Page 122\]

#### Enlightenment focus for improvement *[obscures]{.underline}* the negative effects of globalization and exploitation that *[reproduce inequality]{.underline}*

**Goldin, PhD, 18**

(Ian Goldin is Professor of Globalization and Development and director
of the Oxford Martin Programme on Technological and Economic Change at
the University of Oxford, UK, and the author of Age of Discovery and The
Butterfly Defect, Nature International Journal of Science, "The
limitations of Steven Pinker's optimism," 2-16 // NC)

Steven Pinker's Enlightenment Now builds on his 2011 The Better Angels
of Our Nature (Viking) in offering another engaging, compelling set of
reasons to be cheerful. In this new combined survey, analysis and
manifesto, he convincingly demonstrates that when it comes to health and
life expectancy, poverty reduction and income, education, human rights,
peace and security, **[the global data provide solid grounds for
optimism.]{.underline}** **[But the book's premise lies in the past: the
Enlightenment]{.underline}**, that period in the eighteenth century
when, Pinker argues, reason, science, humanism and progress became the
centre of intellectual endeavour in Europe and North America. That
legacy, he asserts, is ripe for resurrection at a time of political
upheaval, the rise of demagoguery, climate scepticism and 'fake news'.
The Enlightenment undoubtedly saw major advances in constitutional
government. The separation of church and state in some nations allowed
new models of society to flourish. But using the era as a premise is
problematic. **[Many of the breakthroughs that Pinker attributes to the
Enlightenment actually pre-date it]{.underline}**. As Chris Kutarna and
I showed in Age of Discovery (Bloomsbury, 2017), [the Renaissance was a
period of even more dramatic progress]{.underline} in science and the
humanities, sparked by luminaries such as the astronomer Nicolaus
Copernicus and the humanist Erasmus (see P. Ball Nature 452, 816--818;
2008). [Before that were extraordinarily innovative epochs in
Asia]{.underline} and other regions, [such as China's Tang
dynasty]{.underline} (ad 618--907) [and the Islamic Golden
Age]{.underline} (750--1260). Another issue we highlighted is that
**[less salubrious aspects of change accompanied eras of rapid
progress]{.underline}**. During the Renaissance [voyages of
discovery,]{.underline} European sailors spread diseases that [killed
millions of indigenous Americans]{.underline}. Europe saw [the rise of
religious extremists]{.underline} --- such as the demagogue Girolamo
Savonarola, who ruled Florence in the 1490s --- along with intolerance
of diversity and science. [Enlightenment advances were tied to empire
building and the nascent Industrial Revolution, predicated]{.underline}
not just [on]{.underline} noble ideas and scientific curiosity, but also
on slavery, genocide, exploitation and cultural triumphalism. Like the
Renaissance, it ended in chaos and conflict, including the French
revolutionary wars of 1792--99. Both eras show that science and
evidence-based thinking do not necessarily triumph over irrationality
and ideology. Shared social norms and ethics are the framework that
allows reason to prevail. Moreover, progress in science and society is a
tapestry that cannot be unpicked through simple periodization. That
said, comparing historical eras can be instructive. Although history
does not repeat itself, it may rhyme; it can offer insights into
predicaments and solutions, as I have found helpful, for instance, in
conceptualizing the future of work. Many of the Enlightenment's
philosophical, social and scientific advances offer lessons for our
reason-starved times, as Pinker ably argues; but context is key.
Although it is framed as a historically informed template for a new age
of reason, [Enlightenment]{.underline} Now ultimately
[becomes]{.underline} something else: [an extended dismissal of the
arguments of despair that Pinker fears are defining politics and
crowding out an alternative approach rooted in rationality and global
cooperation.]{.underline} He does not frame the thesis in economic
terms. [Yet he essentially defends globalization]{.underline} and the
growth of market economies by claiming that it has brought more progress
than any force in history. As an economist, I agree. [But globalization
has also led to an escalation of risks. What is rational for individuals
is increasingly irrational for society. **The drivers of progress are
rising incomes and connectivity; these also lead to greater negative
spillovers and systemic risk**]{.underline}. Managing globalization's
underbelly is essential, and the gulf between what needs to be done and
what is being done is widening. **[Economic growth has come at the
expense of ecosystems]{.underline}**. Because nature does not respond to
price signals (rhinos do not reproduce more when their horns are more
valuable), **[increasing freedom of choice has led to overexploitation
of a growing number of natural systems.]{.underline}** Pinker does cite
climate change, but as a worrying exception to a relentlessly positive
narrative, rather than as the most glaring example of a wider failure of
global commons management.

#### Pinker's theories are *[gross mischaracterizations]{.underline}* of data -- globalization doesn't check other countries rather increases the magnitude of one crisis 

**Goldin, PhD, 18**

(Ian Goldin is Professor of Globalization and Development and director
of the Oxford Martin Programme on Technological and Economic Change at
the University of Oxford, UK, and the author of Age of Discovery and The
Butterfly Defect, Nature International Journal of Science, "The
limitations of Steven Pinker's optimism," 2-16 // NC)

[Pinker devotes a chapter to inequality.]{.underline} Drawing on the
encyclopedic website Our World in Data by economist Max Roser of the
Oxford Martin School, UK, [he shows that global inequality]{.underline}
(between richer and poorer countries[) is decreasing, whereas inequality
within countries is rising]{.underline}. He concludes that much current
anxiety around inequality --- spurred by the idea that the top 1% is
absorbing the lion's share of economic growth --- is misplaced. I don't
agree[. First, the data he presents do not reflect recent sharp
**economic reversals in many places.** These are likely to become more
significant in some regions.]{.underline} [For instance, in the
U]{.underline}nited [S]{.underline}tates, [cuts to health-care subsidies
and corporate tax rates over the past few months will reduce
redistribuion and weaken the safety nets that]{.underline} (as Pinker
identifies) [helped to alleviate inequality after the 2008 financial
crisis]{.underline}. [Second, he does n]{.underline}o[t]{.underline}
[give enough **weight to the plight of the jobless**]{.underline} ---
and, in the United States, its association with [the growing problem of
opioid misuse --- or the threat that]{.underline} robots and artificial
intelligence ([AI]{.underline}) [pose to jobs.]{.underline} [Growing
inequality]{.underline} within countries is [underminin]{.underline}g
[social cohesion and promot]{.underline}ing [extremist political
views]{.underline}, and this is likely to be compounded by technological
change. If most tasks that are repetitive and do not demand dexterity
are automated --- which is possible, according to research at the Oxford
Martin School --- what is the model of future employment in
administrative back offices, call centres or manufacturing? [Pinker is
too sanguine about such immediate threats]{.underline} (although he
argues cogently in favour of minimizing the expenditure of intellectual
and political capital on remote risks, such as the existential threat
posed by AI). He also underestimates the threats that Mike Mariathasan
and I set out in our 2015 book The Butterfly Defect (Princeton
University Press): interconnected systems raise interdependence,
complexity and systemic risk[. With globalization, as connectivity,
incomes and consumption grow, so **risks become multidimensional and
transnational --- witness**]{.underline} **[pandemics, cascading
financial crises and cyberattacks. New technologies turbocharge these
risks and give new powers to individuals and small
groups.]{.underline}** Pinker looks in some depth at the rise in
scepticism about science, in society and in parts of academia such as
the humanities. He gives shorter shrift to the erosion of faith in
expertise elsewhere, even though this is justified in some notable
instances. The financial sector, for example, is home to the biggest and
most powerful global expert system, from banks and treasuries to the
International Monetary Fund. The 2008 financial crisis highlighted the
inadequacy of that system, whose primary mission is financial stability.
Similarly, accounting firms have given clean bills of health to corrupt
or collapsing enterprises. The denial of evidence is irrational, but it
is not necessarily irrational to challenge experts and authorities.
[Pinker]{.underline} highlights the importance of ideas in human
advances, but claims that "intellectuals hate progress" --- a surprising
generalization in a case for evidence and reason. And herein lies both
the strength and weakness of this book. It [provides copious reasons for
optimism.]{.underline} It claims to be a scientific, evidence-based
account; but that **[is undermined by its scant attention to the
mounting evidence on new risks.]{.underline}** I share Pinker's optimism
that this could be our best century, in which poverty and many of the
challenges humanity has historically confronted are addressed. Yet there
is also a real potential for dystopian outcomes as sea levels, strife,
temperatures and resistant infections rise, and biodiversity, democratic
institutions, social ties, mental health and resource security are
eroded. We need to face up to these and other daunting challenges while
nurturing the positivity required to tackle them.

#### Pinker's focus on war based violent deaths ignore pro-longed cause and effect deaths and proliferation of proxy wars that *[increase]{.underline}* violence

**Gray, BA, 15**

(John Gray is the author of False Dawn: the Delusions of Global
Capitalism, Black Mass: Apocalyptic Religion and The Death of Utopia,
and Al Qaeda and What It Means to Be Modern. He attended South Shields
Grammar-Technical School for Boys from 1959 until 1967, then studied at
Exeter College, Oxford, reading Philosophy, Politics and Economics
(PPE), completing his B.A., M.Phil. and D.Phil., 3-13-2015, \"John Gray:
Steven Pinker is wrong about violence and war,\" Guardian,
<https://www.theguardian.com/books/2015/mar/13/john-gray-steven-pinker-wrong-violence-war-declining?source=post_page>
// NC)

[The picture of declining violence presented by this new orthodoxy is
not all it seems to be]{.underline}. As some critics, notably John
Arquilla, have pointed out, [it's a mistake to focus too heavily on
declining fatalities on the battlefield]{.underline}. If these deaths
have been falling, one reason is the balance of terror: nuclear weapons
have so far prevented industrial-style warfare between great powers.
[Pinker dismisses the role of nuclear weapons on the grounds that the
use of other weapons of mass destruction such as poison gas has not
prevented war in the past; but nuclear bombs are incomparably more
destructive]{.underline}. No serious military historian doubts that fear
of their use has been a major factor in preventing conflict between
great powers. Moreover, deaths of non-combatants have been steadily
rising. Around a million of the 10 million deaths due to the first world
war were of noncombatants, whereas around half of the more than 50
million casualties of the second world war and over 90% of the millions
who have perished in the violence that has wracked the Congo for decades
belong in that category**[. If great powers have avoided direct armed
conflict, they have fought one another in many proxy
wars.]{.underline}** [Neocolonial warfare in south-east
Asia]{.underline}, the [Korean]{.underline} war and the Chinese invasion
of [Tibet]{.underline}, British counter-insurgency warfare in [Malaya
and Kenya]{.underline}, the abortive Franco-British invasion of Suez,
the Angolan civil war, the Soviet invasions of Hungary, Czechoslovakia
and Afghanistan, the Vietnam war, the Iran-Iraq war, the first Gulf war,
covert intervention in the Balkans and the Caucasus, the invasion of
Iraq, the use of airpower in Libya, military aid to insurgents in Syria,
Russian cyber-attacks in the Baltic states and the proxy war between the
US and Russia that is being waged in Ukraine -- these are only some of
the contexts in which great powers have been involved in continuous
warfare against each other while avoiding direct military conflict.
Kalahari !Kung bushman on a hunter-gatherer expedition. Facebook Twitter
Pinterest 'Tribal peoples who have been praised by anthropologists for
their peaceful ways, such as the Kalahari !Kung, [in fact have rates of
death by violence not unlike those of contemporary
Detroit]{.underline}.' Photograph: Kim Walker/Robert Harding/Rex While
it is true that war has changed, it [has not become less
destructive]{.underline}. Rather than a contest between well-organised
states that can at some point negotiate peace**[, it is now more often a
many-sided conflict in fractured or collapsed states that no one has the
power to end.]{.underline}** The protagonists are armed irregulars, some
of them killing and being killed for the sake of an idea or faith,
others from fear or a desire for revenge and yet others from the world's
swelling armies of mercenaries, who fight for profit. [For all of them,
attacks on civilian populations have become normal.]{.underline} The
ferocious [conflict in Syria,]{.underline} [in which methodical
starvation]{.underline} and the systematic destruction of urban
environments [are deployed as strategies]{.underline}, [is an example of
this type of warfare. It may be true that the modern state's monopoly of
force has led]{.underline}, in some contexts, **[to declining rates of
violent death. But it is also true that the power of the modern state
has been used for purposes of mass killing,]{.underline}** and one
should not pass too quickly over victims of state terror. With
increasing historical knowledge it has become clear that the
"Holocaust-by-bullets" -- the mass shootings of Jews, mostly in the
Soviet Union, during the second world war -- was perpetrated on an even
larger scale than previously realised. Soviet agricultural
collectivisation incurred millions of foreseeable deaths, mainly as a
result of starvation, with deportation to uninhabitable regions,
life-threatening conditions in the Gulag and military-style operations
against recalcitrant villages also playing an important role. Peacetime
deaths due to internal repression under the Mao regime have been
estimated to be around 70 million. Along with fatalities caused by state
terror were unnumbered millions whose lives were irreparably broken and
shortened. How these casualties fit into the scheme of declining
violence is unclear. Pinker goes so far as to suggest that the
20th-century Hemoclysm might have been a gigantic statistical fluke, and
cautions that any history of the last century that represents it as
having been especially violent may be "apt to exaggerate the narrative
coherence of this history" (the italics are Pinker's[). However, there
is an equal or greater risk in abandoning a coherent and truthful
narrative of the violence of the last century for the sake of a spurious
quantitative precision.]{.underline} **[Estimating the numbers of those
who die from violence involves complex questions of cause and
effect]{.underline}**, which cannot always be separated from moral
judgments. [There are many kinds of lethal force that do not produce
immediate death]{.underline}. Are those who die of [hunger]{.underline}
or [disease]{.underline} during war or its aftermath counted among the
casualties? Do [refugees]{.underline} whose lives are cut short appear
in the count? Where torture is used in war, will its victims figure in
the calculus if they succumb years later from the [physical and mental
damage]{.underline} that has been inflicted on them? Do infants who are
born to brief and painful lives as a result of exposure to Agent Orange
or depleted [uranium]{.underline} find a place in the roll call of the
dead? If women who have been [rape]{.underline}d as part of a military
strategy of sexual violence die before their time, will their passing
feature in the statistical tables? There is something repellently absurd
in the notion that war is a vice of 'backward' peoples While the seeming
exactitude of statistics may be compelling, much of the human cost of
war is incalculable**[. Deaths by violence are not all
equal]{.underline}**. It is terrible to die as a conscript in the
trenches or a civilian in an aerial bombing campaign, but to perish from
overwork, beating or cold in a labour camp can be a greater evil[. It is
worse still to be killed as part of a systematic campaign of
extermination as happened to those who were consigned to death camps
such as Treblinka]{.underline}. [Disregarding these distinctions, the
statistics presented by those who celebrate the]{.underline} arrival of
the [Long Peace are morally dubious if not meaningless]{.underline}. The
radically contingent nature of the figures is another reason for not
taking them too seriously. (For a critique of Pinker's statistical
methods, see Nassim Nicholas Taleb's essay on the Long Peace.) If the
socialist revolutionary Fanya Kaplan had succeeded in assassinating
Lenin in August 1918, violence would still have raged on in Russia. But
the Soviet state might not have survived and could not have been used by
Stalin for slaughter on a huge scale. If a resolute war leader had not
unexpectedly come to power in Britain in May 1940, and the country had
been defeated or (worse) made peace with Germany as much of the British
elite wanted at the time, Europe would likely have remained under Nazi
rule for generations to come -- time in which plans of racial
purification and genocide could have been more fully implemented.
Discussing the Cuban missile crisis of 1962 in which nuclear war was
narrowly averted, Pinker dismisses the view that "the de-escalation was
purely a stroke of uncanny good luck". Instead, he explains the fact
that nuclear war was avoided by reference to the superior judgment of
Kennedy and Khrushchev, who had "an intuitive grasp of game theory" --
an example of increasing rationality in history, Pinker believes. But a
disastrous escalation in the crisis may in fact have been prevented only
by a Soviet submariner, Vasili Arkhipov, who refused to obey orders from
his captain to launch a nuclear torpedo. Had it not been for the
accidental presence of a single courageous human being, a nuclear
conflagration could have occurred causing fatalities on a vast scale. A
screengrab from a video released by the Nigerian Islamist extremist
group Boko Haram on 13 July 2014. Facebook Twitter Pinterest A
screengrab from a video released by the Nigerian Islamist extremist
group Boko Haram on 13 July 2014. Photograph: HO/AFP/Getty Images There
is something repellently absurd in the notion that war is a vice of
"backward" peoples. Destroying some of the most refined civilisations
that have ever existed, the wars that ravaged south-east Asia in the
second world war and the decades that followed were the work of colonial
powers. One of the causes of the genocide in Rwanda was the segregation
of the population by German and Belgian imperialism. Unending war in the
Congo has been fuelled by western demand for the country's natural
resources. If violence has dwindled in advanced societies, one reason
may be that they have exported it. Then again, the idea that violence is
declining in the most highly developed countries is questionable. Judged
by accepted standards, the United States is the most advanced society in
the world. According to many estimates the US also has the highest rate
of incarceration, some way ahead of China and Russia, for example.
[Around a quarter of all the world's prisoners are held in American
jails,]{.underline} many for exceptionally long periods[. Black people
are disproportionately represented, many prisoners are mentally ill and
growing numbers are aged and infirm]{.underline}. [Imprisonment in
America involves continuous risk of assault by other
prisoners]{.underline}. There is the threat of long periods spent in
[solitary confinement]{.underline}, sometimes (as in "supermax"
facilities, where something like Bentham's Panopticon has been
constructed) for indefinite periods -- a type of treatment that has been
reasonably classified as torture. [Cruel and unusual punishments
involving flogging and mutilation]{.underline} may have been abolished
in many countries, but, along with unprecedented levels of mass
incarceration, **[the practice of torture seems to be integral to the
functioning of the world's most advanced state]{.underline}**. It may
not be an accident that torture is often deployed in the special
operations that have replaced more traditional types of warfare. [The
extension of counter-terrorism to include assassination by unaccountable
mercenaries and remote-controlled killing by drones is part of this
shift]{.underline}. A metamorphosis in the nature is war is under way,
which is global in reach. With the state of Iraq in ruins as a result of
US-led regime change, a third of the country is controlled by Isis,
which is able to inflict genocidal attacks on Yazidis and wage a
campaign of terror on Christians with near-impunity. In Nigeria, the
Islamist militias of Boko Haram practise a type of warfare featuring
mass killing of civilians, razing of towns and villages and sexual
enslavement of women and children. In Europe, targeted killing of
journalists, artists and Jews in Paris and Copenhagen embodies a type of
warfare that refuses to recognise any distinction between combatants and
civilians. Whether they accept the fact or not, advanced societies have
become terrains of violent conflict. Rather than war declining, the
difference between peace and war has been fatally blurred. [Deaths on
the battlefield have fallen and may continue to fall. From one angle
this can be seen as an advancing condition of peace. From another point
of view that looks at the variety and intensity with which violence is
being employed, **the Long Peace can be described as a condition of
perpetual conflict.**]{.underline}

### AT: Pinker -- Warming

#### **Doesn't assume warming which, when sponsored by developed countries and institutions, makes overshoot *[inevitable]{.underline}*** 

**Lent, MBA ,18**

(Jeremy Lent graduated from the University College School and earned an
undergraduate degree in English Literature at Emmanuel College,
Cambridge University in 1981. Lent left the UK to live in the United
States. In 1986, he earned an MBA from the University of Chicago. Lent
is an author whose writings investigate the patterns of thought that
have led civilization to its current crisis of sustainability. He is the
founder of the non-profit Liology Institute, which is dedicated to a
worldview that could enable humanity to thrive sustainably., 5-21-2018,
\" Steven Pinker's ideas are fatally flawed. These eight graphs show
why,\" Open Democracy,
<https://www.opendemocracy.net/en/transformation/steven-pinker-s-ideas-are-fatally-flawed-these-eight-graphs-show-why/>
// NC)

In November 2017, [around the time when Pinker was likely putting the
final touches on his manuscript, over fifteen thousand scientists from
184 countries issued a dire warning to humanity]{.underline}. [Because
of our overconsumption of the world's resources, they declared, we are
facing "widespread misery and catastrophic biodiversity loss." They
warned that time is running out]{.underline}: "Soon it will be too late
to shift course away from our failing trajectory." JeremyLent1.png
Figure 1: Three graphs from World Scientists\' Warning to Humanity: A
Second Notice. They included nine sobering charts and a carefully
worded, extensively researched analysis showing that, on a multitude of
fronts, the human impact on the earth's biological systems is increasing
at an unsustainable rate. Three of those alarming graphs are shown here:
the rise in CO2 emissions; the decline in available freshwater; and the
increase in the number of ocean dead zones from artificial fertilizer
runoff. This was not the first such notice. Twenty-five years earlier,
in 1992, 1,700 scientists (including the majority of living Nobel
laureates) sent a similarly worded warning to governmental leaders
around the world, calling for a recognition of the earth's fragility and
a new ethic arising from the realization that "we all have but one
lifeboat." The current graphs starkly demonstrate how little the world
has paid attention to this warning since 1992. [Taken together, these
graphs illustrate ecological overshoot:]{.underline} the fact that, [in
the pursuit of material progress, our civilization is consuming the
earth's resources faster than they can be replenished]{.underline}.
Overshoot is particularly dangerous because of its relatively slow
feedback loops: if your checking account balance approaches zero, you
know that if you keep writing checks they will bounce. In overshoot,
however, it's as though our civilization keeps taking out bigger and
bigger overdrafts to replenish the account, and then we pretend these
funds are income and celebrate our continuing "progress." In the end, of
course, the money runs dry and it's game over. [Pinker claims to respect
science, yet he blithely ignores fifteen thousand scientists' desperate
warning to humanity. Instead,]{.underline} he uses the blatant
rhetorical technique of ridicule to paint those concerned about
overshoot as part of a "quasi-religious ideology... laced with
misanthropy, including an indifference to starvation, an indulgence in
ghoulish fantasies of a depopulated planet, and Nazi-like comparisons of
human beings to vermin, pathogens, and cancer." [He]{.underline} then
[uses a couple of the most extreme examples he can find to create a
straw-man to buttress his caricature]{.underline}. There are issues
worthy of debate on the topic of civilization and sustainability, but to
approach a subject of such seriousness with emotion-laden rhetoric is
morally inexcusable and striking evidence of Monbiot's claim that Pinker
"insults the Enlightenment principles he claims to defend." When
[Pinker]{.underline} does get serious on the topic, he [promotes
Ecomodernism as the solution: a neoliberal, technocratic belief that a
combination of market-based solutions and technological fixes will
magically resolve all ecological problems. This approach
fails]{.underline}, however, [to take into account the structural
drivers of overshoot: **a growth-based global economy reliant on
ever-increasing monetization of natural resources and human
activity.**]{.underline} [**Without changing this structure, overshoot
is inevitable**. Transnational corporations]{.underline}, which
currently constitute sixty-nine of the world's hundred largest
economies, [are driven only by increasing short-term financial value for
their shareholders]{.underline}, regardless of the long-term impact on
humanity[. As freshwater resources decline,]{.underline} for example,
[their incentive is to buy up what remains]{.underline} and sell it in
plastic throwaway bottles or process it into sugary drinks, propelling
billions in developing countries toward obesity through sophisticated
marketing[. In fact, until an imminent collapse of civilization itself,
increasing ecological catastrophes are likely to enhance the GDP of
developed countries even while those in less developed regions suffer
dire consequences.]{.underline}

### AT: Battle Deaths Decreasing

#### Battle death metrics are overly optimistic -- data sets aren't comparable over history, omit conflicts, and don't assume improvements in military medicine or military technologies like drone strikes and cyberwarfare

\*also at: Goldstein and Pinker

**Fazal, PhD, 14**

(Tanisha, Ph.D.: Political Science, Stanford University, Stanford, CA,
2001. "Dead Wrong?: Battle Deaths, Military Medicine, and Exaggerated
Reports of War\'s Demise." 2014.
http://muse.jhu.edu/article/553214.)//bc

[Rethinking the approach to how scholars]{.underline} of international
relations [count wars may require significant revisions to major
datasets such as the Correlates of War and the UCDP lists of armed
conflicts]{.underline}. Both datasets have a fatality threshold for
inclusion, and that threshold is constant for the entire period they
cover.113 But if, [because of improvements in military
medicine]{.underline}, **[the same conflict that produced 1,200
fatalities in 1860 is likely to have produced 800 fatalities in
1980]{.underline}** (or 35 versus 20, in the case of UCDP), **[the
ahistorical nature of the battle death threshold means that the latter
conflict(s) will have been left out of the data set]{.underline}**.
[Including these conflicts in Goldstein's and Pinker's analyses could
force a closer look at whether war has really declined]{.underline}. A
brief survey of UCDP's list of armed conflicts yields more than a dozen
conflicts that produced more than 500 battle deaths in the post-1946
period. [Among them is the 1963 "Sand War" between Morocco and
Algeria]{.underline}, where Micheal Clodfelter reports 300 Algerian and
200 Moroccan fatalities. Based on Algeria's wounded-to-killed ratio from
the independence war just prior to the interstate conflict with Morocco,
it is possible [that Algerian casualties alone exceeded the
1,000-combatant-death threshold used by the Correlates of
War]{.underline}.114 More recently, [Clodfelter reports 700 fatalities
in Thailand's civil war with the Patani in the south]{.underline}, which
began in 2003. This conflict is another candidate for inclusion in a
list of wars that takes all casualties into account. [Depending on a
number of factors---including the percentage of fatalities that have
been sustained by the government]{.underline}---usin[g past
wounded-to-killed ratios for Thailand suggests that this conflict may
have produced more than 1,000 battle casualtie]{.underline}s in a single
year.115 One cannot be confident in any of these adjustments, however,
until additional data on the battle wounded are available. [The
possibility that wars and armed conflicts have been undercounted because
existing datasets use a battle death threshold that does not change over
time **raises questions about the comparability of cases over
time**]{.underline} and the soundness of quantitative analyses that
cover the nineteenth and twentieth centuries, in particular. For
example, several scholars have debated whether democracies are more
likely to win the wars they fight and, if they are, why that would be
so.116 [What if democracies]{.underline}, especially twentieth-century
democracies, [are likely to have especially good military medical
care]{.underline}? Posing this \[End Page 123\] question raises at least
two possible implications for the debate. First, it may be that [certain
wars are not included in the observations under analysis]{.underline},
especially for the twentieth century. Second[, if democracies are more
likely to have stronger military medical systems]{.underline}, then,
[all else being equal, this could tip the balance in their favor in
prosecuting wars]{.underline}. In other words, relative capability in
