military medicine could constitute an alternative explanation for the
finding that democracies are winners. To the extent that the general
result that democracies are more likely than nondemocracies to win the
wars they fight is sensitive to recodings of data,117 incorporating the
rise of military medicine could alter this finding. [Bringing military
medicine into the discussion of what counts as war also has implications
for society and policy]{.underline}. T[he massive investment that modern
militaries have made in military medicine reflects their view of its
importance]{.underline}, but the story of the battle wounded has
typically remained untold.118 For example, [in the first year of
Operation Enduring Freedom]{.underline}, [the New York Times published
just one story on the military wounded]{.underline}; its "Casualties"
column effectively redefined "casualty" to mean only fatalities.119
Moreover, [these reports include only the visibly wounded]{.underline},
[ignoring the thousands of veterans suffering from posttraumatic stress
disorder and other]{.underline} war-induced [psychiatric
conditions]{.underline}.120 Similarly, an influential book on casualty
aversion and public support for war in the United States explicitly
takes "casualty" to mean "fatality," and finds (in a robustness test)
that [including the wounded in survey experiments]{.underline} \[End
Page 124\] on casualty aversion [did not alter respondents' level of
support for conflict]{.underline}.121 [The wounded]{.underline}, in
other words**[, seem not to figure into "the human costs of war" in any
significant way.]{.underline}** More broadly[, using fatality counts as
a measure of war is increasingly questionable given new technologies of
warfare]{.underline}. U.S. [drone strikes]{.underline} in Afghanistan
and Pakistan [have probably killed fewer than 1,000 combatants each
annually]{.underline}.122 [Yet]{.underline}, in Pakistan's Federally
Administrated Tribal Areas, **[civilians and soldiers alike must surely
feel like they are living in a war zone]{.underline}**.123 In addition,
[the development of nonlethal weapons]{.underline}---used today
primarily for police actions---**[could result in conflicts with many
fewer direct casualties]{.underline}**.124 Similarly, a barrage of
[cyberattacks]{.underline}, which could cause a great deal of damage
with or without generating casualties directly, [could make a population
feel it was under siege]{.underline}.125 If, as Carl von Clausewitz
observed, "war is the continuation of politics by other means," must
those other means cause fatalities to qualify as war? More to the point,
must they cause a specific, constant number of fatalities? The [rapid
advances in military medicine in the last half century alone suggest
that an ahistorical measure of war may deny the severity]{.underline}
(and possibly incidence) [of many recent conflicts.]{.underline}
**[Although war may be wounded, it is]{.underline}**, sadly for all its
victims, **[not dead.]{.underline}**

### 2NC/2AC -- Temporary Peace

#### Reliance on *[rates-based]{.underline}* data ignores *[actual human suffering]{.underline}* -- Pinker doesn't assume *[temporary peace]{.underline}* as warming, resources shortages and territorial disputes could single handedly outweigh other conflicts in deaths

**Koehrsen**, **1-5**-2019

(Will, Master\'s degree in mechanical engineering from the case western
reserve university, Applied data science researcher and data scientist
for cortex building intelligence, \"Has Global Violence Declined? A Look
at the Data,\" Medium,
[https://towardsdatascience.com/has-global-violence-declined-a-look-at-the-data-5af708f47fba
//](https://towardsdatascience.com/has-global-violence-declined-a-look-at-the-data-5af708f47fba%20//)
NC)

[In a narrow point of view, Pinker is correct:]{.underline} rates of
violent conflict have substantially decreased and there is reason to
believe they are at the lowest point in human history (if only because
data is limited). Also, at least among modern democracies, violence is
no longer seen as the default option for solving problems as it has been
for almost all of human history. That being said, it's worth thinking
about [how Pinker's argument made be misleading]{.underline}. [The first
point is that by focusing on rates rather than numbers, we are
**neglecting the actual human suffering**]{.underline}. More people
dying from war is more people dying from war even if the percentage is
decreasing. The numbers can be used to support multiple conclusions
depending on one's argument. **[A second central issue is, especially
for pre-historic societies, reliable data is hard to get]{.underline}**.
Most of the numbers for conflict rates and deaths in prehistoric
societies come by examining archaeological sites and looking at
artifacts for evidence of violent death. However, [it could be possible
these marks have been misinterpreted and anthropologists could
not]{.underline} possibly [have studied all prehistoric
societies]{.underline}. [Simple extrapolation what has been found is the
only option]{.underline}, but it's also important to not draw immature
conclusions. [The last major issue we'll cover here]{.underline} (for
more see Taleb's article**[) is that we could be living in an anomalous
age.]{.underline}** Pinker calls the most recent 70 years of peace (no
wars between modern nations[), "The Long Peace",]{.underline} but it
[could]{.underline} be **[really only the temporary
peace.]{.underline}** Furthermore, as pointed out by Taleb, [wars tend
to be power law distributed, that is, they have long tails skewed to the
right. The vast majority of deaths in armed conflicts are caused by a
small number of the conflicts]{.underline} as can be seen below:
Distribution of war casualty deaths on log **[scale One single conflict
can completely outweigh all others in terms of deaths]{.underline}**.
Most of the armed conflicts since 1945 have fallen on the low end of the
scale, but it only takes a single major battle to overshadow the peace
since 1945. [It's possible **a new conflict, one caused by global
warming, resource shortages, or territorial disputes,** could entirely
undo the millennia of progress humans]{.underline} have made on violence
reduction efforts.

## Ethnocentrism

### AT: Ethnocentrism Inevitable

#### Game theory models prove ethnocentrism is not inevitable -- rather an effect of *[structural immobility]{.underline}* 

**University of Maryland, 15**

(\"Moving matters: Ethnocentric behavior decreases when societal
mobility rises: Society\'s level of mobility influences whether its
members treat outsiders as individuals.\" ScienceDaily. ScienceDaily, 8
December 2015. [www.sciencedaily.com/releases/2015/12/151208133616.htm
//](http://www.sciencedaily.com/releases/2015/12/151208133616.htm%20//)
NC)

The trend over the last century has been toward greater mobility for
more people around the world. Many people today live in a place
different from where they were born, with different social norms and
customs[. A new study by University of Maryland researchers points to a
surprising byproduct: increased mobility may help people to treat each
other as individuals rather than as members of a defined social group.
The work suggests]{.underline} that **[mobility counteracts the tendency
of populations to become more ethnocentric]{.underline}** \-- or prone
to favor members of their own ethnic, tribal or national group while
being hostile to other groups \-- over time. The study appears online in
the journal Nature Scientific Reports on Dec. 8, 2015. \"Our study marks
the first time that a computational model of evolutionary game theory
has been used to examine whether the well-studied psychological
phenomenon of ethnocentrism develops differently with increased
mobility,\" said Soham De, lead author of the study and a graduate
student in computer science at UMD. \"[The underlying message is very
hopeful: **ethnocentric behavior is not inevitable**, so perhaps there
are additional steps that human societies can take to reduce
conflict]{.underline}.\" Historically, most group conflicts have been
between people who define themselves primarily by the groups to which
they belong. **[Previous psychology research has established that such
categorization is natural]{.underline}**, with humans classifying others
into \'in groups\' and \'out groups.\' A common outcome is for
individuals to favor those in their own group while being hostile to
outsiders. [Indeed, many cultural psychology studies have suggested that
that this tendency toward ethnocentrism is inevitable]{.underline} in
the development of human societies[**. The current study supports the
idea that ethnocentric behavior is not inevitable**, but is affected by
structural conditions within a society, including overall levels of
mobility.]{.underline} The team began with a well-established computer
model of human behavior based on the tenets of game theory, a concept
that economists, political scientists and psychologists use to study
patterns of human decision-making. Such collaborations between computer
scientists and social scientists have become more common, as the former
can use computational models to test theories put forward by the latter.
\"[Evolutionary game theoretic models can help explain how a society\'s
characteristics may evolve over time, as individuals change their
actions and strategies in order to adapt to their current
conditions]{.underline},\" said study co-author Dana Nau, a professor of
computer science at UMD with a joint appointment in the UMD Institute
for Systems Research. \"[Although these models are much simpler than
real human societies, they can help to tease apart causal relationships
and provide support for social science theories]{.underline}.\" [By
introducing mobility into their model, the researchers dramatically
reduced the emergence of ethnocentric behavior]{.underline}. [Their
results indicated that when societies are more mobile]{.underline},
**[their actions are more likely to depend on seeing people as
individuals]{.underline}**, **[rather than as part of a perceived
group]{.underline}**. In addition[, the findings show that there is less
hostility toward other groups in]{.underline} highly [mobile places
compared with those with low mobility.]{.underline} Research in cultural
psychology suggests that in mobile societies, people strategically adapt
to their situation by changing their relationships often, having a broad
network of casual acquaintances and being open toward strangers. They
are likely to evaluate others based on their individual trustworthiness
and worth. But in places where people are not very mobile, opportunities
to form new relationships are limited \-- and ending existing
relationships can carry harsh consequences. In these places, maintaining
loyalty to one\'s own group while keeping a safe distance from other
groups is a good adaptive strategy. \"In contexts that are mobile,
people are constantly coming and going from different relationships;
being open to strangers is presumably adaptive in these circumstances,\"
said study co-author Michele Gelfand, a professor of psychology at UMD.
\"On the other hand, in low-mobility contexts, individuals have far
fewer opportunities to form new relationships. They get most of their
needs met through the group they identify with; in this context, in
makes more sense to treat people according to the groups to which they
belong.\" The researchers ran their game theoretic model through tens of
thousands of iterations, covering various scenarios on UMD\'s
Deepthought2 supercomputer cluster. In low-mobility scenarios, the
researchers found that 75 percent of the population adopted strategies
that favored their own group and were hostile to others. When the
researchers increased the amount of mobility in the model, the inverse
scenario developed, with about 70 percent of the population using
strategies that were more dependent on individual behavior. Thus,
mobility thwarted the evolutionary dominance of group perception and
ethnocentric strategies[. The team then tested their results against
real-life data from the U.S. Census Bureau,]{.underline} which provides
measures of mobility for all 50 states. [Mobility was positively
correlated with responses to the question,]{.underline} \"I am
interested in the cultures of other countries\" and negatively
correlated with responses to questions related to ethnocentric
attitudes[. This analysis of census data was consistent with the results
from the model]{.underline}. \"It\'s always good to test whether what
you predict in your model actually happens in real life,\" Nau said.
Gelfand is careful to note the research does not address how mobility
affects people\'s attitudes, but rather how it affects people\'s
interactions with one another. \"One characteristic an agent can have in
our model is whether they treat another person more as an individual,
rather than a member of a particular group,\" Gelfand said. \"As the
level of mobility goes up a bit, they start to need to have interactions
with new groups. They do much better in these kinds of circumstances if
they treat others as individuals. It becomes adaptive.\" Gelfand also
cautioned that because the model is very abstract, it will not apply to
every real-world scenario. \"But overall, in a mobility context, we
start treating people as individuals rather than recognizing them only
as a member of a category,\" she said.

### AT: Evolution Explains Ethnocentrism

#### Evolutionary explanations for ethnocentrism are non falsifiable and aren't empirically accurate at explaining intra-ethnic conflicts.

**Bizumi, PhD, 12**

(Boris, PhD from Australian National University. (2012) Theories of
Ethnocentrism and Their Implications for Peacebuilding. In: Simi O.,
Voli Z., Philpot C. (eds) Peace Psychology in the Balkans. Peace
Psychology Book Series. Springer, Boston, MA) //bc

[Evolutionary theorists have argued that ethnocentrism enhances the
survival of ethnic groups.]{.underline} For example,
[Darwin]{.underline} ( 1879 ) [assumed that intergroup
competiti]{.underline}on [makes people more cooperative with ingroup
members]{.underline} [and that cooperative groups are more likely to
prosper than non-cooperative groups]{.underline}. The social Darwinist
Spencer ( 1892 ) argued: A life of constant external enmity generates a
code in which aggression, conquest, revenge, are inculcated, while
peaceful occupations are reprobated. Conversely, a life of settled
internal amity generates a code inculcating the virtues conducing to
harmonious cooperation -- justice, honesty, veracity, regard for other's
claims (p. 471). According to Spencer, these two moral codes exist
together in modern and nonindustrial societies. Such ideas were also put
forward by Sumner ( 1906 ) . According to Keith ( 1949 ) , group
survival is possible only when intragroup cooperation and intergroup
competition are combined and in balance. Otherwise, Keith assumed, too
much cooperation or too much competition is detrimental to group
survival. More recent evolutionary explanations, based on
sociobiological theory, maintain that ethnocentrism is an extension of
the basic mechanism underlying kin group selection. Van den Berghe's (
1978, 1995 ) explanation for ethnocentrism illustrates this approach. He
argues that it is beneficial for animal species, including humans, to
cooperate and the main genetic mechanism for cooperation is kin
selection that maximizes inclusive fitness. Inclusive fitness assumes
that genes can be transmitted to ancestors through direct and kin
reproduction. Animals, therefore, tend to cooperate with their kin,
which they favour over non-kin. Because of its evolutionary usefulness,
humans have extended their kinship groups to ethnic groups. As Van den
Berghe states, "ethnocentrism evolved during millions, or at least
hundreds of thousands of years as an extension of kin selection" (p.
404). [Evolutionary explanations can be applied to the present
conceptualization of ethnocentrism]{.underline}. Stressing the
importance of the ethnic ingroup over others and over individual ingroup
members should help ethnic group survival and enhance the transmission
of the ethnic group's genetic material (i.e., extended kin group).
[**Evolutionary explanations of ethnocentrism have been criticized.** It
has never been convincingly demonstrated that ethnocentric groups are
more likely to survive than other groups]{.underline}. Similarly,
[mechanisms that enable the extension of kin group
selection]{.underline} [to]{.underline} [ethnic groups have not been
clearly elucidated]{.underline} (A. D. Smith, 2001 ) . In addition, it
has been argued that [central elements of sociobiological
explanations]{.underline} **[are often not testable]{.underline}** and
that [alternative explanations of the data]{.underline} **[are often
equally plausible and some data]{.underline}** (e.g., hostility,
non-cooperation, and destruction within kin groups) **[even contradict
the theory]{.underline}** (Ross, 1991 ) . Summary and Implications
Evolutionary theories often paint a bleak picture of humanity suggesting
that ethnocentrism is generally difficult, if not impossible, to change,
and may often result in conflicts and wars. [It is]{.underline},
however, [more likely,]{.underline} as M.B. Smith ( 1992 ) [put it, that
"ethnocentrism ... may well have biological roots in human evolution**,
although not necessarily of the sort assumed by the biodeterministic
pessimists**]{.underline}" (p. 84). The relationships between most
ethnic groups in the world tend to be relatively harmonious or often
neutral, and not necessarily confl ictual. Nevertheless, a certain
amount of ethnocentrism may exist in perhaps all ethnic groups, and this
may be due to evolutionary influences. Furthermore, although
ethnocentrism may be evolutionary, and perhaps inevitable, [most
scholars today reject the view that ethnic conflicts]{.underline}, such
as these in ex-Yugoslavia, are **[directly caused by inherent
evolutionary propensities of certain ethnic groups.]{.underline}**
Accordingly, although peace psychologists need to be acquainted with
evolutionary explanations of ethnocentrism, [it is important to note
that evolutionary influences are distal and do not necessarily result in
confl icts.]{.underline}

### AT: Ethnocentrism Intrinsic

#### Ethnocentrism is caused by group categorization and depersonalization -- it's not an intrinsic trait

**Bizumic, PhD, and Duckitt, PhD, 12**

(Boris, PhD from Australian National University, and John, Ph.D from the
University of the Witwatersrand in South Africa. (2012), What Is and Is
Not Ethnocentrism? A Conceptual Analysis and Political Implications.
Political Psychology, 33: 887-909. doi:10.1111/j.1467-9221.2012.00907.x)
//bc

There have been many theories attempting to explain what causes
ethnocentrism. These theories have focused on different levels, ranging
from genetic to societal. Most theories dealt with diverse
conceptualizations of ethnocentrism and often did not specifically
differentiate mere ingroup positivity, outgroup negativity, and group
self-centeredness. In terms of social psychological theories, we argue
that this reconceptualization fits in well within three complementary
theories of intragroup and intergroup processes: [realistic conflict
theory]{.underline} (Sherif, 1966), [social identity]{.underline} theory
(Tajfel & Turner,1986), [and self-categorization theory]{.underline}
(Turner et al., 1987). A theoretical explanation follows (for a shorter
explanation, see Bizumic et al., 2009).[All three theories argue that
people become transformed when they are in groups]{.underline}, **[and
the process through which this happens is most fully explained by
self-categorization theory.]{.underline}** According to
self-categorization theory, the basis of group formation involves
categorization, which enables switching from a definition of oneself as
a separate individual to a definition of oneself as a group member.
[This involves depersonalization]{.underline}, through which
[individuals cognitively group themselves together with a social
category]{.underline} (in this instance, one's ethnic or cultural group)
and tend to see themselves as interchangeable with other group members.
As a result of this process, the person experiences himself or herself
at a different level, as a group member, and tends to give more
importance to the group than to his or her own separate individuality
and separate individuality of other ingroup members. Identification with
the group, accordingly, gives rise to what we called intragroup
ethnocentrism and involves two expressions: group cohesion (group unity
and integration become more important than individual freedoms) and
devotion (group interests take over personal self-interest and the
person is ready to sacrifice for the sake of the group if it needs to be
done). In short, [as a result of perceiving the self as one with the
group, the group and its interests]{.underline} (i.e., the whole)
**[become more important than one's own individuality and separate
individual group members]{.underline}**(i.e., the elements of the
whole).Sumner (1911) stated that [ethnocentrism primarily involves
sentiments of group cohesion and devotion]{.underline}, but he also
noted that [these dimensions carry with themselves other
sentiments]{.underline}, [which correspond to intergroup
ethnocentrism]{.underline}. He did not explain the social psychological
processes through which this happens. The social identity perspective
(involving social identity and self-categorization theories) offers such
an explanation. It argues that [group formation]{.underline} [can lead
to intergroup ethnocentrism]{.underline} **[because of the existence of
other groups]{.underline}**. [When people give strong importance to
their ethnic group]{.underline} (in relation to other ingroup members
and themselves individually), and [when they perceive other ethnic
groups]{.underline}, **[they tend to give more importance to their
ethnic group over other groups.]{.underline}** The process of social
comparison is responsible for this because through this process
individuals and groups strive for a sense of positive distinctiveness.
The sense of strong importance of the ingroup in relation to outgroups
is what we called intergroup ethnocentrism, and it has four expressions:
preference (favoring the ingroup), superiority (hyper evaluation of the
ingroup), purity(rejection of outgroups), and exploitativeness (tendency
to impinge on the rights of those who are outside the group).

### 2NC/2AC -- Heg Bad -- Interventionism

#### Heg is counterproductive -- causes endless interventionism -- risks nuclear conflict

**Faris**, **19**, \[David Faris is an associate professor of political
science at Roosevelt University\], (5-17-2019, \"The dangerous myth of
American hegemony\",
https://theweek.com/articles/841854/dangerous-myth-american-hegemony,
accessed 7-19-2019), //Snitty

The Trump administration [unveiled
plans](https://www.nytimes.com/2019/05/13/world/middleeast/us-military-plans-iran.html)

are attacked or if work on a nuclear weapons program is resumed. This
stunning revelation, in the absence of any real provocation from Tehran,
has justifiably caused great alarm for anyone who doesn\'t want to see
the United States stumble into another ruinous war in the Middle East.
[It also should worry us about the overall trajectory of American
foreign policy, as the U.S. looks increasingly like it is in dangerous
decline --- drunk on vanishing power, fearful of a reshuffling of
hierarchies, and driven by emotional decision-making and irrational
fears. The United States today seems incapable of correctly appraising
how its power to coerce other actors in the international system is
diminishing.]{.underline} The wars in Afghanistan and Iraq waged this
century not only failed to achieve even the most charitable
interpretations of their objectives, [but also further destabilized the
region, empowered hostile actors like Iran, and should have confirmed
for any sane observer that the United States lacks the ability to
transform distant societies with military force. Yet]{.underline}
President [Trump\'s]{.underline} [administration]{.underline} [continues

decade following the collapse of the Soviet Union. Indeed, the
administration\'s general attitude seems lifted directly from \"The

written by the late Charles Krauthammer back in 1990. \"The center of
world power is the unchallenged superpower, the United States, attended
by its Western allies,\" he wrote. [He warned ominously of \"the
emergence of a new strategic environment, marked by the rise of small
aggressive states armed with weapons of mass destruction and possessing
the means to deliver them]{.underline},\" [and urged America to
aggressively confront them in order to maintain its dominion over the

past 30 years have crammed their thinking i]{.underline}nto
Krauthammer\'s framework, [flipping out over the slightest provocation
from what became known as \"rogue states]{.underline},\" Iran prominent
among them, and [drawing the United States into a series of unnecessary
and disastrous conflicts]{.underline}. But the immolation of America\'s
designs in Iraq and Afghanistan [led to a revision of American hegemony
by the Obama administration --- admittedly incomplete, and haphazard,
but nevertheless meaningful efforts to extract the United States from
the Middle East and from our seemingly incurable monomania to spend
precious resources hounding and punishing poor, isolated countries with

during the 2016 campaign about the stupidity of Middle East

poor and isolated North Korea to heel, the humiliating impasse over the
details of trade with China, and now this farcical effort to impose
terms on the Iranians are all the follies of an over-the-hill global

influence, or is gripped with panic over the impending erosion of
American power.]{.underline} [**Declining powers** aren\'t just
inept]{.underline}. They **[are also dangerous]{.underline}**. A
c[ountry whose power is deteriorating, particularly one that has fallen

aggressive with rhetoric and who has surrounded himself with
unreconstructed militarists, could act aggressively with both rising
challengers as well as less powerful countries like Iran. What is most
concerning about recent American foreign policy behavior is the extent
to which it may be driven by the need to settle scores before our
relative power declines any further.]{.underline} As political scientist
Jack Levy explained [in a 1988 World Politics
article](https://www.jstor.org/stable/2010195?seq=1#page_scan_tab_contents),
\"[The temptation is to fight a war under relatively favorable
circumstances now ... to avoid both the worsening of the status quo over
time and the risk of war under less favorable circumstances
later.\"]{.underline} America\'s bizarre obsession with Iran fits
Levy\'s framework perfectly. Terrified that the Iran Deal might lead to
an increase in Iranian power, the Trump administration set it on fire,
and has since taken a series of aggressive steps designed to ratchet up
tensions. Apart from the egregious waste of time and resources on a weak

unworkable. The problem is actually much simpler than anyone would like
to acknowledge. The United States today lacks the power to compel Iran
to sign an agreement more favorable than the one the Trump
administration tore up last year, and more importantly it lacks the
expertise, resources, and political will to fight a protracted war.
American threats are empty, not just because the president keeps saying
in public that he doesn\'t want war, but because his goals are
unachievable. [The truth is that America is deliberately running a
trillion-dollar peacetime deficit in the middle of a decade-long

plan.]{.underline} Fresh off [spending \$5.6
trillion](https://news.brown.edu/articles/2017/11/costssummary)
despoiling Iraq and spinning our wheels in the endless Afghanistan
quagmire, the United States can ill afford to prosecute a border
skirmish, let alone a full-blown war with an enormous country of 82
million people. Not only that, but the odds of getting appropriations
for an Iran war through the House of Representatives are steep.

### 2NC/2AC -- China Rise Good

#### China rise good --- its focus on [territorial integrity]{.underline} ensures a stable international order 

**Griffiths, 16** - Associate Professor in the Department of Political
Science at the Maxwell School of Citizenship and Public Affairs (Ryan
D., \"States, Nations, and Territorial Stability: Why Chinese Hegemony
Would Be Better for International Order\", Security Studies, 7-8-2016,
Vol. 25, Issue 3, p. 519-545))SK

China\'s acknowledgement of the external meaning of territorial
integrity is a contested topic. [The cynical view holds that a
predominant China will try to expand]{.underline} and redraw political
borders [because rising states are revisionist]{.underline} and
territorial expansion is par for the course. Such behavior would clearly
undermine the territorial integrity norm. But I contend that alt[hough
China will increasingly insist on a hegemonic relationship]{.underline}
with its neighbors, [it will do so with an eye on **preserving the
territorial integrity** of states]{.underline}. It might press its
current territorial claims, but [it would not strip states of their
sovereignty and transform them into Chinese possessions]{.underline}. My
position is built on two related arguments. First, C[hina\'s territorial
disputes are not based on claims over other sovereign
states]{.underline}, in total, [nor are the disputes over key sections
of their landmass]{.underline}. There are important disputes to be sure,
such as with India, but these do not lay claim to vital sections of the
Indian state. More importantly, [the trend has been toward
**reconciliation** rather than escalation]{.underline}. Indeed, [China
has shown a proclivity toward **negotiation**]{.underline}, especially
when it is strong. [One might challenge my argument by pointing to
Taiwan]{.underline} and arguing that China\'s territorial claim
threatens the very existence of a sovereign state. [But in this case
China actually invokes **territorial integrity** and treats **Taiwan as
an internal matter**]{.underline}. Since it has never recognized the
sovereignty of Taiwan, [its claim is framed as]{.underline} a matter of
sovereignty---an [intrastate rather than]{.underline} an
[interstate]{.underline} issue. Overall, the real flashpoints in Chinese
territorial disputes are maritime in nature, pitting it against its
neighbors over [control of sea-lanes and various uninhabited
islands]{.underline} like the Spratlys or the Senkakus/Diaoyus. These
[contests are long running]{.underline}, their rightful ownership is
debated, [and]{.underline} increased [assertiveness over them is
unlikely to challenge the territorial integrity norm]{.underline}.
Second, [China has a strong interest in **maintaining the territorial
integrity norm** and this is unlikely to change.]{.underline} Although
its chief application of the norm is aimed at internal regions, it has
to take the norm in full. [It cannot appeal to the norm in its dealings
with internal nations while also engaging in]{.underline} conquest and
[blatant territorial aggression]{.underline}. Winning the normative
argument in these internal disputes requires that it raise territorial
integrity above self-determination. [The purpose of the borders norm is
to promote stability by emphasizing the existing territorial
grid]{.underline}. In all, [China regularly invokes the
norm]{.underline}, and its full weight should continue to shape Chinese
policy regarding territory. This discussion raises the interesting
question of how the territorial status quo is determined. After all, the
government of [Taiwan rejects China\'s denial of Taiwanese
sovereignty]{.underline} on the basis of territorial integrity. [Does
this ambiguity imply that]{.underline} the norm can be stretched to
cover all claims and that [the powerful can casually determine whose
integrity is threatened]{.underline}? The answer is [**no**. The
existing territorial grid has already been determined through diplomacy,
war, and the other practices,]{.underline} both fair and unfair[, which
shape **i**]{.underline}nternational **[r]{.underline}**elations. Of
course, [conflicting claims over territory]{.underline} do exist on the
margins of that grid---Taiwan being one of the most prominent---and it
is precisely in cases such as these, where territorial integrity can be
invoked by both sides, that conflict becomes more likely. But these
cases [are limited]{.underline} in number, [rooted in history, and not
simply conjured whole cloth]{.underline}. The existing territorial grid
would shape China\'s ability to call on the territorial norm. To
conclude[, a hegemonic China ought to influence international order by
shifting the balance from self-determination toward territorial
integrity]{.underline}. It[s insistence on supporting territorial
integrity in the internal sense is significant]{.underline}, and only in
instances of consent would the state recognize independence claims. [As
such,]{.underline} the [prohibition on conquest should endure during a
time of Chinese hegemony]{.underline}, but the rate of state birth would
decrease. State [proliferation would be controlled relative to the
partly controlled international order]{.underline} that has
characterized the post-1945 period. How would a future period of Chinese
hegemony compare with the current international order or orders of the
past? I have argued that [Chinese hegemony would privilege territorial
integrity at the expense of self-determinatio]{.underline}n. The result
would be an international order that would resemble earlier periods in
some ways and be unique in others. [Sovereign norms would once again be
dominant and liberal norms would be subordinated to the right of
state]{.underline}s. One result of this shift would be a decline, if not
disappearance, in nonconsensual secession. However, since a Chinese
hegemon is likely to hold on to the territorial integrity norm,
[conquest would]{.underline} also [remain rare]{.underline}. [The
overall result would be a surprisingly stable international order, a
**Pax Sinica.**]{.underline}

#### The US does the whole heg thing badly 

**Griffiths, 16** - Associate Professor in the Department of Political
Science at the Maxwell School of Citizenship and Public Affairs (Ryan
D., \"States, Nations, and Territorial Stability: Why Chinese Hegemony
Would Be Better for International Order\", Security Studies, 7-8-2016,
Vol. 25, Issue 3, p. 519-545))SK

It is when we examine the relationship between the two norms that we
obtain a more nuanced understanding of the tension between state and
nation. Self-determination has encouraged minority nations to seek
independence, and the principle of territorial integrity has both
enabled and thwarted them in their efforts. The enabling effect is that
post-independence security concerns no longer act as a deterrent to
obtaining
independence.[24](https://www-tandfonline-com.proxy.lib.umich.edu/doi/full/10.1080/09636412.2016.1195628)Fazal
and Griffiths, "Membership Has its Privileges."View all notesLeaders
may choose other forms of political association---such as local
autonomy---for a variety of reasons, but the fear of invasion and forced
accession is not one of them. James D. Fearon argues that the
territorial integrity norm has functioned like an arms control
agreement.[25](https://www-tandfonline-com.proxy.lib.umich.edu/doi/full/10.1080/09636412.2016.1195628)James
D. Fearon, "Separatist Wars, Partition, and World Order,"Security
Studies13, no. 4 (October--December 2004): 394--415, esp. 9.View all
notesThis contrasts with earlier times when leaders like Thomas Masaryk
argued for increased Czech autonomy instead of full independence by
saying: "We want a federal Austria. We cannot be independent outside of
Austria, next to a powerful Germany with Germans on our
territory."[26](https://www-tandfonline-com.proxy.lib.umich.edu/doi/full/10.1080/09636412.2016.1195628)Ezra
Stern,Opinions of T. G. Masaryk(Prague, 1918), 60.View all notesThese
norms have effects at the system level, and systemic changes that make
conquest rare, and by extension raise the odds of state survival, ought
to increase the rate of secessionism.

The negative consequence of territorial integrity is that while it has
made the international system less perilous and more attractive to
independence movements, it has simultaneously made sovereignty more
difficult to obtain. A consequence of the norm is that the community of
states has worked to preserve political boundaries, and that presents an
obstacle to secessionists who wish to alter them. Jeffrey Herbst argues
that "state boundaries have been singularly successful in their primary
function: preserving the territorial integrity of the state by
preventing significant territorial competition and delegitimizing the
norm of
self-determination."[27](https://www-tandfonline-com.proxy.lib.umich.edu/doi/full/10.1080/09636412.2016.1195628)Jeffrey
Herbst,States and Power in Africa: Comparative Lessons in Authority and
Control(Princeton, NJ: Princeton University Press, 2000), 253.View all
notes

I contend that neither territorial integrity nor self-determination has
been completely consolidated (that is, the relevant actors have not
become fully socialized to them) because the two norms contradict one
another. An unrestricted acceptance of self-determination would
undermine the territorial integrity norm because minority nations would
be free to secede against the wishes of the state. Conversely, full
commitment to maintaining the existing territorial grid would invalidate
the right of secession for aspiring nations. It is on account of this
tension that the post-1945 international order has permitted a measure
of discretion when arbitrating between the right of states and that of
nations. But that discretion prevents the full internationalization of
either norm since actors are expected to balance the merits of both in
any given situation. Their application is conditional---should the state
be privileged or the nation? That conditionality prevents the
consolidation of either norm since their application often depends on a
choice of the best solution for a specific instance.

Recent events in Kosovo and Ukraine highlight the contest between
self-determination and territorial integrity, as well as the potential
for accusations of hypocrisy. Kosovo declared independence in 2008
against the wishes of Serbia, and 108 countries, including the United
States, have recognized it as a sovereign
state.[28](https://www-tandfonline-com.proxy.lib.umich.edu/doi/full/10.1080/09636412.2016.1195628)Daniel
H. Meester, "Remedial Secession: A Positive or Negative Force for the
Prevention and Reduction of Armed Conflict?"Canadian Foreign Policy
Journal18, no. 2 (2012): 151--63.View all notesKosovo\'s success in
garnering international support is regarded by many as a victory for
self-determination, and by some as the closest we have come to
recognizing a remedial right to secession in the face of human rights
abuses by the larger state. In regard to the American recognition of
Kosovo, Secretary of State Condoleeza Rice stated: "The unusual
combination of factors found in the Kosovo situation---including the
context of Yugoslavia\'s breakup, the history of ethnic cleansing and
crimes against civilians in Kosovo, and the extended period of UN
administration---are not found elsewhere and therefore make Kosovo a
special case. Kosovo cannot be seen as a precedent for any other
situation in the world
today."[29](https://www-tandfonline-com.proxy.lib.umich.edu/doi/full/10.1080/09636412.2016.1195628)Condoleeza
Rice, "U.S. Recognizes Kosovo as Independent State," 18 February
2008,<http://2001-2009.state.gov/secretary/rm/2008/02/100973.htm>.View
all notesAlthough the United States chose to call Kosovo a special case
and not one that should set a precedent, it at the least specified
conditions under which territorial integrity should be subordinated to
self-determination. In a recent execution of normative judo, Vladimir
Putin invoked the Kosovo precedent and pointed to western hypocrisy when
he supported Crimean self-determination at the expense of Ukrainian
sovereignty.[30](https://www-tandfonline-com.proxy.lib.umich.edu/doi/full/10.1080/09636412.2016.1195628)On
Russia\'s view of the Kosovar case see James Ker-Lindsay,The Foreign
Policy of Counter Secession: Preventing the Recognition of Contested
States(Oxford: Oxford University Press, 2012), 24.View all notesThe
Ukrainian crisis is still playing out, and it is too early to determine
its effects on the strength of the two norms, but that crisis
demonstrates how the ebb and flow of the normative contest with its
resulting ambiguity and potential for hypocrisy can provide rhetorical
ammunition for political
actors.[31](https://www-tandfonline-com.proxy.lib.umich.edu/doi/full/10.1080/09636412.2016.1195628)Stephen
D. Krasner,Sovereignty: Organized Hypocrisy(Princeton, NJ: Princeton
University Press, 1999).View all notes

The product of this normative tension is an international order
characterized by a semicontrolled proliferation of states. The
international legal community has attempted to balance the norms by
specifying the conditions where minority nations have the right to
independence. The right holds when the central government gives its
consent and thus removes the home state
veto.[32](https://www-tandfonline-com.proxy.lib.umich.edu/doi/full/10.1080/09636412.2016.1195628)Pl
Kolst, "The Sustainability and Future of Unrecognized
Quasi-States,"Journal of Peace Research43, no. 6 (November 2006):
723--740.View all notesThe right holds in instances of decolonization,
but this was always a controlled process limited to overseas
administrative
units.[33](https://www-tandfonline-com.proxy.lib.umich.edu/doi/full/10.1080/09636412.2016.1195628)The
application of self-determination to colonized peoples was subject to
the so-called "saltwater test" and governed by the principle ofuti
possedetis(as you possess), and therefore limited to first order
overseas administrative units. See Robert H. Jackson,Quasi-States:
Sovereignty, International Relations, and the Third World(Cambridge:
Cambridge University Press, 1990); Malcom N. Shaw, "The Heritage of
States: The Principle ofUti Possidetis JurisToday,"British Yearbook of
International Law67 (1996): 75--154; Steven R. Ratner, "Drawing a
Better Line:Uti Possidetisand the Borders of New States,"American
Journal of International Law94, no. 4 (October 1996): 590--624; Tom
Barto, "Uti Possidetis. Quo Vadis?"Australian Yearbook of
International Law18 (1997): 37--96; and Fabry,Recognizing States.
Jackson estimates that the legal emphasis on administrative boundaries
reduced the number of acceptable independence claims in Africa from
400--500 to 40--50. Robert H. Jackson, "The Weight of Ideas in
Decolonization: Normative Change in International Relations," inIdeas
and Foreign Policy: Beliefs, Institutions, and Political Change, ed.
Judith Goldstein and O. Robert Keohane (Ithaca, NY: Cornell University
Press, 1993), 122.View all notesThe right holds for nations of dissolved
states like Yugoslavia and the Soviet Union, but these are ex post
determinations that in any case were not applied to all nations (for
example, Chechnya and Tatarstan). Finally, as we saw with Kosovo, the
right can hold under somewhat ill-defined circumstances when powerful
states conclude that there is cause to recognize the breakaway region.
The end result is an evolving and fuzzy set of criteria for admission to
the club of sovereign states. The gate is not wide enough to admit all
claimants nor sufficiently defined to cleanly sort out those who qualify
from those who do
not.[34](https://www-tandfonline-com.proxy.lib.umich.edu/doi/full/10.1080/09636412.2016.1195628)yvind
sterud, "The Narrow Gate: Entry to the Club of Sovereign
States,"Review of International Studies23, no. 2 (April 1997): 167--84;
Fazal and Griffiths, "Membership Has its Privileges."View all notes

Although the postwar effort to balance these norms is born from good
intentions, the promotion of both has created an ambiguity and fuzziness
around the path to statehood. Jonathan Paquin effectively argues that
the United States balances the trade-off between territorial integrity
and self-determination by choosing the path that promotes the greatest
regional
security.[35](https://www-tandfonline-com.proxy.lib.umich.edu/doi/full/10.1080/09636412.2016.1195628)Jonathan
Paquin,A Stability-Seeking Power: U.S. Foreign Policy and Secessionist
Conflicts(Montreal: McGill-Queen\'s University Press, 2010).View all
notesTypically, as with Kosovo, it defends the territorial integrity of
the state in question until a point is reached where security would be
greater if it switched sides and supported the self-determination
movement. It then defends that switch with various liberal arguments and
the assertion that the case in question is sui generis, or part of a
dissolved state, and not a precedent-setting event. Unfortunately, this
response to secessionism yields unintended consequences insofar as it
shapes the behavior of secessionist movements. First, aspiring nations
now have incentive to create regional
instability.[36](https://www-tandfonline-com.proxy.lib.umich.edu/doi/full/10.1080/09636412.2016.1195628)Alan
J. Kuperman, "The Moral Hazard of Humanitarian Intervention: Lessons
from the Balkans,"International Studies Quarterly52, no. 1 (March
2008): 49--80.View all notesSecond, a perception endures from Kurdistan
to Somaliland that nations can secede if they hold out long
enough.[37](https://www-tandfonline-com.proxy.lib.umich.edu/doi/full/10.1080/09636412.2016.1195628)On
Kurdistan, see Dexter Filkins, "The Fight of Their Lives: ISIS, Iraq,
and the Kurds' Big Opportunity,"New Yorker, 29 September 2014. On
Somaliland, see "Separatism in Africa: Why Can\'t We Do It
Peacefully?"Economist, 27 September 2014.View all notesResearch
indicates that secessionist leaders are typically well-informed,
perceiving the international recognition regime as somewhat flexible and
potentially accommodating of their
demands.[38](https://www-tandfonline-com.proxy.lib.umich.edu/doi/full/10.1080/09636412.2016.1195628)Fazal
and Griffiths, "Membership Has its Privileges."View all notesAfter all,
powerful international supporters can apply pressure on the central
government to remove its home state veto, as Khartoum did with South
Sudan, or treat the instance as sui generis, as with Kosovo, and simply
recognize the breakaway region against the wishes of the sovereign.

In sum, the international order since 1945 has been characterized by
semicontrolled state proliferation, where there are three defining
features. First, states have proliferated, a reversal of the trend
toward state aggregation that characterized earlier periods
(see[Figure1](https://www-tandfonline-com.proxy.lib.umich.edu/doi/full/10.1080/09636412.2016.1195628#f0001)).
Second, independence movements have also proliferated. Minority nations
have responded to the international climate by seeking independence, and
new secessionist movements have continued to rise and replace those who
have seceded. Third, nations sometimes secede even in the absence of
sovereign consent, and thus state proliferation is only partly
controlled. David Armitage refers to these processes as a "contagion of
sovereignty," exemplified by a proliferation of states, an increased
number of independence movements, and an attempt by existing states to
limit
secession.[39](https://www-tandfonline-com.proxy.lib.umich.edu/doi/full/10.1080/09636412.2016.1195628)

#### US hegemony is [collapsing]{.underline} in on itself --- embracing [transformative politics]{.underline} is necessary ensure a [successful transition]{.underline}

[subjectivity, practices, and institutions]{.underline}. At present, as

and economic expansion by "emerging powers", including the BRICS nations

high and increasing structural tension between the persistence of the

2000; Gills, 2011). Arguably, a Polanyian "double movement" is still in
motion, but of its "left" and "right" ideological manifestations, the
latter appears dominant, at least for the time being. The whole idea of
a world order, however, is once again contested. [The historical outcome
of this global contestation]{.underline}, both ideologically and
practically, [will turn upon how states and social forces around the
world will act and respond in the coming period of global
histor]{.underline}y. This outcome is historically indeterminate, as
reality involves complex multi-path developmental processes that can be
interwoven, or contradictory in numerous ways. Finally, in our view it

claim]{.underline}, from a dialectical perspective, [that]{.underline}

[rather than limitation to actions]{.underline} and responses
[from]{.underline} particular ([powerful) actors'

## Indicts

### Western Bias

#### Western academia has become infected with an [ethnocentric]{.underline} hold on truth--- be highly skeptical of their scholarship

**[Young](https://search-proquest-com.proxy.lib.umich.edu/indexinglinkhandler/sng/au/Young,+Alex/$N?accountid=14667),
14** -- M.D. Harvard University (Alex, \"Western Theory, Global World:
Western Bias in International Theory\", [Harvard International
Review](file:///C:\Users\seamu\Documents\Harvard%20International%20Review),
Summer 2014,
[Vol.36,Iss.1](https://search-proquest-com.proxy.lib.umich.edu/indexingvolumeissuelinkhandler/32013/Harvard+International+Review/02014Y07Y01$23Summer+2014$3b++Vol.+36+$281$29/36/1?accountid=14667)))SK

[The danger of western bias is that it shuts out]{.underline} those
**[alternative perspectives]{.underline}**. [Limiting the diversity of
theory we consider in]{.underline} the field of
**[i]{.underline}**nternational **[r]{.underline}**elations [limits the
truth we can generate and **taints what knowledge we**]{.underline} do
**[produce]{.underline}**. [This is]{.underline} especially [problematic
given]{.underline} the growing importance of [knowledge as an economic
phenomenon - **as a good with value**]{.underline}. [Knowledge and
information are increasingly sources of economic worth]{.underline} and
measures of economic success. Whereas Marx proposed a philosophy of
historical materialism whereby material factors-such as productive
capacity and technology-are the sources of power and the drivers of
history, [we are likely now living in a **post-Marxist age of
informationism**]{.underline}, in which [knowledge]{.underline} and its
spread [are the most important sources of power]{.underline}. Examples
abound. A 2009 World Bank report found that a 10 percentage-point
increase in high-speed Internet connections is accompanied by an
increase in economic growth of 1.3 percentage-points. The US budget for
intelligence work has risen every year since 2006 and now sits at around
US\$80 billion per year. A recent [New York Times]{.underline} opinion
piece by Karl Taro Greenfeld [highlighted the importance of information
gleaned from]{.underline} social [media in building social and
**cultural capital**]{.underline}. If the search for truth drives
contemporary history, and [**westerners dominate the search for truth**
about international affairs]{.underline}, then much of [the world has
been unjustly prevented from participating]{.underline} in an important
world-historic and power-generating process. [Western-skewed
**i**]{.underline}nternational **[r]{.underline}**elations [scholarship
excludes much of the world from touching the benefits of the pursuit of
knowledge about the international system]{.underline}, while at the same
time [generating an **incomplete picture of world
affairs**]{.underline}. This exclusion does not seem to the result of
some intentional or malicious grand conspiracy. It would be incorrect to
argue that individual westerners are at fault. Instead, [this problem is
the result of a sort of organic growth]{.underline}, an unintentional
historical process. [It is more correct to view western dominance of
international relations theory as a disease that has **infected
academia**-and one that harms westerners as well as
non-westerners]{.underline}. The non-universality of international
relations thought prevents western theorists from arriving at good
understandings of the international system and from gaining access to
the truth. [The **ethnocentrism** at play is implicit and harmful to all
interested in the pursuit of truth, from any culture or
region]{.underline}. Despite this lack of intentionality or fault,
Robert Cox\'s insight holds: \"Theory is always for someone and for some
purpose.\" [International relations theory is by and for westerners,
even if it is not intentionally so.]{.underline}
